Preface
This thesis has been developed by Barbara Eschen Danielsen and Morten Niels Baxter, as a part of
the Master of Science degree in Architectural Engineering, at the Technical University of Denmark
(DTU). The thesis corresponds to the workload of 30 ECTS points per person and was carried out
during the period from the 6th of February 2023 to the 24th of August 2023.
The thesis supervisor was Per Sieverts Nielsen, who provided great guidance whenever it was
needed, in addition to asking critical questions, which resulted in deeper research and a more elab-
orate project. We would therefore like to thank you Per, for all the help and guidance over the
last seven months. It has been a pleasure working with you.
The collaborative business of the thesis was with Rudersdal Municipality, where we both work. We
would therefore like to thank all our coworkers for all the support, guidance, and help, we received
during the thesis. An extra big thank you to Marie Gammelmark, Sandra Scheller, Karen Skov
Nielsen, Trine Louring Nielsen, Jørgen Heegaard, Bo Rønnov-Jessen, Jesper Repstorff Holtveg,
Jane Riskjær Hansen, Jakob Elkjær, Birgitte Kortegaard, Kasper-Emil Ørtved Tornfeldt, and
Emil Julius Hansen, who played an active role in the thesis.
We would also like to thank Indoor Environment and Energy professor Jakub Kolarik, and Indoor
Climate engineer Dan Aas Christensen, who both provided great knowledge about renovation and
energy labels. Lastly, we would like to thank everyone who replied to the survey that was created
in the thesis.
Technical University of Denmark, Kongens Lyngby, Thursday August 24th 2023

==PAGE:4==
Abstract
On the 14th of March 2023, a new version of the Energy Performance of Buildings Directive
(EPBD), was approved by the European Parliament. The new version includes ambitious energy-
efficiency requirements, that will lead to an upgrade of Europe’s buildings (Taylor, 2023). To help
Rudersdal municipality and its residents to prepare for the new directive, the thesis goal was to
map the renovation potential in Rudersdal municipality based on sustainability factors. To make
the goal achievable within the thesis time span, it was scoped to focus on detached dwellings and
energy renovations.
The following eight sustainability factors, that can influence whether a detached dwelling should
be renovated, were found through a literature review: 1) Heating consumption, 2) Heating Supply,
3) Building parts, 4) Materials, 5) BR18 rules, 6) Energy label, 7) Indoor climate, and 8) Level of
obsolescence and dangerous minerals and gasses. Based on these factors, in combination with an
initial analysis of the buildings in Rudersdal, and available data, the indicators were developed,
which were used to map the renovation potential. Due to time and data limitations, it was not
possible to create indicators for the renovation process itself, or indicators measuring the social
impact. The result of the indicator development was four indicators: 1) Energy Consumption, 2)
CO2 Emissions, 3) Price of Heating, and 4) Energy Label, including three scenarios for indicator
one to three: 1) Improving the Building’s Envelope, 2) Switching the Heating Supplier, and 3) Both;
Switching the Heating Supplier and Improving the Building’s Envelope. By using the tool Indica-
tors, it was possible to evaluate and grade each detached dwelling based on data from Denmark’s
national building and housing register (BBR) and Boliganalysen. The average of these grades was
then used to map the total renovation potential in Rudersdal, showing that Vedbæk, Birkerød
Lake, Bistrup, Center of Holte, and Kollemosevej, were the areas with the highest renovation po-
tential.
The indicators in this thesis were used to investigate the physical renovation potential in Ruders-
dal. However, detached dwellings are privately owned, and it does not help that there is a physical
renovation potential, if there is no interest from the owners to carry it out. An online quantitative
survey was therefore used to evaluate the psychological renovation potential and to understand
the resident’s thoughts and knowledge about energy renovation. The survey got 258 replies from
residents in Rudersdal. The results show that there is a high psychological renovation potential in
Rudersdal, whereas 90% replied that energy renovation is a good idea. When asked about energy
labels, 36.8% answered that they did not know if their home has an energy label, and 10.1% an-
swered that they knew that their home has no label. The reason why people believe that energy
renovation is a good idea is because renovating is good for the environment, money can be saved
in the long run, and the indoor climate and comfort of the building, are improved by renovating.

M.Sc. Thesis

1
Introduction
Europe is battling multiple crises, including the climate crisis, the energy crisis, and the war in
Ukraine, which all would benefit from a decrease in energy consumption. When analysing Europe’s
use of energy, it is seen that Europe’s buildings are responsible for 40% of its energy consumption,
in addition to 36% of Europe’s greenhouse gas emissions. This means that upgrading Europe’s
building stock is one of the possible solutions to some of the crises Europe is facing. The Eu-
ropean Parliament had the same thought on the 14th of March 2023, when they agreed on a
new version of the Energy Performance of Buildings Directive (EPBD). This new version includes
ambitious energy-efficiency requirements, that will lead to an upgrade of Europe’s buildings, but
before becoming an official European Union (EU) law, the legislation needs to be approved by the
Council of the European Union. This means that the next step, is for Parliament to negotiate
with the Council of the European Union, to make this into a law, so the enforcement of upgrading
Europe’s buildings can begin. (Brahm and Martini, 2022; European Parliament, n.d.; Taylor, 2023)
According to an analysis made by Jyske Bank, the new EPBD initiative will have consequences for
approx. 800,000 Danish families. They estimate that the families will need to spend 120 billion
DKK in total on energy-efficient upgrades to their homes. The initiative can lead to illegal houses
by 2030 because homes with an energy label of G have to be renovated to reach at least an energy
label of F before 2030. In 2033, these houses need to be renovated to a minimum energy label of
E. The initiative will be enforced by building owners not being able to sell without a valid energy
label, which is an energy label less than 10 years old, and over F in 2033. According to Ane Arnth
Jensen, deputy managing director of Finans Danmark, it will also be difficult to sell properties
before 2030, since buyers will not be interested in buying houses that do not meet these energy
label requirements. Some of the older houses, especially in rural areas, economically speaking, will
not make sense to renovate, and some people cannot afford to do so. This means that there is a
possibility that the initiative can end up trapping people; not being able to renovate and not being
able to sell. (Brahm and Martini, 2022; Energistyrelsen, n.d.-b)
This thesis has investigated how a municipality and its residents can prepare for the new possible
initiative. Rudersdal municipality, located in the northern part of the capital region of Denmark,
was chosen as the case study. The goal of the thesis has been to make an overview of the current
renovation potential in Rudersdal, by showing the areas with the highest renovation potential. By
doing this, the municipality should be better equipped to help residents and plan for the future.
Due to limited amount of time, the project was scoped to focus on energy-efficient renovations of
detached dwellings. Detached dwellings were chosen because the majority of all buildings in Rud-
ersdal municipality are detached dwellings. According to data from Denmark’s national building
and housing register (BBR), extracted in July 2023, it is seen that out of the total area of build-
ings in the municipality, 57% are detached dwellings. Additionally, there is more data on detached
dwellings, compared to other types of buildings, and when looking at a renovation’s energy savings,
detached dwellings are easier to analyse because they do not share walls or floors with neighbours.
Scoping the project to detached dwellings was also a wish from the municipality.
The goal of the thesis was to make a map, highlighting the areas with the highest renovation
potential in the municipality.
To be able to create this map, it was decided to use the tool
Indicators, to asses each detached dwelling in the municipality, individually. Before assessing the
buildings, the indicators needed to be created, and to do this, information gathering was essential.
This led the thesis to go through six phases: Creating the Thesis Goal, Information Gathering,
Development & Creation, Produce Results, Evaluation and Mapping (more information about the
six phases can be found in Section 2.2).
Page 1
==PAGE:15==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
To ensure a clear structure and plan for the thesis, the following research questions were defined:
• What are some of the overall sustainability factors that can influence whether a detached
dwelling should be renovated?
• Which areas in Rudersdal have the highest renovation potential when looking at these sus-
tainability factors?
• Are people interested in renovating their houses? If so what is their motivation to do it?
The first bullet point was the overall question used to gather all the information needed, to develop
the best possible indicators, that could evaluate the renovation potential. The second bullet point
covers the goal of the thesis, and the last bullet point is a research question about the psychological
renovation potential. The thought behind the last question was, that it does not help that there
is a physical renovation potential, if the residents in Rudersdal municipality are not ready, or not
well enough informed to make the changes needed.
The development of the indicators was the academic foundation of the thesis, which led the thesis
to become a method-orientated project. This is reflected in the report. The report takes you
through the overall method, the literature review, the initial analysis of the buildings in Rudersdal,
indicators, a survey, and a mapping of the renovation potential, with a discussion and conclusion
at the end. Chapter 3 to 7 are written so each chapter can stand alone, which means that an
introduction, method, results, discussion and a key takeaways section, are included within each
chapter. The report structure is illustrated in Figure 1.
Figure 1: The report structure.
Before getting to the overall method, Chapter 2, some background information will be introduced.
1.1
The Energy Crises 2022-2023
The heating season 2022-2023 was expensive for danish households, especially for people using gas
and electricity as a heating supply. A global energy crisis led several European countries to prepare
for energy rationing, but fortunately, it did not come to that. The start of the crisis can be dated
back to 2021, when the energy market started tightening due to a variety of factors, including
the economic rebound and increasing energy demand, following the COVID-19 pandemic. The
situation escalated dramatically into a global energy crisis, in February 2022, when Russia invaded
Ukraine. Due to the war and international sanctions against Russia, Russian oil and coal prices
increased, and Russia itself chose to reduce their gas export to Europe. A combination of two large
nuclear power plants in France being closed, and less water than normal in the water reservoirs in
Norway, resulted in prices skyrocketing especially for gas and electricity. Prices for biomass, oil,
and district heating running on gas, were also affected. (Boding, 2022; Gregersen, 2022; iea, n.d.;
Page 2
==PAGE:16==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
R¨udiger, 2022)
In Denmark, the average gas price was 6-7 DKK per m3 in 2020, but in 2022 the price became
almost five times larger: 30-35 DKK per m3. For a standard house of 130 m2 with an annual
consumption of 1,800 m3 of gas, the annual gas bill was around 60,000 DKK in 2022, compared
to 12,000 DKK two years earlier. The gas price has since fallen again. The increased gas prices
affected the electricity prices, due to a lot of electricity production in Europe being based on gas.
Denmark does not use a lot of gas in electricity production, but the prices are affected by the Euro-
pean market, hence affected by the countries that do use gas to produce electricity. On the 23rd of
August 2022, the electricity prices hit a record high price of 9.37 DKK per kWh, including taxes and
VAT, in households with a flexible electricity price agreement, with hourly billing of consumption.
According to Danmarks Statistik table ENERGI1, one kWh cost 2.11 DKK in 2020, which meant
that the annual electricity bill, in a household with an average consumption of 4,000 kWh, was
8,440 DKK in 2020. The bill was almost twice as big in 2022, with one kWh cost of 3.89 DKK, the
household would then have an annual electricity bill of 15,560 DKK. (Boding, 2022; Statistik, n.d.)
The energy crisis has resulted in people becoming more aware of how much heat and electricity they
use, what kind of heating supply they have and how energy efficient their home is. To save money
people have, among other things, gotten up in the middle of the night to turn on their washing
machine, charged their phones at work, made cold dinners, and chosen not to put their Christmas
lights up. In 2022, Danes lowered their private electricity consumption by 10.8%, compared to
2021. Especially in October (18.3%), February (16.4%) and November (15.3%), private electricity
consumption decreased compared to the same month in 2021. This happened even though there
were more electric cars and heat pumps, which caused private electricity consumption to rise. Ac-
cording to a survey that YouGov did for Bolius in October 2022, when people were asked what
motivated them to use less power, 85% had financial motives, 43% did it to be environmentally
friendly, and 43% did it because they thought it benefited the society, so there was enough power
for everyone. In addition to saving electricity, people used less heating. This was done by having a
lower indoor temperature and starting the heating season later. Private households and small busi-
nesses saved, in 2022, 15% on gas consumption compared to 2021. (Boding, 2022, 2023; Gregersen
and Boding, 2023)
The energy crisis and the war between Russia and Ukraine, led the Danish government, in April
2022, to present a plan for how Denmark would become independent of Russian gas, as soon as
possible. The plan is called Danmark Kan Mere II, and the idea behind the plan is to strengthen
national security and accelerate the environmentally friendly transition. To be able to be inde-
pendent of Russian gas, Danish households that use gas as a heating supply should be offered an
alternative heating supply. This is a big part of the Danmark Kan Mere II plan. The plan gave
the Danish municipalities a deadline, the end of 2022, to inform their residents of an alternative
heating supply. That meant that the municipalities had eight months to make a heating plan and
inform their residents of the part they play in it. Where it was seen that the alternative heating
supply should be district heating, and the implementation of district heating should be completed
by 2028. Not all areas will be able to get district heating, but it is expected that people who
live in these areas, look for other options like heat pumps. Rudersdal municipality has also made
a heating plan, and information about the plan can be found in Section 3.8. (Finansministeriet,
2022; Regeringen, 2022a)
1.2
Energy Performance of Buildings Directive IV
The energy crises have played a central part, together with the climate crisis and the war in
Ukraine, in the motivation to make a new version of the EPBD. The proposal introduces a new
energy label system, which is going to be implemented equally in each member country of the Eu-
ropean Union (EU). The labelling system goes from A to G, where the 15% least energy-efficient
residences are placed in category G, and residences that meet special criteria are placed in group
A. The residences that are left, are distributed evenly across categories B to F. The division of the
residences is done country by country. This means that category G represents the least energy-
efficient houses in each country, but not the worst in all of Europe. E.g. Denmark has, on average,
Page 3
==PAGE:17==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
residences that are in better condition than in southern and eastern Europe. (Brahm and Martini,
2022)
Additionally to introducing the new energy label system, the new EPBD version sets requirements
for the future building standards: Before 2030, all residences from category G must be energy-
improved to a minimum of category F. Three years later, in 2033, all residences in categories G
and F must be energy-improved to a minimum of category E. (Brahm and Martini, 2022)
The new requirements mean, according to an analysis from Jyske Bank, that approx. 30% of the
Danish building housing stock must be energy improved by 2033. This corresponds to 800,000
Danish families being affected by the legislation. The bank cautiously estimates that the average
expense will be 150,000 DKK per house, corresponding to a total investment of 120 billion DKK.
Mikkel Høegh, from Jyske Bank, questions the Danish construction industry’s ability to carry out
this enormous task, in addition to whether it will be worth renovating a lot of the older houses,
especially in rural areas. He believes many properties need to be energy efficient to such an extent,
that it is hardly realistic. All that remains is to tear them down, which can lead to bulldozers
being driven through large areas in Denmark. As it is illegal to sell your house without a valid
energy label, house owners can be caught between not being able to afford renovating their homes,
and not being able to sell them. (Brahm and Martini, 2022)
The European Parliament is planning on using the directive as a central part in achieving the
goals in the climate plan: Fit For 55 and the plan for becoming independent of Russian gas:
RepowerEU. According to Ciaran Cuffe, the person in charge of the Parliament’s negotiations on
the buildings directive, the EPBD will: “not only lower emissions but will also lower energy bills,
boost European jobs and industries and strike a blow to Europe’s dependence on fuel imports
from Russia and elsewhere”. He further explains that the proposed legislation will save approx.
50 billion m3 of fossil fuels per year, which corresponds to the gas consumption of 35 million
households. (Brahm and Martini, 2022; Taylor, 2023)
1.3
Rudersdal Municipality
Rudersdal Municipality covers an area of 73.4 km2, and is, therefore, one of Denmark’s smaller mu-
nicipalities, it is the 77th largest municipality in Denmark out of 98 when looking at area. Figure 2
shows where Rudersdal is, Rudersdal can be seen by its borders being red, and the municipalities
around Rudersdal being black. The municipality is a part of the capital region of Denmark and
has municipality number 230. There are a total of eight different zip codes in the municipality,
some of which are shared with neighbouring municipalities. The municipality has a population of
57,247. In Chapter 4 an initial analysis of the buildings in Rudersdal will be done, where, amongst
other things, the types of buildings that are in Rudersdal will be presented. (Danmarks Statistik,
2023; Region Hovedstaden, 2023; Rudersdal Municipality, 2023)
The municipality as a workplace has 11 different departments, where the authors of this project
work in the City Planning Department. In this project, the authors have collaborated with 3 of
the 11 departments, including the City Planning Department, the Environmental and Technical
Department, and the Personnel and Digitisation Department, which the Communication Division
and the Strategy and Organisational Development Division are under.

1.4
Sustainability and Climate Change
One of the research questions in this thesis, is looking into sustainability factors that can influence
renovation, but what does sustainability mean? The UN describes sustainability as ”meeting the
needs of the present without compromising the ability of future generations to meet their own
needs” (United Nations, 2023a). Sustainability means to ensure that the world is a good place
now and for future generations to come. The world only has the resources it has and those re-
sources have to last forever. Sustainability is based on three pillars: Environmental, Economic and
Social. Environmental sustainability is when humanity does not consume more than the world
can produce, and when pollution and greenhouse gas emissions do not exceed what the world can
handle. Economic sustainability is when everyone in the world can maintain their independence
and have access to the resources they need. Social sustainability looks at human rights and human
health, ensuring that people are respected and safe in their communities. (McGill Sustainability,
2023)
One of the reasons why sustainability is important is because the world is being threatened by
climate change. Climate change refers to shifts in weather and temperature patterns, where hu-
mans are the main cause of these shifts, due to e.g. the burning of fossil fuels like coal. Burning
fossil fuels produces greenhouse gas emissions, which act like a blanket on the earth that traps the
sun’s heat and thereby leads to a rise in temperature. To combat climate change, the world has
created frameworks and agreements to help reduce greenhouse gases such as The UN’s Sustainable
Development Goals and The Paris Agreement. (United Nations, 2023b)
Denmark’s goal is to reduce CO2 emissions by 70% by 2030 compared to 1990. The building sector
is one of the biggest contributors to greenhouse gases in Denmark, where 30% of CO2 emissions
come from this sector. 2/3 of the 30% come from the heating and operational use of the building,
and about 1/3 of the emissions come from the construction process along with the production of
building materials. Rudersdal has its own goal to reduce CO2 emissions by 85% by 2030 compared
to 1990, and since there is not much industry or farming going on in Rudersdal, they see the
reduction as a realistic goal. The biggest contributor to CO2 emissions in Rudersdal is the heating
of buildings, which accounts for 36% of Rudersdal’s CO2 emissions. One of Rudersdal’s biggest
plans for reducing CO2 emissions is to phase out oil and gas heaters by expanding district heating.
The expansion of district heating is explained in Rudersdal’s heating plan and will be done in three
phases, where phase one has already begun. An additional reason, why Danish municipalities want
Page 5
==PAGE:19==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
to phase out oil and gas heaters quickly, is the Energy Crises 2022-2023, see Section 1.1. (Nielsen
et al., 2020; Regeringen, 2022b; Rudersdal Municipality, 2022a, 2022b)
1.5
Indicators
Indicators are a major part of this thesis, because they are used to evaluate the renovation poten-
tial of each detached dwelling in Rudersdal, and therefore an explanation of what an indicator is
will be given below.
According to Gudmundsson et al., an indicator is ”a variable, or a combination of variables, selected
to represent a certain wider issue or characteristics of interest”, and is used to measure or evaluate
that particular characteristic of interest. The issue could for example be Climate Change, where the
measured indicator could be: 390 ppm CO2 of atmospheric air. The measured indicator consists of
a value (390), a unit or scale (ppm), and the variable (CO2 of atmospheric air). In Gudmundsson
et al.’s definition of an indicator, it is seen that the words ”selected” and ”wider” are used. An
indicator is ”selected” by the developer, and is therefore always subjective. The variable has a big
influence if you get a positive or negative result. E.g. looking at the total transportation generated
CO2 emissions, Luxembourg has a two times lower emission than Denmark. Are you on the other
hand looking at transport emissions per vehicle or per capita, Luxembourg is substantially higher.
The word ”wider” is used in the definition because it is not always possible to measure a problem
directly. (Gudmundsson et al., 2016)

Use of AI
Artificial Intelligence (AI) has not been used in this project.

2
Method
This chapter will go through the creation of the thesis goal, the overall method used in the thesis,
and general data findings and challenges. As already explained in the introduction (Chapter 1),
this chapter explains the overall process of the thesis, where Chapter 3 to 7 have their own more
detailed explanation of their specific method. This chapter will therefore only mention the details
that are necessary to understand the overall method, how these drove the project forward, and
how the different activities overlapped.
2.1
Creating the Thesis Goal
The goal of this thesis was created through a co-creation process between us (the authors), Per
Sieverts Nielsen (the supervisor), and the head of the City Planning Department at Rudersdal
municipality, at that time, Birgitte Kortegaard. We (the authors) are studying Architectural En-
gineering, and have a background in City Planning, Sustainability, and Design and Innovation.
Both of us were therefore interested in combining these backgrounds in the thesis. Per Sieverts
Nielsen has great experience in Sustainable Urban Development, and teaches a class in this sub-
ject, at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU). It was therefore decided to ask him to be the
supervisor of the thesis. When looking at Sustainable City Planning, it is great to have a specific
case to analyse, and thanks to us both working in the City Planning Department, in Rudersdal
municipality, it was a great opportunity to use Rudersdal as a case. By already working there, we
had better access to data, in addition to knowing the workflow and the employees, which lead to a
better process, collaboration and data. It was therefore decided, to create a thesis in collaboration
with us, Nielsen and Rudersdal municipality. In order to create a good project, it was important
to define a goal all parties were excited about.
Through a series of meetings with Nielsen and meetings with Rudersdal co-workers, wishes for
what the thesis should include were defined:
• We (the authors) were interested in combining City Planning and Sustainability, with Design
and Innovation.
• Nielsen works in the Energy Economics and Modelling section at DTU and was therefore
interested in supervising a project that included Energy and Heating.
• Kortegaard from Rudersdal municipality was interested in:
– A focus on detached dwellings, including the challenge of implementing changes, due to
detached dwellings being private, meaning that the municipality cannot control what
the building owners do.
– Looking at how to encourage building owners to preserve and renovate, instead of de-
molish and build anew, and in general more information on this subject.
– Looking at how to go from sustainable plans to implementation, especially with the
challenge of private building owners.
– That the thesis should be usable in the building application processes (byggesagsbehan-
dling) or/and future municipality plans.
Later in the co-creation process, we were introduced to the new EPBD initiative. After reading
the proposed initiative, it was clear that by looking into how the municipality and their residents
could prepare for these new possible requirements, would satisfy all of the parties’ interests. The
new initiative requires the building owners to energy renovate, and by looking at this, the Heat
and Energy wish, from Nielsen, was fulfilled. Choosing to focus on detached dwellings, scoped
the project and made it realistic to finish, within the project time span. The energy-efficiency
requirement from the initiative did unfortunately not solve the problem of some people choosing to
demolish and build new houses, instead of renovating. This can realistically be the solution some
building owners choose, instead of renovating. To fulfil the wish of the municipality of looking into
how to encourage building owners to preserve and renovate, it was decided to investigate it in a
Page 7
==PAGE:21==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
literature review.
To explore how the municipality and their residents can prepare for the new possible EPBD
initiative, it is essential to take a step back and investigate the current condition of the buildings
today. In Rudersdal’s Climate Action Plan, it is mentioned that Rudersdal already believes there
is a potential for energy renovating their building stock (Rudersdal Municipality, 2022a). This
belief is only based on the old age of many of the buildings in Rudersdal, but other than age, there
are no other details about the conditions of the buildings that can contribute to the renovation
potential. To be able to prepare for the new EPBD, the municipality needs to know more about
the current potential in the municipality: how many and who can possibly be affected by this
initiative, and are there any areas to focus on in future planning? This lead us to define the goal
of the thesis to be:
Mapping the Renovation Potential in Rudersdal Municipality Based on Sustainability Factors.
The goal includes the plan of a map as a result, highlighting the areas with the highest renova-
tion potential in the municipality. Rudersdal should be able to use this map in future planning
by knowing which areas will most likely need an energy renovation. For the building application
processes (byggesagsbehandling), the data of each house could give the municipal employee (sags-
behandler) a quick indication of how energy efficient the house is, and in that way be able to guide
the applicant better. One of the goals of Rudersdal’s climate action plan, is to increase Rudersdal’s
focus on guiding citizens in terms of energy saving in private buildings. Therefore, we decided to
look into the resident’s knowledge of energy renovation through a survey. By doing this, it was
possible to see how Rudersdal can go from a goal, in a sustainable plan, to implementation.
To summarise, a goal was defined that fulfilled all parties’ wishes, and it was a goal everyone was
excited about. In addition to the goal, two sub-tasks were created: 1) Investigating renovations
compared to building anew through a literature review, and 2) Investigating Rudersdal’s resident’s
knowledge of energy renovation through a survey.
2.2
Overall Method
This thesis was a method-oriented project with the use of Design and Innovation tools and pro-
cesses. The process of this thesis started with a clean slate, without having a predefined goal,
method, or plan for a final product. The overall Design and Innovation tool used in the thesis,
was the Double Diamond (UK Design Council, 2005), which is illustrated in Figure 3. The Double
Diamond consists of two connecting diamonds that diverge and converge, going through four steps:
1) Discover, 2) Define, 3) Develop, and 4) Deliver. The overall process has not been linear, and
the diamonds have been repeated multiple times, which is illustrated in Figure 3 with arrows going
around in a circle. The method used to drive the process forward was the Trial and Error method.
Due to not having a predefined goal, method, or plan for a final product, the method of trying
what we believed to be the best choice, in that particular situation, evaluating that choice and
that choice´s results, removing the errors, and if needed, repeating the process, was the best way
of ensuring the best possible result.
Figure 3: The Double Diamond.
Page 8
==PAGE:22==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
The first example of completing the Double Diamond, was the creation of the thesis goal, as ex-
plained in Section 2.1. Starting the diamonds off with a clean slate, and by facing the challenge
of creating a thesis all parties were excited about. To solve this, all parties’ needs were discovered
by having multiple meetings. Combining the parties’ needs with the new EPBD initiative, it was
possible to define the problem: how to help the municipality and its residents prepare for the new
possible initiative. This completed one of the diamonds, starting the second diamond by devel-
oping ideas of how to solve this problem, by having more meetings and exploring available data.
Combining the developed ideas, it was possible to deliver the final goal of the thesis: mapping the
renovation potential in the municipality. Even though the creation of the thesis goal is explained
as a linear process, this was not the reality. For example, there were multiple times defining of the
problem was tried, but failed. From this, it was realised that more information about the parties’
needs was missing, and therefore a need of repeating the first diamond. This could often be because
the different needs did not align, which meant that discovering more details of the parties’ needs
was required.
Creating the thesis goal, was one out of the six phases the thesis went through, and the six
phases were: Creating the Thesis Goal, Information Gathering, Development & Creation, Produce
Results, Evaluation and Mapping. The workflow between the six phases and the content of each
phase is illustrated in Figure 4. The workflow is shown through arrows, and it can be seen that
multiple loops were completed between the four phases: Information Gathering, Development &
Creation, Produce Results and Evaluation, throughout the thesis. The arrows going up and down
between the phases, show that one phase was used in the next phase, but if anything was missing,
the phase before was revisited, creating a loop between the two phases. The dotted arrows show
larger loops, where the dotted line to the right, illustrates every time information was missing, and
the dotted line to the left, illustrates every time the Development & Creation phase needed to be
revisited. This shows how big of a part the Information Gathering phase and the Development
& Creation phase, played in the overall process. When these larger loops were conducted, all the
previous phases needed to be completed again, to get back to the phase that started the loop. E.g.
if information was missing in the Produce Results phase, the Information Gathering phase and the
Development & Creation were repeated, before getting back to the Produce Results phase. There
were no loops going back to Creating the Thesis Goal, and no loops when Mapping started.
Page 9
==PAGE:23==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
Figure 4: The six phases the thesis went through, including the workflow
between the phases and the content of each phase.
2.2.1
Information Gathering
After Creating the Thesis Goal, the Information Gathering phase started, which was a combination
between: Literature Review, Initial Analysis of Buildings in Rudersdal, and Exploring Available
Data. All three parts of the phase were done simultaneously. Due to the goal being defined in an
area, we did not have a lot of knowledge about, a literature review was needed to understand the
field of energy renovation better. The literature review included finding a definition of renovation
and energy renovation, information about renovation approaches and tendencies, sustainability in
renovation, and renovation rules in Denmark, among other things. A more detailed explanation
of the literature review method and what was included in the literature review can be read in
Section 3.1.
While conducting the Literature Review, an initial analysis of the buildings in Rudersdal munic-
ipality was carried out. This analysis was done on the basis of BBR data, which has been the
thesis’ main source of specific building data. By doing the initial building analysis, it was possible
to check if it made sense to scope the project to detached dwellings, as Kortegaard requested. This
was confirmed, as 57% of Rudersdal’s total building area are detached dwellings. Additionally,
the analysis gave an initial overview of the building stock and information about available data
on each building. It was seen that Rudersdal’s building stock consists of buildings with different
construction years, heating supplies and areas. The oldest detached dwelling can be dated back to
the year 1600, and the newest was built in 2023. This told us that Rudersdal was a municipality
that would be interesting to map, compared to another municipality that could have less variation
in building age. The initial analysis of the buildings in Rudersdal can be seen in Chapter 4, along
with more information about the method used.
At the same time as carrying out the Literature Review and Initial Analysis of Buildings in Rud-
ersdal, we were aware of exploring available data. Available data means data that can be collected
and used for this thesis. Knowing what data was available, drove the project forward. For example,
due to learning that the heating supply was available through BBR, it was decided to investigate,
how the heating supply could influence the renovation potential of a detached dwelling, through
Page 10
==PAGE:24==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
the literature review. When looking for available data, there was sometimes data, that would
have been ideal to fulfilling the goal, but could not be found in the time frame of the thesis. An
example of this happening was the lack of data on the insulation thickness of the houses. By
doing the literature review, it was seen that insulation has a big influence on energy renovations,
more specifically it has an influence on the heat loss of a building, and therefore an influence
on a building’s heat demand and consumption. Due to its importance, alternative data on heat
demand and consumption were explored. When investigating Rudersdal’s Heating Plan, in the
literature review, it was seen that the building’s heating consumption was used to find the areas
with the highest sustainable potential, for changing the heating supply (more information about
the heating plan can be seen in Section 3.8). This meant that the developers of this plan, probably
had information about the heat consumption of Rudersdal’s building stock, that could be used
in this thesis. Jakob Elkjær, from the Environmental and Technical Department at Rudersdal,
was one of the developers of this plan, and was therefore contacted. Elkjær explained that they
hired an external company, Rambøll, to do the calculations of the building’s heating consumption.
The exploring of available data, was then extended to contacting Rambøll. They shared an SBI
report they had used to do the calculations: SBI 2014:01 (Wittchen et al., 2014). Rambøll told us
that they used table 11 on page 26 in the SBI report, and a translated version of this table, with
only detached dwelling’s data included, can be seen in Table 1. To calculate the heat consumption
Rambøll used the building’s area, and year the building was built, which was taken from BBR, and
combined it with this table. This gave a result of how many kWh each building used every year.
If we used the same method, we could also get this information, which could solve the problem of
the missing insulation thickness data.
Table 1: Translated version of table 11 in SBI 2014:01, with only detached dwelling data included. Table expla-
nation in SBI 2014:01 translated: Calculated current unit consumption in the analysed building types and building
periods, calculated from records of the structures’ current thermal properties in connection with energy labeling.
(Wittchen et al., 2014)
A study of SBI 2014:01 was therefore conducted. Later, when trying to use Table 1 to evaluate
the building’s renovation potential, a problem was encountered; data on the newest buildings was
on buildings built after 2006. Additionally, the SBI report was written in 2014, and is therefore
nine years old. The data was therefore believed to be outdated, and could not be used, especially
for newer buildings.
Rudersdal’s heating plan is from 2022, which meant that Rambøll must
have faced the same problem when making their analysis. When asked, they explained that they
had a rule of thumb that newer buildings use 50 kWh/m2 per year in heat consumption. When
talking with a newly graduated indoor climate engineer, Dan Aas Christensen, he believed that
this was an unrealistically high consumption.
It was therefore decided to explore newer data.
When conducting this exploration, a newer version of SBI 2014:01 was found: BUILD Rapport
2021:08 (Kragh and Aggerholm, 2021). The BUILD report was written by two out of the three
authors that wrote the SBI report, and has newer data on a lot of the same content. Since the
BUILD Rapport 2021:08 is rather new, it was a great discovery for the thesis. Additionally, the
BUILD report has more details and results that can be used compared to the SBI report. Both
the SBI and the BUILD report give an estimate of how much a building most likely uses on energy
consumption based on the year built and area, but the BUILD report also gives information on
how much CO2 a building emits depending on the heating supply, and how much consumption
and CO2, a house can save by renovating. This meant that the BUILD report gave a larger and
more precise definition of Rudersdal’s building stock’s renovation potential. Due to SBI 2014:01
not being used in the analysis or the mapping of the renovation potential, it was decided not to
write about it in the literature review, however the BUILD report can be seen in Section 3.7. The
BUILD report plays a central part in this thesis - it has been used in all the phases shown in
Figure 4, except the Creating the Thesis Goal phase.
Page 11
==PAGE:25==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
2.2.2
Indicators
To be able to evaluate the renovation potential for each detached dwelling, it was decided early
on in the thesis to use the tool: Indicators. As explained in Section 1.5, indicators are a tool
that is used to measure or evaluate a characteristic of interest. In this thesis, indicators were
developed to give an indication of the renovation potential of each detached dwelling. As can be
seen in Figure 4, indicators were a part of three out of the six phases; the indicators were devel-
oped in the Development & Creation phase, used in the Produce Results phase, and evaluated in
the Evaluation phase. As already explained, there were multiple loops between the different phases.
The development of the indicators started approx. two months into the project, after one month
of creating the goal and one month of working only on the Information Gathering phase. The
plan was to try and start developing the indicators, and in that way, see what information was
missing to finish the development - the trial and error method. It was believed that this was the
most optimal process because it was not possible to predict all the information and data needed to
develop the indicators. This resulted in multiple loops between the Development & Creation phase
and the Information Gathering phase. After finishing developing the indicators, they were tested
in the Produce Results phase. This phase could lead into three paths: 1) the realisation of missing
data on the buildings, making a loop back to the Information Gathering phase, 2) the realisation of
an error in the design of the indicators, making a loop back to the Development & Creation phase,
or 3) results are complete and ready to be evaluated. After completing the results the Evaluation
phase could start, and this could lead into four paths: 1) going back to the Information Gathering
phase, if something was not clear, to check with literature, data or experts, 2) going back to the
Development & Creation phase, to optimise the indicators, 3) going back to the Produce Results
phase, to check if there was an error in the calculations, or 4) all indicators were as good as they
could be, and it was time to start mapping. The development, use and evaluation of the indicators
in this thesis, can be found in Chapter 5.
2.2.3
Survey
In the early stages of the literature review, an article by Irwin, 2019 was found, about how building
owners can be affected by neighbours renovating. The article said that if someone in your neigh-
bourhood renovates, it is more likely that you yourself renovate. We believed that investigating
this psychological aspect of the renovation potential, would be beneficial to our thesis. A survey
was therefore chosen as an additional tool to the indicators, to evaluate the renovation potential in
Rudersdal municipality. The survey was created to understand the resident’s thoughts and knowl-
edge about energy renovation. In addition to being beneficial to the thesis, it was also created
to help the municipality fulfil its goal of becoming better at guiding their citizens about energy
savings in private buildings, which is stated in their climate action plan, as explained in Section 2.1.
The survey is, like the indicators, a part of three out of the six phases; the survey was created in
the Development & Creation phase, conducted in the Produce Results phase, and the results were
evaluated in the Evaluation phase. This can be seen in Figure 4, as well as the multiple loops, that
also occurred for the survey, between the different phases. Before conducting the final survey, it
was tested on a small group of people at an event at Birkerød Library. By doing this, it was pos-
sible to evaluate the results, go back to the Development & Creation phase and optimise the survey.
The survey was an online Google Forms survey, which started on the 2nd of May, approx. three
months into the project, and ended on the 1st of July. Choosing the survey to be online was
deliberate, to make the process as efficient as possible; the survey was working in the background,
meaning it was possible to be focused on making the indicators as good as possible. The survey
was, therefore, an additional activity on the side, while working on the main objective of mapping
the physical potential. For more information on the survey’s method and results, see Chapter 6.
2.2.4
Final Remarks
To summarise, the overall process is illustrated in Figure 4, and includes six phases. It was a
complex non-linear process, with multiple loops between the six phases, and multiple activities
Page 12
==PAGE:26==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
conducted simultaneously. To do this a combination between the Double Diamond, and the Trial
and Error method, was used. After the first full completion of the Double Diamond, the second
round started by discovering as much information as possible in the Information Gathering phase.
While doing this, multiple small decisions and possibilities were defined, which helped the thesis
move into the Development & Creation phase. In this phase, possible indicators and survey ques-
tions were developed. By testing and evaluating the indicators and the survey, it was possible to
optimise them, arriving at the delivery of the final set of indicators and a final developed survey,
finishing the Double Diamond for the second time. Decisions were made on the basis of literature
and available data.
The final round of the Double Diamond began by discovering how to best map the renovation
potential, and then it was defined how it should be done. Then all that was left was to develop the
map, and deliver the final map. More information on the Mapping phase can be found in Chapter 7.
To make the use of the Double Diamond clear, it was explained as a linear process, but as explained
multiple times, this has not been the case. The same method was used to write the report; instead
of writing it in chronological order, the report is divided into chapters, based on different activities,
to make it a readable report. Additionally, it is important to mention that the literature review
chapter, does not include everything that has been investigated but focuses on literature that has
had an impact on the results. An example of this is Section 3.3 about Sustainability in Renovation,
where it mainly focuses on detached dwellings, even though sustainable renovation in all building
types was investigated before scoping the thesis to detached dwellings. The same can be mentioned
for the results in general, only the final results are included in the report, and not the results that
were generated in the earlier loops.

platform MIRO was used. MIRO is an online whiteboard, with functions like sticky notes, different

and share information, work and develop ideas, make diagrams and figures, among other things,
where they would instantaneously be saved. A picture of our MIRO board can be seen in Figure 5.

3
Literature Review
A literature review was carried out to learn more about energy renovations, and in that way gain
a better basis to create as good as possible indicators, using available literature as stepping stones
to the Development & Creation phase. The main focus of the literature review was to answer the
following research question:
What are some of the overall sustainability factors that can influence whether a detached dwelling
should be renovated?
This chapter will go through the method of the literature review, followed by the results of the
research, which will then be discussed and lastly, key findings of the literature review will be given.
As stated in Subsection 2.2.4, this chapter does not include everything that has been investigated,
but focuses on literature that has had an impact on the results.
3.1
Method
Literature Review is a part of the Information Gathering phase, in the overall process, as described
in Chapter 2 and illustrated in Figure 4. After approx. one month of creating the thesis goal,
the Information Gathering phase started, which Literature Review was an essential part of. The
literature used in the review was found through Desktop Research, and Meetings and Emails, as
illustrated in Figure 6. The different desktop research topics, and the different people that were
contacted through meetings and emails, are also shown in Figure 6.
Figure 6: Overview of the Literature Review. The Literature Review is in the Information Gathering

• What is the definition of a renovation and an energy renovation, and is there a difference

but it is still beneficial to the thesis to understand the system that is in place in Denmark today.
When looking for literature to answer the sub-research-questions, the temporal scope was to find
the newest possible literature, preferably not older than 2019. The spatial scope was literature
regarding Denmark, preferably Rudersdal municipality.
In addition to desktop research, meetings and emails were used to find and understand literature.

were given into how the renovation potential could be looked at in a municipality, and where lit-

about renovating and building anew. He also suggested that the Building Regulations of 2018
(BR18) should be reviewed, to get an idea of what the renovation rules are in Denmark. Another
meeting with an Indoor Climate engineer, Dan Aas Christensen, helped us find more information
on renovation and building anew. For example, he suggested looking at the book ”Den lille bl˚a

also worked for the energy labelling firm Gh Energi & R˚adgivning, and therefore contributed with
literature about energy labelling and general knowledge about it.

the city planners Sandra Scheller and Emil Julius Hansen, from the City Planning Department,

3.2
Energy Renovation
Renovation of a building can mean a lot of different things.
In everyday speech, people use
renovation for everything from changing windows, to rebuilding the whole structure, but what
is the exact definition of a building renovation? When should one renovate and what different
renovation approaches are there? It is known that energy efficiency is one of the parameters that
can be focused on when renovating, but what is the definition of an energy renovation?
This
section will try to answer these questions.
3.2.1
Definition of a Renovation
A building goes through a few maintenance cycles throughout its lifespan to make minor repairs,
so the building maintains its original quality, value, and function. These maintenance cycles focus
on general repairs on the building’s fabric and service systems, which should prolong the building’s
lifetime. Repeating these maintenance cycles is beneficial until the building no longer is able to
satisfy the users’ demands. A building can be maintained regularly, but eventually, more signifi-
cant changes to the building are needed to keep the building’s quality good enough for the user
and environment. It is these more significant changes that are called renovation. (Leskovar and
Premrov, 2019)
Leskovar and Premrov, 2019, defines renovation as the execution of measures that aim at improving
the building’s physical condition and its functional performance. These improvements can range
from small to big, where a small renovation has a limited impact on the building’s improvement
and performance, and the bigger the renovation is, the more measures taken to improve a building,
the bigger the improvement will be upon the building. (Leskovar and Premrov, 2019)
An example of a renovation measure could be the renewal of the building’s thermal envelope. A few
different measures can be done to improve this, like e.g. replacing the existing windows with more
energy efficient windows. Changing the windows will reduce the heat loss through the windows,
decreasing the energy needed to heat and cool the building, which improves the thermal comfort
inside the building. This was just one of the many measures that can be taken to improve the
building’s thermal envelope, and the more measures taken to improve the thermal envelope, the
bigger the improvement. Looking at sustainability and the 3 pillars renovation has environmental,
social and economical benefits. The environmental benefit would be using less energy on heating
and cooling consumption for the building, resulting in a decrease of CO2 emissions among other
benefits. The social benefit would be the improvement of the building’s thermal comfort inside and
the increase of the market value of the house, which would raise the quality of life for the residents
and their human well-being. Finally the economical benefit would be the reduction in energy costs
due to a decrease in energy consumption. (Leskovar and Premrov, 2019)
Page 16
==PAGE:30==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
Leskovar and Premrov, 2019, divides renovation into three main categories; partial, deep and
comprehensive renovation:
• Partial renovation, is a process where one small measure or a few small measures are in focus.
This type of renovation would e.g. be taking down old windows and replacing them with
new ones. A partial renovation would therefore mean a smaller impact, e.g. there would be
a smaller change in energy consumption, compared with the two other categories below.
• Deep renovation, is a bigger renovation than a partial renovation and would therefore give
e.g. a substantial decrease in energy consumption. A deep renovation improves the buildings’
technical systems and uses measures to improve the properties of e.g. the building envelope.
This renovation has a big effect on lowering environmental, economic, and social burdens.
Deep renovations focus on one target. The target could for example be, to make the building
energy efficient. In a deep renovation, it will not only be the windows that will change,
but everything that will lower the building’s energy demand enough, to have a substantial
decrease in energy consumption, and make the building highly efficient.
• Comprehensive renovation is the biggest type of renovation, and has multiple targets, fo-
cusing on improving multiple areas of the building’s quality and their sustainable aspects.
A comprehensive renovation looks at the building’s potential enhancements and renovates
them to a high degree. Many aspects are looked at when doing a comprehensive renovation,
and this is seen through the following description of Leskovar and Premrov, 2019: “Many
questions related to building safety, structural and seismic stability, indoor environment
quality associated with thermal, acoustic, visual comfort and health aspects, usefulness, its
adaptability to the needs of the current user, accessibility for persons with disabilities, aes-
thetic appearance, economic issues, etc., are considered within the process of comprehensive
building renovation.”
3.2.2
When Should one Renovate?
A renovation is defined as the execution of measures that aim at improving the building’s physical
condition and its functional performance, which means that a building should be renovated when
these parameters need to be improved.This would be when the building is seen as obsolete or
inadequate for the users or/and the environment. (Leskovar and Premrov, 2019)
According to Leskovar and Premrov, 2019, there are six types of obsolescence:
• Physical obsolescence is when a building experiences physical decay over time.
• Economic obsolescence is when a building does not meet the cost expectations of the
owner.
• Functional obsolescence is when the use of the building changes.
• Technological obsolescence is when a building’s components no longer perform the way
they should technologically.
• Social obsolescence is when the building no longer meets the users’ needs.
• Legal obsolescence is when a building does not meet the new rules or regulations which is
required of it.
A building can be obsolete in a mixture of these six types of obsolescence. An example of this
happening could be the damage after a fire or an earthquake. It could be argued that all of the
above-mentioned obsolescence would come into play if this happened, but it would depend on the
damages. (Leskovar and Premrov, 2019)
Page 17
==PAGE:31==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
3.2.3
Renovation Approaches
Different renovation approaches, and priorities within each approach, can be seen in Table 2. It
is the type or types of obsolescence, in combination with the type of approach, that determine
the renovation priorities needed in a renovation. The renovation priorities focus on improving the
buildings’ properties, performance, and/or function. (Leskovar and Premrov, 2019)
Table 2: Table recreated from Leskovar and Premrov, 2019: Types
of renovation approaches and priorities.
3.2.4
Definition of an Energy Renovation
This thesis is focusing on the approach Renovation targeting improvement of the building state and
performance, with the priorities: Energy efficiency and Indoor environment quality. Energy Reno-
vation and Energy-Efficiency Renovation are two different ways of explaining the same renovation.
A more detailed description of these two priorities will be presented in this section.
Energy-Efficiency Renovations
An energy-efficient renovation’s goal is to reduce the energy consumption for the building’s opera-
tion. The energy consumption in a building is used on heating and cooling the building, domestic
hot water, and electricity. Electricity is used on lighting and other technical systems, e.g. ventila-
tion system and household appliances. This means that an energy-efficient renovation focuses on
reducing the energy consumption from heating, cooling, domestic hot water, and electricity. In ad-
dition to this, an energy-efficient renovation can include adding solar or wind energy to the building.
In regards to sustainability, one might think energy-efficient renovation only relates to the eco-
nomic and environmental pillar, as an energy-efficiency renovation emphasises mainly energy, but
this is not the case. Energy-efficiency renovation also focuses on the social pillar in regard to indoor
environment quality. An energy-efficient renovation improves air quality, and thermal- and visual
comfort, which affect the users’ health and well-being. (Leskovar and Premrov, 2019)
According to Leskovar and Premrov, 2019, there are three groups of energy-efficient renovations:
• Optimisation of the energy flows between the building and its surroundings. This
group looks at improving the building’s envelope, e.g. adding more insulation to the external
walls and the roof, or/and changing the old windows to newer and improved ones.
• Improvement of technical systems and integration of renewable energy. This group
looks at adding systems to an existing heating, cooling, and ventilation system, or adding
completely new heating, cooling, and ventilation systems to the building. This group looks
at replacing old technical systems with modern, more efficient technical systems.
• Energy generation. This group looks at reducing the dependency on local energy supply.
It has become popular to install systems for electrical energy generation. An example of this
Page 18
==PAGE:32==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
would be installing a photovoltaic system, that turns solar radiation into energy. This group
looks at installing systems that can properly conduct, convert, control, distribute, and store
produced energy by the system installed.
Indoor-Environment-Quality Renovations
People spend in general a lot of time indoors, which makes this renovation important. Indoor-
environment-quality renovations look at humans’ well-being, health, and productivity in the build-
ing. The parameters indoor-environment-quality renovations look at are thermal, visual and acous-
tic comfort, together with the indoor air quality. It focuses on public health, by looking at reducing
harmful biological, chemical, and physical loads in the building. The main sources of these loads
are caused by building materials, electromagnetic fields, other radiation sources, poor ventilation,
and inadequate design. If these issues are not taken care of, the building can cause people harm.
For example, there could be toxic material in the building that can cause people to become sick.
The indoor-environment-quality renovation would then remove this toxic material and replace it
with a non-toxic material. All in all, the main goal of the indoor-environment-quality renovation,
is to improve design conditions that have a harmful effect on the users’ health and well-being.
(Leskovar and Premrov, 2019)
3.3
Sustainability in Renovation
Denmark has to reduce greenhouse gasses by 70% in 2030 compared to the levels in 1990. Where
buildings are responsible for about 30% of Denmark’s total CO2 emissions and are therefore one
of the biggest emitters in Denmark. This means working on decreasing the CO2 emissions caused
by buildings, is an essential part of reaching Denmark’s goal, but what should be done and how
to do it? Is it more sustainable to renovate or build anew? And if you choose to renovate, what
are some of the overall sustainability factors you need to consider? This section will try to answer
these questions.(Energistyrelsen, n.d.-a; Nielsen et al., 2020)
3.3.1
Renovation vs. Build Anew
To investigate what is more sustainable, renovating or demolishing and building anew, two reports
have been explored; a report developed by Rambøll, which is an analysis of CO2 emissions and
the total economy in renovation and build anew, and BUILD Rapport 2022:37, a report about
the climate potential of renovation versus demolition and building anew. These 2 reports focus
on the economical and environmental side of sustainability and do not really get into the social
side. Unfortunately, no literature was found where the social aspect is investigated, measured, or
accumulated, maybe because the social aspect of indoor climate is very subjective and hard to
measure. Still, it would be very interesting to investigate this, to see what the social sustainability
difference is between renovation and build anew, but time was a limitation here. This subsection
will therefore focus on making a review of the Rambøll report and BUILD Rapport 2022:37.
Rambøll
The report from Rambøll, written by Sørensen et al., 2020, discusses whether building anew or
renovating is best economically and environmentally speaking. It is an analysis of 16 cases, going
through four scenarios. The first three scenarios are about renovation and the last scenario is
about building anew:
1. Scenario: Renovation of the roof.
2. Scenario: Renovation of the roof, exterior walls, and windows.
3. Scenario: Renovation of the roof, exterior walls, windows, and installations.
4. Scenario: Building anew, including tearing down the existing building and building a new
house.
The analysis covers detached dwellings, terraced houses, industrial buildings, apartment buildings,
and public buildings. However, as the main focus of this thesis is detached dwellings, this has been
Page 19
==PAGE:33==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
the focus when reviewing the analysis. It is worth noting that in results of the analysis detached
dwellings and terraced houses are grouped into one group. A Life Cycle Assessment (LCA) and
Life Cycle Cost (LCC) were done on the 16 building cases, to see which scenario is the most en-
vironmentally friendly, and which is the cheapest option. There was a total of five cases in the
detached dwellings group, and the rest of the 16 cases are divided between industrial buildings,
apartment buildings, and public buildings.
The overall results of the analysis show that renovation is best, both economically and environ-
mentally, compared to building anew for all 16 building cases. Taking a closer look at the detached
dwellings group, Figure 7 shows a comparison between renovation scenario 3 and build anew. It is
seen that building anew is both more expensive and has a larger climate impact than a renovation
in all five building cases. The report explains that the mean climate impact for renovations was
7.1 kg CO2-equivalent per m2 per year, compared to 9.5 kg CO2-equivalent per m2 per year for
building anew, for the detached dwelling group.
Figure 7: Figure recreated from Sørensen et al., 2020: Renovation
scenario 3 vs. building anew for detached dwellings and terraced
houses, one square represents one house. The figure shows the
climate impact vs. the total economy.
The report investigates one of the five cases in more detail - building case 5. This case is based on a
typical house built to the BR1966 standards. This means that the building is badly insulated and
has leaking constructions. Figure 8 shows the climate impacts and the net present values for this
building case. In the figure to the left, the accumulated climate impact and net present value over
a 50-year period can be seen. On the 2 y-axes the kg CO2-equivalent/m2 and the DKK/m2 can
be seen, and the x-axis represents the different scenarios. The triangles represent the net present
value and the bars represent how much CO2 a building emits after a 50 year period. Starting by
looking at renovation scenario 1, one can see that scenario 1 slightly improves the climate impacts
compared to the basis case (the case without any changes). Renovation scenarios 2 and 3 have
a larger improvement on both CO2 emissions and costs. It is however seen that the larger the
renovation the more CO2 emissions are caused due to building parts, building parts being new ma-
terials that replace the old ones. It can also be seen that the larger the renovation the smaller the
emissions are for the building’s use, meaning that the building would use less energy. For building
anew it can be seen that the biggest accumulated climate impacts come from the building parts,
where the impacts from the use of the building are very small. Comparing renovation scenario 3
and build anew, it is seen that they have approximately the same climate impact accumulated over
50 years, but build anew is clearly the most expensive choice.
The accumulated climate impacts over time over a 50-year period, seen in Figure 8 to the right, is a
great tool for the building owner to use when deciding on which scenario to go with. On the y-axis
the emissions can be seen and on the x-axis the years can be seen. Looking at Figure 8, it can be
seen that building anew has a much higher climate impact today than renovating does, but that
Page 20
==PAGE:34==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
building anew slowly starts to catch up to the renovation scenarios. Building anew uses approx.
20, 35 and 50 years to catch up with renovation scenarios 1, 2 and 3 respectively. Comparing the
different renovation scenarios, it is seen that scenarios 2 and 3 roughly impact the environment the
same where scenario 3 has a slightly smaller impact after 50 years, it is also seen that renovation
scenario 1 has a much lower impact today but much higher impact after 50 years.
Figure 8: Figure recreated from Sørensen et al., 2020: Climate Impacts and the Net Present Value for building
case 5. The left figure shows the accumulated climate impacts and net present value in a 50-year period for the
basis case, without any changes, and the 4 scenarios. The right figure shows the accumulated climate impacts over
time over a 50-year period.
The key takeaway from this Rambøll report, is that a deep renovation is the better option when it
comes to both climate impact and economy compared to build anew. However it is worth noting
that there can be factors that can make a renovation impossible. This could for example be due to
the condition of the building, e.g. if the building was about to collapse. Then it would be better
to build anew.
BUILD Rapport 2022:37
The BUILD Rapport 2022:37, written by L. Eberhardt et al., 2022, focuses on the environmental
impact, and like the Rambøll report, the tool uses LCA to find environmental impacts. The BUILD
report looks at three cases: a detached dwelling, an apartment building, and an office building.
The cases and the data used in each case are taken from real-life renovation projects. The extent
of the renovations are large enough, that building anew could have been a realistic alternative.
When this report was reviewed, the detached dwelling case was the main focus. The detached
dwelling is from 1969 and the area is 92 m2. The case had a tile roof, two brick exterior walls
by the gable and two light weight timber framed exterior walls by the fa¸cades. The building had
windows with two layers of glass, that had timber frames, and timber floors covering a concrete
deck. The inner constructions were timber walls and ceilings, and the detached dwelling’s heating
system was mainly gas. The renovation of this case covered a building extension of 68 m2, giving
a total area of 160 m2. In addition to the extension, the renovation included:
• Changed building parts:
◦New concrete deck with heating and laminated floor, insulated with 300mm of EPS
insulation.
◦Exterior walls are changed to walls with timber construction, covered by fiber-cement-
fa¸cade-plates, and insulated with 250mm mineral wool.
◦All windows and doors are swapped out, to three-layer energy windows with timber
frames, where the total window area is reduced from 18 to 13.6 m2.
◦Replacement of gas boiler, water-, heat-, and sewage pipes and removal of all radiators.
• Remodelled:
◦Timber ceilings are painted, and the timber floors are swapped out for laminate floors.
Page 21
==PAGE:35==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
◦The tile roof is upgraded to a new tile roof, that is primarily post-insulated with 200mm
of mineral wool and 200mm of air-tight EPS-insulation on the footbridge.
• Other:
◦Inner walls between the utility room and kitchen, and the kitchen and living room, are
removed.
◦New kitchen and bathroom.
The BUILD report goes on to explain what climate impacts the renovation had both with and
without the building extension. The BUILD report shows that the building extension has a massive
impact on the environment compared to renovating other parts of the building. Two of the other
more impactful renovations, for this building case, were the renovation of the floor and ceiling, and
the post-insulation of the ground floor. The reason why the renovation of the floor and ceiling
had a big impact, was due to the amount of timber that was disposed of. However, these two
renovations were still not close to being as impactful as the building extension was. From this
renovation case, it could be seen that using a lot of new raw materials, for the building extension,
lead to huge environmental impacts. This was also the case in the Rambøll report where new
raw materials in a newly built detached dwelling were the reason that building anew impacted the
environment so much.
The results for the detached dwelling case are split up into two different heating supply scenarios,
being district heating and heat pumps. On Figure 9 to the left, it can be seen that the largest
climate impact the building has before renovating, comes from the operation of the building. By
switching from a gas heater to district heating, without renovating, it can be seen that operation
of the building is still the largest impacter on the environment, but is substantially reduced. Com-
bining the district heating, with a renovation, the climate impacts are reduced even further, and
even further still when renovating with a building extension. According to the BUILD report, the
reduction of climate impacts associated with the extension, are due to the climate impacts being
spread across a larger floor area. For the two types of renovation with district heating, building
operation is still the biggest impacter. Comparing the district heating scenarios with the building
anew scenario, it is seen that building anew is slightly better for the environment than renovating,
with the biggest impacter coming from new materials.
It can also be seen in Figure 9 to the left, that switching from a gas heater to a heat pump, reduces
the climate impacts even further than building anew with district heating. Switching to heating
pumps reduces the impacts to such an extent, that the climate impacts are under the coming CO2
requirements, that will come into effect in 2025. This requirement says that all newly built build-
ings (built in 2025 or after) can at the most emit 12 kg of CO2-equivalent per m2 p.a. (Kromann
Reumert, 2022). Renovating the detached dwelling with a heating pump reduces impacts even
further.
Figure 9, on the right, shows the accumulated climate impact of renovating with a heat pump and
the accumulated climate impact of renovating with district heating compared to building anew.
From the figure, it can be seen that building anew, with district heating, has the highest imme-
diate impact on the environment, compared to the renovation scenarios, but that building anew
catches up to some of them over time. Renovating with a heating pump, without an extension, is
both the best immediate and overall choice, when looking at the figure, it has the lowest impact
over the 50-year period. Renovating the house, with district heating and without an extension,
is the second best immediate option, but over the 50-year period, it eventually becomes the most
impactful.
All in all, material choice is very important especially when building anew since it is the largest
impacter on the environment. The choice of a building’s heating supplier is also very important,
since heat pumps gave a lower impact on the environment than district heating and gas heaters
did over a 50-year period.
