dwelling over a 50-year period depending on the heating supply (on the right). Accumulated climate impacts over
a 50-year period for a renovation of a detached dwelling, depending on the heating supply (on the left)
3.3.2
How Should One Renovate?
To investigate how one should renovate reports written by Rambøll, and SBI, and a book called
Den Lille Bl˚a Om Varme, will be studied. The three studies go through different building parts
and what is most environmentally friendly.
Rambøll
The Rambøll report described in Subsection 3.3.1 (Sørensen et al., 2020), further investigates the
three renovation scenarios and the material choice when building anew. From Subsection 3.3.1 it
could be seen that raw materials caused high impacts on building anew and was the main reason
building anew had such high impacts on the environment today. Therefore material choice is also
an important factor of sustainability. The Rambøll report concludes that wood-based buildings
have a smaller impact on the climate than other material-based buildings do and should therefore
be considered more often in building materials.
In Figure 10, the climate impact for scenario one to three, as described in Subsection 3.3.1, can
be seen. The figure shows that renovation scenario three has the least impact on the environment,
in that the mean value for this renovation is the lowest (marked as an x in Figure 10).
It is
seen that the bigger the renovation, the less impact the building would have on the environment.
However, Figure 10 also shows that renovation scenario two has the lowest spread, meaning that
this renovation will be more reliable, and therefore have a bigger chance of having a lower impact.
It is therefore not clear which scenario is the best option.
Figure 10: Figure recreated from L. Eberhardt et al., 2022: Climate impacts for different
renovation degrees. The mean value is shown by an x and the variation in CO2 is shown as a box.
Page 23
==PAGE:37==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
SBI 2020:04
The SBI report 2020:04 looks at 60 buildings in Denmark, where 11 of the cases were detached
dwellings. Table 3 gives an overview of the materials used in the different building parts of the 11
detached dwelling cases. The interior wall includes both load-bearing and non-load-bearing walls
because it was not possible to differentiate them.
Table 3: The table is recreated from Zimmermann et al., 2020: Materials used in the different building parts for
detached dwellings. Materials are colour coded by the colours given at the bottom of the table.
In the SBI report, the program LCAbyg was used to analyse the 60 building cases, to see how
their environmental quality is. LCAbyg is at the moment not able to compute all environmental
problems in every stage of a building’s life cycle. The stages LCAbyg could compute, were:
• In the product stage: A1 - raw materials, A2 - transporting material, and A3 - manufacturing
material.
• In the use stage: B4 - change of materials and B6 - energy consumption for operation
• In the end-of-life stage: C3 - waste treatment of material, and C4 - disposal of material.
The LCAs were done over a 50- and 80-year period, where the results for the 11 detached dwelling
cases over a 50-year period can be seen in Figure 11, and the results for the 80-year period are
explained below.
In Figure 11 to the right, the climate impact from the building parts, for each detached dwelling,
can be seen. The results shows that the exterior walls, roof, and ground floor slabs are the three
buildings parts that have the highest climate impact. They impact the product and the end-of-
life stages the most, including obtaining raw materials, transporting the materials, manufacturing
them, and disposal of the materials.
Looking at the roof construction, the house with the smallest amount of environmental impact is
Enf05, and the largest impact is Enf08. Comparing it with Table 3, it is seen that Enf05’s roof
is made of wood and metal, while Enf08 is built with wood and roofing felt. The fact that Enf08
uses roofing felt and Enf05 does not, must mean that roofing felt must be quite impactful on the
environment.

Looking at exterior walls, Enf09 has the lowest impact on the environment, and has exterior walls
made of wood. Enf02 and Enf06 on the other hand, have the exterior walls with the highest im-
pact. Both Enf02 and Enf06 have concrete and cement load bearing exterior walls, with bricks
as the fa¸cade. From this it is seen that wood is more sustainable since it is less impactful on the
environment, whereas using both cement, concrete, and bricks for your exterior walls are more
impactful.
Lastly, the ground floor slabs will be looked at, where Enf03 and Enf05 have the lowest impact on
the environment, these detached dwellings’ ground floor slabs are made up of wood. All the other
detached dwellings’ ground floor slabs are made up of cement and concrete. It can therefore be
seen that concrete and cement have a much higher impact on the environment than wood does.
Comparing the results from the 50-year period analysis, with the 80-year period analysis, it is seen
that the same three building parts (exterior walls, roof, and ground floor slabs) have the highest
climate impact. It is also seen in the 80-year period analysis, that the building parts that do
not contain wood are most impactful on the environment. However, it is seen that the materials
impacts are substantially higher for the 80-year period, due to materials having to be replaced
more often, because the materials lifetime does not span 80 years.
Both the SBI report and Rambøll’s report show that wood is the best environmental material,
when renovating than other materials. In SBI report, it was seen that the roof, exterior walls, and
ground floor are the most worth renovating, since they have the biggest impacts on the environ-
ment over a 50- and 80-year period.
Den Lille Bl˚a Om Varme
“Den lille bl˚a om varme” is a book about energy saving in new and existing buildings. The book
is from 2008 and therefore only takes into account energy savings methods from 2008 and earlier,
and regulations from BR08.
Figure 12 shows the heat consumption for different building parts for the same detached dwelling in
three different stages of its lifetime: when it was built in 1971, after a renovation with an unknown
date, sometime between 1971 and 2007, and after the final renovation in 2007. The original old
dwelling is seen on the right, the dwelling before the renovation in 2007 can be seen on the left,
and after the renovation in 2007 can be seen in the middle.

==PAGE:39==

Figure 12: Figure recreated from Hvenegaard et al., 2008: Heating
consumption for an old detached dwelling before and after a renovation and for
the original detached dwelling built in 1971.
In Figure 12, it is seen that heat consumption from exterior walls, roof, and floor, is highest for all
the life stages of the detached dwelling. The old detached dwelling from 1971, has the highest heat
consumption, with about 160 kWh/m2. This is due to the amount of heat these building parts
lose, also known as the transmission loss. The exterior wall from 1971 has no insulation, which
is a big reason for the huge transmission loss. It can also be seen that windows from 1971 have
the second largest heat consumption, and that the windows have a heat consumption of about 70
kWh/m2, which is also due to the amount of heat that the windows lose.
For the detached dwelling before a renovation in 2007, it is seen in Figure 12, that there is a
decrease in the heat consumption for the exterior walls, roof, floor and windows, compared to the
same dwelling in 1971. Here the exterior walls, roof and floor decreased to about 150 kWh/m2,
and the window decreased to about 60 kWh/m2. It can, however, be seen that the boiler increased
in heat consumption, where it went from about 35 kWh/m2 to 65 kWh/m2, which is most likely
due to the age of the boiler, it would most likely need an inspection and to be replaced.
Looking at the detached dwelling after it was renovated in 2007, it could be seen that all the
building parts were improved when it came to heating consumption. There is however two extra
heating consumption factors: Ventilation Heating Surface and Solar Heating. The ventilation heat-
ing surface uses heat, whereas solar heating adds heat. Even though there is an increase due to the
ventilation heating surface, it is seen that the boiler consumption disappears. After having reno-
vated the detached dwelling, the exterior wall, roof, and floor’s heating consumption was reduced
from about 150 kWh/m2 to about 70 kWh/m2, so a renovation reduced the heat consumption from
the exterior wall, roof, and floor’s by over 50%, which was done by adding more insulation to the
exterior wall and roof. The windows were changed to energy windows, and therefore the heating
consumption fell from about 60 kWh/m2 to about 40 kWh/m2. The boiler was replaced and now
has a heating consumption of about 0 kWh/m2. From Figure 12 it can be seen that renovating
an old detached dwelling will reduce heat loss substantially, especially in the exterior walls, roof,
floor, windows, and boiler.
From the three reports it could be seen that the exterior wall, roof and floors are the most worth
renovating since they have the biggest impacts on the environment and they lose the most heat,
where a renovation reduces this impact and heat lose substantially. It was also seen that the most
environmentally friendly material to build with is wood.

Renovation Tendencies in Denmark
Now that a renovation and an energy-efficiency renovation have been defined, Dane’s tendencies
to renovate will be looked at. The following section will look at Dane’s tendencies to renovate in
general, but also why Danes decided to renovate instead of building anew and vice versa, will be
looked at.
3.4.1
Danes Tendencies to Renovate
Another analysis done by BUILD (BUILD Rapport 2022:35) looks at the tendencies and types of
renovations done in Denmark. The data was collected through two surveys, one made by Viegand
Maagøe A/S & Wilke A/S and the second made by Bolius & Kantar Gallup. The surveys were
needed as data on renovations extent and type are not collected due to the fact that a big amount
of renovations do not require building permits. As it is only surveys, only a sample of Danes were
asked about their renovations tendencies, however the survey tries to capture the overall tendencies
by sending the survey out to all different regions of Denmark. The surveys show the tendencies for
detached dwellings, townhouses, terraced houses, semi-detached houses, apartment buildings, and
low- and high-rise housing. The data from Viegand Maagøe A/S & Wilke A/S shows renovations
that have happened between 2019-2020, and the data from Bolius & Kantar is based on big ren-
ovations either done, started, or thought about in 2020. The best and most detailed data in this
report is on detached dwellings. (L. C. M. Eberhardt et al., 2022)
From Figure 13 the renovation tendencies can be seen for homeowners, based on the survey done
by Viegand Maagøe A/S & Wilke A/S, where a total of 926 answers were received. Looking at
Figure 13 it can be seen that the most common renovation in Denmark is to renovate the kitchen
or bathroom, and the second most common type of renovation is windows. This is then followed by
renovating the heating installation, exterior walls, and facades including insulation of the cavity,
and roof and/or roof construction respectively. The least common type of renovation for Danes,
according to this survey, is renovating the floor construction towards the ground.
The lowest
answer given is other, but it is not known which type of renovation this is.

Why did they renovate:
After taking a look at what types of renovations Danes did, L. C. M. Eberhardt et al., 2022, looked
at why Danes decided to renovate. From the BUILD report 2022:35, it can be seen that Danes
decided to renovate mainly because it was necessary due to the erosion of the building or to fix
leaks. The top three reasons why Danes decided to renovate, were because it was necessary, wanted
to save energy or to insulate, and to increase the comfort of the building. There were also a few
people who said they renovated to keep the aesthetics of the building, which could be due to people
wanting to keep the older buildings in Denmark, the way that they are.
3.4.2
Tendencies to Renovate vs. Build Anew
BUILD has also written a report that looks at statistics in Denmark for building anew - BUILD
Rapport 2022:36 (Jensen et al., 2022). The report looks at three different analyses:
1. Tendencies to tear down a building, based on BBR data for detached dwellings, apartment
buildings, offices, and other building types, that have been torn down and later new buildings
have been built.
2. Reason to tear down detached dwellings, based on surveys given to people who have newly
torn down a building and built anew. (205 people have answered this survey)
3. Reasons for renovating detached dwellings, where there was potential for tearing down and
building anew, based on surveys given to people who have recently renovated instead of
building anew, even though there was potential for it. (126 people answered this survey)
Analysis 1
Analysis 1 looks at BBR data in a time period from 2010-2021. In this period a total of 5.5 million
m2 has been teared down, and a total of 8.9 million m2 has been rebuilt, which means that new
buildings are bigger than the buildings they replaced. Detached dwellings are the biggest con-
tributor to both the torn down area and the rebuild area. Between 2015 and 2021, roughly 1200
detached dwellings were torn down and rebuilt. The capital region (Region Hovedstaden) is the
biggest contributor to tearing down and building new detached dwellings in Denmark, where, in
2019, 54% of newly built detached dwellings were built. The capital region has also clearly been the
biggest contributor to newly built detached dwellings since 2011. The average lifetime for a torn
down detached dwelling is 85 years, and the 20 municipalities with the shortest average lifetime of
torn down detached dwellings are all in the capital region. The data shows that 77% of the detached
dwellings that are torn down, are due to new owners moving in and rebuilding within three years,
whereas 10% of detached dwellings are torn down by owners who have lived there for over 10 years.
From Figure 18, it can be seen that the bigger the detached dwelling is the less likely it is to be
torn down. The smaller the detached dwelling, the more likely it is to be torn down and rebuilt
bigger, however detached dwellings that are under 100 m2, are rarely rebuilt in that size, they are
usually built in a size of 160 m2 or bigger. This means that newly torn down detached dwellings
have a tendency to get bigger.
Figure 18: Figure and figure text translated from Jensen et al., 2022: Percentage distribution of
the torn down detached dwellings from BBR data. (identified as new built year and new area).

Analysis 2
As mentioned above, analysis 2, looks why people wants to tear down and build their detached
dwelling anew, where Figure 19 shows the results, based on a survey sent out to 205 people. The
graph shows that the main reason people wanted to tear down and build anew, is to design their
detached dwellings according to their own wishes. The second highest option, was to make the
detached dwelling more flexible. From Figure 19, it can also be seen that the factor that people
thought was least important, was the architecture, conservation value/cultural values of the de-
tached dwelling. This was then followed by the construction price of building anew compared to
renovating, where 37% of people said this was of no importance. What is surprising to see from
this survey, is that 32% of people said that the environmental purposes had no importance to
them, when choosing to build anew, which means that 32% of people who answered this survey,
chose to tear down and build anew without thinking of the environment, or they though about it,
and realised that the environment was not important to them, when choosing to build anew.
The analysis included an open answering option, where people could write reasons why they built
anew. From the written answers, people wanted to build anew because they wanted to shape the
detached dwelling to their wishes, or the detached dwelling did not fit their personal needs, like
e.g. if a handicapped person moved in, but could not move upstairs. A large group of people,
also said that they needed more space than what they could get from the old detached dwellings.
Maintaining old detached dwellings was too expensive was also a reason to build anew. People also
mentioned, sustainability and energy optimising as a reason to build anew, which makes it more
surprising that 32% of people said that the environmental purposes had no importance to them,
however 63% of people did give environmental reasons a importance 3 or higher.

3.5
Renovation Rules in Denmark
In the Danish Building Regulations of 2018 (BR18), following rule is defined for a person who
wants to renovate their building: When renovating a building energy saving must be done to the
extent that it is profitable and so that the renovation does not cause dampness.. The profitability
of a renovation is calculated by taking the yearly savings, multiplied by the lifetime, and divided
by the investment to renovate, if this is bigger than 1.33 the renovation is seen as profitable. The
energy requirements that a building has to follow can either comply with the requirements given in
Table 4 for the different building parts, or it can follow the renovation classes. Renovation classes
are an energy framework for existing buildings. (Bygningsreglementet, 2023)
To be able to apply for the renovation classes, the following rules have to be followed, taken from
B18 chapter 11 (Bygningsreglementet, 2023):
• The need for added energy has to fall by at least 30.0 kWh/m2 per year.
• The inspection has to take place in accordance with the SBI instruction 213 Energy Require-
ments of Buildings. (This SBI explains how the program Be18 is used, which is a program
that is used to calculate and check if the energy regulations in BR18 are met (BUILD -
Institut for Byggeri, By og Miljø, 2018).)
• There must be a share of renewable energy in the total energy supply for buildings.
• When using renovation class 1, the requirements for the indoor climate in §§382-384, §386,
and §§443-449 must be withheld (which are rules about the indoor climate in BR18).
There are two renovation classes for housing in BR18, and they are classified as, also taken from
B18 chapter 11 (Bygningsreglementet, 2023):
• Renovation class 2: When the total need for supplied energy for heating, ventilation, cooling,
and domestic hot water, per m2 heated floor area, does not exceed 70.0 kWh/m2 per year,
plus 2,200 kWh per year, divided by the heated floor area.
• Renovation class 1: When the total need for supplied energy for heating, ventilation, cooling,
and domestic hot water, per m2 heated floor area, does not exceed 52.5 kWh/m2 per year,
plus 1,650 kWh per year, divided by the heated floor area.
Page 34
==PAGE:48==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
Table 4: The minimum energy requirements for building parts in the building
regulations of 2018. U-value is how much heat is transferred through a structure, the
lower the u-value the less heat is lost. Line Loss is how much heat is lost at a building’s
foundation. Translated from Bygningsreglementet, 2023
.
3.6
Energy Labels
In short terms, an energy label can be defined as a report describing a building’s energy consump-
tion and the building’s energy effectiveness. Energy labels were introduced as legislation in 1997,
stating that every residential house was to be labelled in situations in regard to a sale or rental
of a house. Although the energy label was a legal requirement, both sellers and buyers did not
take it seriously until 2010, when the law was made more strict, making it impossible to rent or
sell without a valid energy label. Additionally, the requirement of labelling a newly built house
was added to the law, resulting in the law that stands today, which requires all buildings above
60 m2 that are sold, rented, or built anew to be labelled. Energy labels are valid for a 10-year
period, and it is possible to use the same energy label multiple times when selling the same house,
if it is still valid. All energy labels and their data are submitted to the Energy Label Orders
(Energimærkningsordningen). (Energistyrelsen, n.d.-b, 2022b; Rados Nenadovic and Henrik Bisp,
2022)
In order to label a house, an energy consultant inspects and measures the specific building in order
to calculate the building’s energy consumption by using variables such as insulation, windows,
heating installations, etc. (Energistyrelsen, 2022a). The calculation is theoretical, meaning that
it is standardised, so the label informs of the quality of the building and not the way it is used -
occupants’ behaviour and weather conditions can have a large impact on the actual energy usage
of buildings, but not a part of the calculations. (Energistyrelsen, 2022a, 2022b)
Besides the theoretical energy use, the label also consists of an overview of possible improvements
that can have a positive impact on energy use. In the report, it is stated in two different ways. On
the front page the three most profitable are listed. Furthermore, every other part of the building
that can be improved profitably is listed - for all of the listed possible energy improvements, de-
tailed information on the renovation is given. (Rados Nenadovic and Henrik Bisp, 2022)
The energy label is categorised on a nine-graded scale and is explained in Table 5. The scale
runs from A to G, with A representing low-energy buildings, while G-labelled buildings consume
the most energy (Energistyrelsen, 2022b). Category A is divided into three, containing A2020,
A2015, and A2010, which each refers to the building regulations for specific years. A2010 refers
to the energy requirements in the building regulations of 2010. A2015 refers to both the building
regulations in 2015 and 2018, since they were similar, and A2020 refers to the former voluntary
building class of 2020, which is now called the voluntary low energy class. (Energistyrelsen, 2022a)
Page 35
==PAGE:49==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
Table 5: Table translated from Energistyrelsen, n.d.-c:
Energy labelling scale for detached dwellings and
multi-family houses - applicable. A is heated area in m2.
3.7
BUILD Report 2021:08: Heat Saving in Existing Buildings
This section will go through the review of the BUILD Report 2021:08 written by Kragh and
Aggerholm (2021), and all information about this report is therefore taken from this source. The
aim of the BUILD report, is to gain more knowledge about where the biggest energy and CO2 saving
potentials are in buildings, when renovating the building’s envelope and changing the building’s
heating supply. The results of the analysis were designed to be able to be used as a general basis
for creating more energy-efficient buildings, in the existing building mass. To do this the report
finds the heating consumption before and after a renovation, and the report finds how much CO2
the buildings emit depending on the heating supply. The report looks at all buildings in Denmark,
but as this project focuses on detached dwellings, this will be the main focus when presenting the
report. The heating consumption for existing buildings will be presented first, followed by the
heating consumption after a renovation.
3.7.1
Heating Consumption for Existing Buildings
How the heating consumption for existing buildings is calculated in the BUILD report will be
presented in the following section. Firstly the BUILD report presents what buildings are apart
of the analysis and how they are sorted. Then the report calculates the transmission loss for the
existing buildings, and the outdoor and indoor temperatures are found, which are then used to
calculate the heating consumption for the buildings.
Buildings
All the buildings in the BUILD report are taken from BBR in November 2020, meaning that all
the buildings in Denmark that were registered in BBR November 2020, including their data, are
extracted. The buildings are split into six groups, one of which is detached dwellings. There is a
total of 1,120,665 detached dwellings, and the average size of these detached dwellings is 149 m2.
The heating supplier is registered in BBR as “hovedopvarmekilden”, and is also extracted as a
part of the building data from BBR.
The building’s heating consumption is not a part of the BBR data, and the BUILD report used
therefore data from the Energy Label Order’s (Energimærkningsordningens) database, to calculate
the heating consumption for each building. In the database, there was a total of 846,299 energy
labels in October 2020. Of those buildings, there was a total of 713,307 labels with complete data,
including a unique definition of the building parts in the building’s envelope. After sorting out
the old energy labels from buildings that had more than one label, there was a total of 652,298
buildings that could be used in the analysis. Out of those 652,298 buildings, 401,973 were detached
dwellings. Comparing this with the total amount of detached dwellings in Denmark according to
BBR, there were energy label data on 36% which was used in the analysis. The 401,973 detached
Page 36
==PAGE:50==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
dwelling spread out through what year they were built, can be seen in Figure 24.
Figure 24: Figure translated from Kragh and Aggerholm, 2021: 401,973
detached dwellings used in the analysis, spread out through what year they were
built.
Transmission Loss
The total transmission loss for each building is calculated using the building part’s and the venti-
lation’s transmission loss. The building parts’ transmission loss is calculated based on data on the
transmission areas (building part areas) and transmission coefficients (U-values) for the building
parts in each building, whereas the the transmission loss is from the ventilation system, is found
from the ventilation type and the knowledge about heat recovery, if there is mechanical ventilation
in the building. The air change (luftskifte) from the ventilation system is assumed to be 0.30 l/s
per m2 heated area, for all of the buildings in the analysis, which is the requirement in terms of
ventilation for housing, according to the building regulations. As mentioned, from the building
part’s transmission loss and the ventilation’s heat loss, the building’s total transmission loss, know
as the building’s dimensioned transmission loss, is found, which is the building’s effective need for
room heating in W/m2 heated area, with an indoor temp of 20 ◦C and an outdoor temp of -12
◦C. When deciding this, outside factors that can heat the building like sun, people, and electronic
devices are not taken into account. The dimensioned transmission loss for the detached dwellings
in the analysis, dependent on the year they were built, can be seen in Figure 25.
Figure 25: Figure translated from Kragh and Aggerholm, 2021: Detached
Dwellings dimensioned transmission loss for each building period in W/m2.
Outdoor Temperature
When calculating the heating consumption the outdoor temperature needs to be found to see how
much heat the building will use. The report splits the year into three: 1) Summer, 2) May and
September, and 3) October to April.
In the summer period, it is assumed that no heating is
functioning. In Table 6 statistics on the outdoor temperature and sunshine hours can be seen for
different time periods. The figure shows that the October to April period is the coldest period of
Page 37
==PAGE:51==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
the year, and has the least amount of sunshine hours, and would therefore need the most heating,
compared to the May and September period, which is slightly warmer and has more sunshine
hours.
Table 6: Table translated from Kragh and Aggerholm, 2021: Outdoor temperatures and
sunshine hours given for different time periods. The cells that are empty are due to the normal
year 1991-2020 being preliminary temperatures, since this data was not complete when the report
was finished and DRY (Design reference year) does not have data on sunshine hours.
Indoor Temperature
In addition to the outdoor temperature, indoor temperature is needed to calculating the heating
consumption. The indoor temperature is found with the following formula taken from Kragh and
Aggerholm (2021):
Tin = 26◦C −0.1233 ∗Pdim
Tin: Average indoor temperature.
Pdim: The dimensioned transmission loss
The dimensioned transmission loss is used in the formula, which ensures that the average indoor
temperature is worse in buildings with worse insulation. Kragh and Aggerholm (2021) sets however
a lower limit for the indoor temperature, Tin cannot go lower than 16 ◦C, even in buildings with
a high transmission loss. Figure 26 show the average indoor temperatures for detached dwellings
in the different time periods.
Figure 26: Figure translated from Kragh and Aggerholm, 2021: Average
indoor temperature for detached dwellings for the different time periods in ◦C.
Heating Consumption
To calculate the final heating consumption, the following formula is used:
Pheat = Pdim ∗(Ti −Tu)/32◦C −Psun −Pint + Phw
Pheat: Heat effect
Pdim: Dimensioned transmission loss
Page 38
==PAGE:52==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
Ti: Average indoor temperature
Tu: Outdoor temperature
Psun: Solar radiation
Pint: Internal heaters (e.g. people, electronics)
Phsw: Hot water
The heat effect is given in W/m2, this heating effect is then multiplied with hours in the periods
to calculate the heating consumption in kWh/m2 pr. year.
If the dimensioned transmission loss is smaller than the internal heaters and solar radiation through
windows, then the heating consumption will equal hot water, Pheat = Phw, this applies for the
summer period. In the May and September period, the heating consumption is assumed to be at
least at 2.0 kWh/m2 per month, and 4.0 kWh/m2 per month in the October to April period, which
is due to the assumption that room heating will never be zero in these periods, because there will
always be at least one room that is being heated, e.g. a bathroom.
The internal heaters are assumed to be at 5.0 W/m2, because it is a standard value for energy
calculations for homes in the building regulations.
The solar radiation through windows is decided by the total area of the windows in the building,
and the orientation and shading of the windows are not taken into account. The radiation is based
on the sunshine hours in the heating and transition period. When deciding the solar radiation, a
factor of 50 Wh/m2 per sunshine hour is used, which is multiplied by the window amount in the
building, which takes into account that about half of the sunlight in theory heats the room.
The energy used to heat water for detached dwellings is set at 15.0 kWh/m2 per year.
Combining all this with the formula above, the unit heat consumption for a detached dwelling in
a certain time period is calculated. The unit heat consumption can be seen in Figure 27, and it
can be seen that the buildings that use the most heat come from buildings built in the 1930s and
40s. The standard deviation of the unit heat consumption is ± 34%, with the highest variation in
buildings built before 1960, and the newer the building the less variations.
Figure 27: Figure translated from Kragh and Aggerholm, 2021: The unit heat
consumption for detached dwellings in the different time periods in kWh/m2
per year.
CO2 Emissions
The BUILD report has also used data from ”Energistatistikken” (energy statistics) 2019 to get
information on the buildings’ CO2 emissions. In the energy statistics, the emissions are calculated
where the CO2 is emitted, and not where the consumption takes place. This affects buildings with
district heating, electricity and heat pumps, where these CO2 emissions are under the electricity
and district heating production, and not the specific house. To make sure these heating suppliers
get assigned the most accurate CO2 emissions, it is assumed that each heating supplier emits the
same amount of CO2 per heating unit for the specific heating supplier type, e.g. the amount of
Page 39
==PAGE:53==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
CO2 emissions the district heating facility emits, is evenly distributed between the users of the
district heating facility. The energy statistics only include the direct CO2 emission, and not the
CO2 emission that come from the extraction and transport of the supplier (e.g. cutting down
trees and collecting straw or transportation of biomass) or from constructing and maintaining
the energy supply facilities (e.g. establishment of wind turbines or solar system). Table 7 shows
the CO2 emissions for little housing, which includes detached dwelling, depending on the type of
heating supplier.
Table 7: Table translated from Kragh and Aggerholm, 2021: CO2 emissions for little
housing for the different heating suppliers, data on this is from ”Energistatistikken”,
emissions are given in kg-CO2/MWh.
3.7.2
Heating Consumption After a Renovation
The BUILD report then goes on to present how the heating consumption for the renovated build-
ings is calculated.
Firstly the report explains how the buildings are upgraded.
Hereafter the
report finds the transmission loss for the upgraded buildings, then a new indoor temperature is
found, which all leads to finding the heating consumption for a building after it has been renovated.
Upgraded Buildings
In the BR18, there is a rule that says when renovating a building, energy saving must be done to
the extent that it is profitable and so that it does not cause dampness, as described in Section 3.5.
In cases where a renovation is not profitable, it should be investigated if a smaller renovation is
profitable. BR18 has a guide about often profitable constructions, including typical values for
how much insulation there should be in different types of constructions. The BUILD report used
BR18 and the guide to assume which building parts of buildings’ envelopes, that is upgraded in
the analysis, and the result is shown in Table 8. The amount of upgraded constructions in the
energy labelled buildings can be seen as a percentage as well as in m2 in Table 8.
Table 8: Table translated from Kragh and Aggerholm, 2021: Building part code, according to
the energy labelling, for constructions in the buildings envelope, the U-value boundary for an
upgrade, U-value that a construction is upgraded to, the area and amount of upgraded
constructions from the energy labelled buildings.
The numbers from Table 8 are then converted to the entire building mass. The renovations will

3.8
Rudersdal’s Plans
To get a better understanding of Rudersdal’s plans for the future, in regard to fighting climate
change, Rudersdal’s climate action plan and heating plan has been reviewed. The climate plan
looks at CO2 emissions and gives a plan of attack on how Rudersdal can reduce these emissions,
and the heating plan focuses more on how Rudersdal plans to lower CO2 emissions from the heating
of buildings. Both plans will be presented in more detail in this section.
3.8.1
Rudersdal’s Climate Action Plan
Figure 31 shows the total CO2 emissions emitted in Rudersdal in 2018. It can be seen that the
biggest CO2 emissions come from heating, followed by electricity and transport. These three are
clearly the biggest emitters in Rudersdal, where these three emitters emitted 286 thousand tons
of CO2 in 2018, in total Rudersdal emitted 301 thousand tons of CO2 that year. The goal of the
climate action plan is to reduce the CO2 emissions in Rudersdal to achieve carbon neutrality by
Page 42
==PAGE:56==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
2040, 10 years before Denmark’s national goal. Rudersdal’s sub-goal is to reduce CO2 emissions
by 85% by 2030 compared to the CO2 emissions emitted in 1990, and to reduce CO2 emissions to
only 68 thousand tons of CO2 by 2030. There is still a long way to go when comparing emissions
to 2018. To reach these ambitious goals everybody in Rudersdal needs to change, they need to
change their behaviour and habits, this includes citizens, businesses, and the municipality, e.g.
people can start taking their bikes more often instead of taking cars, or carpooling if they have to
drive. (Rudersdal Municipality, 2022a)
Figure 31: Figure and figure text translated from Rudersdal
Municipality, 2022a: Rudersdal’s CO2 emissions in 2018 in Ktons.
Total CO2 emission = 301,000 tons CO2 equivalent.
To work their way to carbon neutrality Rudersdal has 4 action areas that should help them reduce
CO2 emissions. These four action plans will be presented below, and are from the climate action
plan (Rudersdal Municipality, 2022a):
1. Green Energy
◦Emissions in this action will be reduced by
– Phasing out oil- and gas boilers.
– Expanding green district heating.
– Saving energy in buildings (a lot of Rudersdal’s buildings are built in 1960-1979,
when there were not many regulations in Bygningsreglementet for buildings energy
consumption, so a lot of energy consumption can be saved here).
– Working on green electricity production.
2. Green Transport
◦Emissions in this action will be reduced by
– Switching to CO2-neutral resources for road transport, by switching to electric cars
and zero emission buses.
– Changing habits and switching to alternative forms of transport, by biking, car-
pooling, or/and taking public transport more often, and by having the option to
work from home more often.
3. Consumption
◦Emissions in this action will be reduced by
– Have a new green procurement policy.
– Focus on the municipality’s purchases, products should be eco-labelled and canteens
should have more focus on buying less meat.
– Focus on green municipal travel, by choosing the green option over anything else.
– Implement new green building policies, with a focus on the environment and think-
ing more sustainable.
Page 43
==PAGE:57==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
– Recycle more and reducing trash and consumption will be a focus when trying to
reduce emissions in this action area.
4. Climate adaption
◦Rudersdal has to be robust against climate change, and will do this by focusing on
– Flood protection from the sewer systems and from cloudbursts.
– Flood protection from storms, erosion, and water levels.
– Protection against rising groundwater.
– Efforts against drought and heat waves.
– Counteract the loss of biodiversity.
3.8.2
Rudersdal’s Heating Plan
As seen above, in Figure 31, the heating in Rudersdal emitted over a third of the total CO2 emis-
sions in 2018, resulting to the goal of reducing this emission, by expanding upon district heating.
The reason behind this explanation goal, is also to become independent of Russian gas, as a part of
the Danish plan Danmark Kan Mere II, as explained in Section 1.1. (Regeringen, 2022b; Rudersdal
Municipality, 2022b)
Rudersdal’s heating plan for the future, is to become fossil-free, where they want oil and gas boil-
ers to be phased out by 2035 at the latest. In 2022, 25% of the heating supply in Rudersdal was
already district heating, but over a fourth of the existing district heating supply was based on gas.
The biggest heating supplier in Rudersdal municipality was natural gas boilers, which covered 61%
of the energy demand in Rudersdal. This needs to change in order to achieve the goal of phasing
out oil and gas boilers by 2035. (Rudersdal Municipality, 2022b)
In collaboration with Rudersdal’s neighbouring municipalities and the district heating supplier
Norfors, five priority areas were created to ensure a fossil-free heating supply, however, Ruders-
dal’s heating plan elaborates only three of the five priority areas. These three priority areas are
(Rudersdal Municipality, 2022b):
1. Fossil free district heating
• New district heating will be based on renewable energy by e.g. using heating pumps
and ground heating systems. Existing district heating using oil and gas will be changed
to renewable energy sources by 2035.
2. Expanding on districts heating
• New district heating areas will be made thanks to the proposal given by Norfors.
3. Expanding renewable energy to areas without district heating
• In areas where district heating cannot be applied or will not be applied in the nearest
future, there will be a focus on smaller locally shared heating solutions like heating
pumps, ground heating systems, etc.
Thanks to Norfors, potential areas where district heating could be expanded to were found, where
these are split into three different phases, the three phases can be seen in Figure 32. Phase 1 will
work on adding district heating in some areas from 2023-2025, and phase 2 will focus on adding
district heating in other areas from 2026-2027. Phase 3 is a future area, where the district heating
companies will clarify the possibilities of establishing district heating in more detail in 2028-2029,
so people who live in the phase 3 areas will be offered district heating between 2028-2035. Not all
areas in Rudersdal are a part of these phases. They will not be offered district heating and have
to figure out what they want to do themselves, although Rudersdal will help find green heating
solutions that work for them through e.g. guiding conversations. Phases 1 and 2 are chosen to
receive district heating first because this is where heating demand is the highest, as seen in relation
to the investment it would cost to expand district heating to these areas. (Rudersdal Municipality,
2022b)
Page 44
==PAGE:58==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
Figure 32: Phases of Rudersdal’s Heating Plan.
3.9
Discussion
This literature review was used to scope the thesis to focus on energy renovation. As described
in Section 3.2 there are many different kinds of renovations, it was therefore important to narrow
the options down since the entire municipality would be looked at. If only one house or one street
was investigated more renovation types could possibly have been included. Energy-efficient reno-
vations were a good choice to investigate, since they focus on a lot of environmental and economic
impacts, that would improve the building’s performance and reduce energy consumption, which is
a big focus in Rudersdal, since the biggest emitter of CO2 comes from the heating of buildings.
In Rudersdal’s climate action plan, it can also be seen that one of their goals in reaching carbon
neutrality is to reduce energy in buildings, so looking at energy-efficient renovations would help
Rudersdal reach this goal.
Energy-efficient renovations do as mentioned focus a lot on environmental and economic aspects
of sustainability, but there are also a few social aspects that could be looked at, such as indoor
climate, they are however hard to measure. Having had a meeting with Jakub Kolarik, Indoor
Environment and Energy professor at DTU, he strongly recommended not looking at the indoor
climate in this thesis. Kolarik explained that this would be too complicated and time-consuming,
due to lack of literature on the subject. This subject could be a Ph.D. project by itself. He further
explained that working with social sustainability in renovations would be hard, because it is sub-
jective and would be about people’s feelings which are hard to measure, and people feel differently
about different things in different settings.
This chapter focuses on building anew vs. renovating since this was one of the sub-tasks defined in
Section 2.1. Why map the renovation potential if building anew is the better option, when it comes
to reducing energy consumption, CO2 emission, price, etc.? From the chapter, it could be seen that
renovating was the better option both economically and environmentally, even for poorly insulated
buildings. Jakub Kolarik also said, in the meeting, that everyone within the sustainability field
say that renovating is the best choice when looking at LCAs, and this chapter only confirms this
statement. If the building is looked at by itself, there could be reasons why renovating is not the
best option, Jakub Kolarik explained that if a building has radon, mould or asbestos in the build-
ing joints, it could be hard to renovate and in these cases it might be cheaper both economically
and environmentally to build anew, since the radon or mould would lead to replacing the building
parts. The results, showing renovating being the overall better option compared to build anew,
Page 45
==PAGE:59==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
can be used by the municipality as arguments when encouraging the building owners to preserve
and renovate, instead of demolish and build anew.
From the chapter it was seen that renovating and changing the heating supply to a heat pump was
the least impactful on the environment. However some heat pumps that can be bought today can
contribute to the increase of TFA- and PFAS contamination of drinking water, which will lead to
people’s quality of life being worsened, so not everything about heat pumps are good. (Teknologisk
Institut, 2023)
Energy labels are a central part of the thesis and the new EPBD, and some time was therefore used
on getting an exact explanation of what is included when choosing which label a building should
get, but without any luck. The danish energy agency explains it, as described in Section 3.6, that
an energy consultant inspects and measures the specific building in order to calculate the build-
ing’s energy consumption by using variables such as insulation, windows, heating installations,
etc.. But what do they mean by ”etc.” - what else is included? After spending some time desktop
researching, without luck, an energy label firm was contacted: Gh Energi & R˚adgivning. Unfor-
tunately, the manager could not provide one to two sentences that include some overall insights
of what is included when labelling either. Due to time limitations, there was not enough time to
get this exact explanation, but one could argue that the definition of energy-efficient renovations
in Subsection 3.2.4, could be applied for energy labels, whereas the definition of an energy label is
a report describing a building’s energy consumption and the building’s energy effectiveness. It is
therefore assumed that energy labels, as energy-efficient renovations, include energy consumption
from heating, cooling, domestic hot water, and electricity, in addition to solar or wind energy.
Comparing the energy labelling system today with the new EPBD initiative, it is seen that the
labelling scale today goes from A to G, as will the new initiative, but the labelling system today
has three types of A: A2020, A2015 and A2010. The new imitative will place the 15% least energy
efficient buildings in G, whereas today a building is placed in G if the building has an energy
consumption above 240 + 6500/the heated area in kWh/m2. One can therefore not be sure if it
will be the same requirements if the new EPBD initiative is implemented. Additionally, it would
have been interesting to know which special criteria there is in the new initiative, to get placed in
an A.
3.10
Key Takeaways
The definition of a renovation is the execution of measures that aim at improving the building’s
physical condition and its functional performance.
An energy-efficient renovation is one of the
priorities of a renovation, with the approach of targeting improvement of the building’s state and
performance. The goal of an energy-efficient renovation is to reduce the energy consumption for
the building’s operation. This means that anything that uses energy, e.g. if a heating system
does not perform the way it should in a detached dwelling, it would be seen as obsolete since the
building’s performance would not satisfy the user, and would therefore need to be renovated.
People should consider renovating rather than building anew since it is better for the environment,
both now and for the future, and cheaper to carry out, even though Danes like the idea of being
able to design and build a new building how they want. From the reviewed reports it could be
seen that renovating the exterior walls, roof, and floor, would be the most beneficial since people
would be able to lower their heating consumption and their CO2 emissions significantly. This is
however at the moment not the renovation Danes typically do, where renovating the kitchen or
bathroom is the most typical, and the most typical energy-efficient renovation a Dane does is to
renovate the windows.
It is also very important to think about what materials to use when renovating since material
choice is a big factor in CO2 emissions. Material choice is also where building anew has the biggest
impact on the environment, and why it could take up to 50 years for a new building to catch up to
a renovated one. Where it is seen from the reviewed reports that building anew has a low impact
when it comes to the operation of the building, but the decision to tear down and build anew,
Page 46
==PAGE:60==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
with new materials, leads to the building having a higher environmental impact today and possibly
not being able to catch up to a renovation, because the start impact from using completely new
materials is too high. Looking at the tendencies, it could be seen that some Danes decided to tear
down and build anew, even though the original house was in good condition, and when tearing
down and build anew, they tend to build even bigger than before. Danes should reconsider this
decision, or at least think about the materials used or to reuse some of the materials from the old
structure, when building anew. It was seen from the report written by Rambøll, that wood was
the more sustainable choice in buildings. It was also seen that renovating was the cheaper decision
over building anew, so if Danes are looking for the cheapest option then, according to the reports
above, renovating is the cheapest option over a 50-year period.
The biggest climate impact from a detached dwelling today comes down to the everyday operation
of the building, this is also true for a renovated building, but the impact is lowered after a renova-
tion. Material choice is less impactful but still has an impact on the climate after renovating, and is
therefore still important to consider when renovating. The everyday operation is where the heating
supply is an important choice. From the reviewed reports, it can be concluded that installing a
heating pump has the lowest impact on CO2 emissions over a 50-year period. Furthermore, it is
also important to think about how extensive a renovation should be, since renovating more of the
building lowers impacts more over a 50-year period, where it was seen that renovating the roof,
exterior walls, windows, and installations, had the lowest CO2 emissions on average over a 50-year
period, however renovating the roof, exterior walls, and windows, was the most reliable.
Using the information gathered through literature, it was possible to list the following overall
sustainability factors, that can influence whether a detached dwelling should be renovated:
• Heating consumption influenced by transmission loss, indoor and outdoor temperature,
solar radiation, internal heaters, and hot water, as seen in the Kragh and Aggerholm (2021).
The transmission loss is again influenced by the transmission areas (building part areas) and
the transmission coefficients (U-values) for the building parts in each building, which include
insulation and windows among other things. The heating consumption is depending on the
year the building was built.
• Heating supply influences CO2 emissions. Buildings where a heating pump is the heating
supplier emit less than buildings with district heating and gas. Heating pumps also reduce
the emission from the operation of the building. It is important to look at a building’s heating
supplier since a heating supplier can reduce CO2 emissions for a newly renovated building.
• Building parts influence transmission loss.
Exterior walls, roofs, floors, windows, and
boilers, are the largest heat losers in an old detached dwelling, renovating so that these
building parts get thicker insulation, better windows (energy windows e.g.), and replace
boilers reduce the transmission loss substantially.
• Materials influences CO2 emissions, buildings that are not built of wood typically emit
more. Materials emit a lot of CO2 in the raw material stage and the disposal stage. Material
choice is therefore important to think about if new materials are needed in a renovation.
• The renovation has to be profitable, according to BR18, which is calculated by taking the
yearly savings multiplied by the lifetime divided by the investment to renovate, if this is
bigger than 1.33 the renovation is seen as profitable.
BR18 also set minimum energy
requirements for building parts, see Table 4.
• To get a professional evaluation of the specific detached dwelling’s energy efficiency, and get
a list of possible energy-efficient improvements, a detached dwelling should get an energy
label.
• Indoor climate or indoor environment quality is the social pillar of a renovation, and is
automatically a part of energy-efficiency renovations. An energy-efficient renovation improves
air quality, and thermal- and visual comfort, which affect the users’ health and well-being.
Unfortunately, this is not possible to measure due to its complexity and subjectivity, in
addition to the lack of literature on the subject.
Page 47
==PAGE:61==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
• The building’s level of obsolescence, including mould, and dangerous minerals and gasses,
including radon and asbestos, influences if a renovation is even possible.
Page 48
==PAGE:62==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
4
Initial Analysis of Buildings in Rudersdal
To get a better understanding of the buildings in Rudersdal an initial analysis was carried out.
The analysis started out looking at all the buildings in Rudersdal, and end up focusing more on
the detached dwellings. Information on Rudersdal’s buildings was found through BBR’s database.
The following chapter will go through the method and results of the analysis, a discussion of the
results, by comparing them to the rest of Denmark, based on the data showed in the BUILD report
2021:08, and give key takeaways from the results.
4.1
Method
The Initial Analysis of Buildings in Rudersdal, like the Literature Review is a part of the Informa-
tion Gathering phase in the overall process, as described in Chapter 2 and illustrated in Figure 4.
Figure 33 shows an overview of the Initial Analysis of Buildings in Rudersdal, where the buildings
in Rudersdal were analysed using BBR data, which was extracted with help through different
meetings and emails with co-workers at Rudersdal municipality.
BBR is the building and housing register where people can see information on all buildings in
Denmark. Information includes e.g. where the building is, when it was built, how big the building
is etc. There are however uncertainties when it comes to BBR data, in that the owners of the
buildings have to update their building information themselves, so data about buildings might be
outdated. It is however important for owners to update their information in BBR because it could
affect how much they have to pay in property tax. (Vurderings Styrelsen, 2023)
BBR does not have very specific data on the buildings in Rudersdal either, which means specific
data on the building materials could not be found/are not registered.
There was nothing on
insulation or materials used, other than cladding materials on exterior walls, and roofs, which is
not enough when looking more specifically at energy renovations. Nonetheless, BBR data was still
the best data that could be used in this project since BBR has data on all the buildings in the
municipality, which would include location, size, year built, which was important information for
the indicators and mapping, although BBR data is not specific on building materials or up to date.

Karen Skov Nielsen and Trine Louring Nielsen were contacted about extracting this BBR data.
Multiple meeting was held and the BBR data was extracted at the meeting on the 21st of February,
meaning that the first analysis of the BBR data could be conducted. This data had information
on all the buildings in Rudersdal.
BBR data is constantly being updated, it was therefore decided to extract the data once again,
closer to the deadline of the project so the data was as up-to-date as possible. After having had
the meeting with Jakob Elkjær about Rudersdal’s heating plan, he said that Marie Gammelmark,
who works with Elkjær in the Environmental and Technical Department at Rudersdal, was the
go-to data person when it came to Rudersdal’s heating plan and climate action plan, and that
she could therefore help extract the BBR data that was needed. Gammelmark explained how the
website used to extract Rudersdal’s BBR data works, which made it possible to extract the data
without any more help from co-workers at Rudersdal. The webside is called KMD Cognito Access
2.1.0, and it is a webside where it is possible to run queries for different data extractions, BBR
data just being some of the data that can be extracted.
The last BBR data extraction was done the 18th of July and is the data used for the whole the-
sis. This ensured that all the data that was needed for the project, was extracted on the same
day and as late as possible, making sure that the project could be finished on time, but with the
newest data possible. From KMD Cognito Access 2.1.0, the sub-queries: GEO BBR Bygning and
GEO BBR AdgangsAdresse, were used to get BBR data with addresses and coordinates, making
it possible to map the final renovation potential as the goal of the thesis. When running these
queries, it was possible to download the data as a CSV-file, which then was imported into Excel,
which was used to make the Initial Analysis of Buildings in Rudersdal. Additionally, data on the
preserved detached dwellings was extracted from the municipality’s internal map, which gets it’s
data from ”SAVE” and ”kulturarv”. The preserved data was also extracted as a CSV-file, which
was imported into Excel together with the BBR data.

Initial Analysis
4.2.1
Overview of the Building Stock in Rudersdal
From Table 9 an overview of the building stock in Rudersdal can be seen. The statistics in the table
includes all types of buildings that are classified in BBR, which include small building structures
like garages, carports, and greenhouses. The table shows the top six building types with the high-
est amount of buildings in Rudersdal, the total area of these building types, and the average size
of the building type in Rudersdal. The data is taken from BBR. There are a total of 90 building
types in Rudersdal, where the 84 other building types are placed in Other, since each amount is
small for these building types.

Table 9: Overview of the building stock in Rudersdal.
From Table 9, it can be seen that there are most detached dwellings in Rudersdal, which constitutes
33.7% of the total amount of buildings. Looking at the area of all the buildings in Rudersdal, it
can be seen that detached dwellings cover 57.2% of Rudersdal’s building stock’s total building
area. This means that detached dwellings account for over 50% of the building area in Rudersdal,
making it by far the building type with the most buildings and building area in Rudersdal. From
the table, it can also be seen that the average size of a detached dwelling in Rudersdal is 176 m2.
Table 9 shows that Terraced House, Several Family House is the building type with the second
most amount of buildings, at about 9%, in Rudersdal, followed by Apartment Buildings at around
2%. However, Apartment Buildings take up a larger Building Area Percentage, around 23%, than
Terraced House, Several Family house, which takes up around 12.7%. After these three building
types the area and building amount drop, where the building type with the fourth most buildings
in Rudersdal is Allotment Buildings, followed by Office Buildings, both of which cover less than 1%
of the total building area. It can be seen that Terraced House, Cluster House and Office Buildings
have the same amount of buildings in Rudersdal but that Terraced House, Cluster House has more
building area in the municipality. From the table, it can be seen that most of the building area in
Rudersdal is used on housing, where Detached Dwellings, Terraced House, Several Family House,
Apartment Building, Allotment House, and Terraced House, Cluster house cover 94.7% of the total
building area in Rudersdal, which does not include the housing types still in Other.
4.2.2
Detached Dwellings
After getting an overview of all the buildings in Rudersdal, it could be seen that detached dwellings
were the building type with the most buildings and the highest building area in Rudersdal, and
therefore a closer look into detached dwellings will be taken. First, the construction year of the
detached dwellings will be investigated, followed by the type of heating.
Detached Dwelling’s Construction Year
Figure 34 shows the period that the different detached dwellings in Rudersdal were built, where
the periods are the same as in BUILD Report 2021:08. From the figure, it can be seen that most
of the detached dwellings are built in 1960-1973, where 32% of the detached dwellings are built.
Most of the detached dwellings are older buildings where 80% of buildings are built in 1978 or
earlier, which means that 80% of the buildings are built 45 years ago or later. 20% of the detached
dwellings are therefore from 1979 or later, and only 4% of the detached dwellings are built in 2016
or later.

Detached Dwelling’s Heating
In BBR the heating installation and heating supplier of the detached dwellings are registered. The
heating installation is the installation used to heat up the building and the heating supplier is the
supplier/source used for heating. The heating suppliers for Heat Pumps and Electric Heating run
on Electricity, while Central Heating with one or two Heating Units, Boiler with Solid or Liquid
Fuel can run on Electricity, Oil, Biomass (Beech Wood), Gas, or other. In BBR District Heating
has no registered data on heating supply, which can be seen in Figure 36 where 4% of buildings
do not have a registered heating supply, due to being District Heating.

central heating with one heating unit, where 73% of detached dwellings have this heating installa-
tion installed. Heat pumps are the second most popular heating installation, 19% of the detached
dwelling use this installation.
After those two installations come district heating and electric
heating where both installations are installed in 4% of Rudersdal’s detached dwellings. Hereafter
central heating with two heating units, and boilers with solid or liquid fuel are installed in 0.2%
of detached dwellings in the municipality.

dwellings are supplied by gas. Electricity is the second highest supplier of heat in the municipality
where 23% of buildings are supplied by electricity. Hereafter 7% of buildings are supplied by oil.

4.3
Discussion
Table 11 is taken from the BUILD report 2021:08 and shows the six building types included in
the report, showing the number of buildings, total building type area, and average building type
size for all of Denmark. If this table is compared to Table 9, it can be seen that like Rudersdal,
the building type there is most of in Denmark are detached dwellings, and they also cover the
most building area. It can be seen that the number of buildings in Denmark matches well with
Rudersdal, where housing is a big focus in both Rudersdal and the Danish building industry, in
that they cover a larger area of Danish buildings, than office buildings and institutions do.
From the tables, it can be seen that the average-sized detached dwelling in Rudersdal is larger
than the Danish average, where Rudersdal’s detached dwellings are an average size of 176 m2 and
Denmark’s are 149 m2. It can also be seen that terraced housing including ”Terraced houses,
Several Family houses” and ”Terraced houses, Cluster houses”, and ”Apartment Buildings” in
Rudersdal also have an average building size, larger than the average in Denmark. Therefore from
the tables it can be seen that housing is built larger in Rudersdal compared to the rest of Denmark.
However, offices are much smaller in Rudersdal, where the average office size in Rudersdal is only
19 m2 compared to 1016 m2 in Denmark. It would therefore seem like Rudersdal has more focus
on housing than on business compared to other municipalities in Denmark.

included in the BUILD Report, looking at all Buildings in Denmark, data from BBR
To see how representative the data in the BUILD report is compared to the buildings in Rudersdal,
one can compare Figure 24 from the BUILD report, with Figure 34. Both show the amount of

report was based on, and it can be seen that it is compatible with Rudersdal’s detached dwellings.
Both sets of data have most buildings built in the period 1960-1973, and least in the period 1999-
2016. The second most common construction period in Rudersdal in 1950-1960, whereas this is
the fifth in the BUILD report. This is however the only period that is less representative, and
one would therefore argue that the BUILD report is very representative of Rudersdal’s detached
dwellings.
4.4
Key Takeaways
From the initial analysis of buildings in Rudersdal, it can be seen that the majority of the build-
ings are detached dwellings, which constitute 33.7% of the total amount of buildings and covers
57.2% of Rudersdal’s building stock’s total building area. Comparing Rudersdal’s building stock
with Denmark’s, it can be seen that Denmark has also a majority of buildings that are detached
dwellings, however, the average size of a detached dwelling is 27 m2 larger in Rudersdal, with an
average size of 176 m2. The majority of buildings in Rudersdal are used for housing, which is also
the case in Denmark.
Furthermore, it was found that most detached dwellings in Rudersdal are built in the period 1960-
1973, accounting for 32% of detached dwellings. Also, 73% of the detached dwellings in Rudersdal
have a central heater with one heating unit installed, while the most used heating source is gas,
accounting for 66% of the detached dwellings.
