5
Indicators
To be able to evaluate the renovation potential for each detached dwelling, indicators were used.
As explained in Subsection 2.2.2, indicators were a part of three out of the six phases in the thesis;
the indicators were developed in the Development & Creation phase, used in the Produce Results
phase, and evaluated in the Evaluation phase. Through a series of loops between the phases, the
indicators became more and more refined, arriving at a final set of indicators used to give an
indication of the renovation potential of each detached dwelling. This chapter will go through the
development of the indicators, the results after using them, and a discussion and evaluation of the
indicator’s performance and results, ending with the key takeaways of this chapter.
5.1
Development of Indicators
The indicators were developed using Maclaren’s steps in the urban sustainability reporting process,
which is illustrated in Figure 37. There are a total of nine steps. The first four are about scoping and
defining the goals, framework, and selection criteria, for the indicators. After setting boundaries
and methods for finding indicators, the identification of the potential indicators begin, resulting in
choosing a final set of indicators. Then, the final indicators can be used to obtain results, which then
can be analysed. In this step, one is supposed to decide if the indicator results show development
towards or away from sustainability. The last two steps consist of presenting the indicators in a
good way, and assessing the indicator’s performance, deciding if the indicators work adequately
and measure what they are meant to measure. In Figure 37 the workflow is seen through arrows,
showing that it is a non-linear process; 1) going back and forth between identifying the potential
indicators and choosing a final set of indicators, 2) after assessing the indicator’s performance, a
completely new loop can start, going back to any of the five first steps, before choosing a final set
of indicators again. (Maclaren, 2022)
Figure 37: Recreation of Maclaren’s steps in the urban
sustainability reporting process. (Maclaren, 2022)
To be able to scope and define the goals, framework, and selection criteria of the indicators, the first
four steps, Maclaren’s report on urban sustainability reporting was used as inspiration (Maclaren,
2022). The next three steps, identify potential indicators, choose a final set of indicators, and
analyse indicator results, in addition to the first step (define goals), were done through loops going
back to the Information Gathering phase, to find more literature and exploring for more available
data. The following subsections will go through the first six steps, and step no. eight how to
present the indicator results in the best possible way, starting with defining the sustainability
goals. The reason for doing this is to explain the method used in both developing the indicators
and presenting them, before getting to step seven, the indicator results and the analysis of them.
The last step, assessment of the indicator performance, can be read in the discussion.
Page 55
==PAGE:69==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
5.1.1
Step 1: Define Sustainability Goals
The first step of Maclaren’s steps to develop indicators is defining the sustainability goals the
indicators should target.
This can be done either by goals that are already defined in other
planning documents or be developed for the specific case (Maclaren, 2022). To figure out what was
best to do in this thesis, a short literature review was conducted to investigate what goals from
different political agendas affect Rudersdal, in addition to the energy labels’ aim. What was found
from this literature review can be seen below:
• The Paris Agreement: “its objective, aims to strengthen the global response to the threat
of climate change, in the context of sustainable development and efforts to eradicate poverty”,
e.g. including “(a) Holding the increase in the global average temperature to well below 2°C
above pre-industrial levels and pursuing efforts to limit the temperature increase to 1.5°C
above pre-industrial levels, recognizing that this would significantly reduce the risks and
impacts of climate change”. (United Nations, 2015)
• The Sustainable Development Goals: “The objective was to produce a set of universal
goals that meet the urgent environmental, political, and economic challenges facing our
world”. (United Nations Development Programme, 2023)
• Rudersdal’s Plan for the Sustainable Development Goal no. 13 Climate Action:
The national goal for Denmark in 2050 is a low-emission society that is independent of fossil
fuels. Denmark supports the EU’s climate target of at least a 40% reduction in emissions of
greenhouse gases in 2030, the EU’s target for renewable energy of at least 27% in 2030, as
well as the EU’s indicative target for energy efficiency of at least 27% in 2030. (Rudersdal
Municipality, n.d.)
• Energy Performance of Buildings Directive III: “Together, the directives promote poli-
cies that will help: a) achieve a highly energy efficient and decarbonised building stock by
2050, b) create a stable environment for investment decisions, c) enable consumers and busi-
nesses to make more informed choices to save energy and money”. (European Commission,
n.d.)
• Energy Performance of Buildings Directive IV: The new proposed directive is planned
to lower CO2 emissions and help the EU become independent of fuel imports from Russia
and elsewhere. Additionally, it is planned to lower energy bills and boost European jobs and
industries. (Brahm and Martini, 2022; Taylor, 2023)
• Some of the goals in Rudersdal’s Climate Action Plan: a) Rudersdal’s intermediate
target for 2030 is a CO2 reduction of 85% compared to 1990. b) Rudersdal should be climate
neutral in 2040. c) Realisation of potential for energy savings in existing buildings (both
private and public) of approx. 7% towards 2030. d) Increased focus on guidance for citizens
and businesses about energy savings in private buildings. e) Increased focus on guidance for
citizens and businesses about green heating solutions. (Rudersdal Municipality, 2022b)
• Energy Labels: “The aim is to promote energy savings by visualising the amount of energy
that a building consumes and by outlining the energy saving possibilities”. (Energistyrelsen,
2022b)
By doing this short literature review, it was possible to get an indication of the targets EU,
Denmark, and Rudersdal have for the future. Additionally, it was possible to get inspiration for
how others set sustainability goals. It was decided to develop new specific goals for this project,
to make sure they served the goal of the thesis as best as possible. Finding inspiration from the
short literature review, and combining the goal of the thesis and Information Gathered from the
big literature review, the following sustainability goals were defined:
• Mapping the Renovation Potential in Rudersdal Municipality based on Sustainability Factors.
• Help the municipality and its residents prepare for the Energy Performance of Buildings
Directive IV.
Page 56
==PAGE:70==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
• Help EU, Denmark, and Rudersdal reach their goal of reducing CO2 emissions, and becoming
climate neutral, by making them aware of the renovation potential.
• Promote energy-efficient building renovations in Rudersdal municipality, and help the mu-
nicipality guide their residents towards energy efficiency.
• Help the municipality in further sustainable city planning, and improve the building applica-
tion process (byggesagsbehandling).
The first point is the goal of the thesis, and the second is the problem defined when creating
the thesis, see Section 2.2. Both are therefore crucial sustainability goals for the indicators, to
make sure the indicators target the thesis goal and the reason behind it. The next two points are
sustainability goals that target some of the goals found in the short literature review. The last
goal is one of the premises, agreed upon with Kortegaard, that the indicators should be usable
for the building application process (byggesagsbehandling) and further planning, as explained in
Section 2.1.
5.1.2
Step 2: Scoping
After defining the sustainability goals, the next step was to scope the indicators to make sure that
they target the correct audience, have the correct level of detail, and still are realistic within the
time frame of the thesis, in addition to temporal and spatial boundaries. Before doing this, the
purpose behind the indicators was defined, which is seen in the sustainable goals in addition to the
second research question of the thesis:
Which areas in Rudersdal have the highest renovation potential when looking at these
sustainability factors?
Where the sustainability factors mentioned are the overall sustainability factors that can influence
whether a detached dwelling should be renovated, found in the literature review, see Section 3.10.
By combining all of this it was possible to define the overall purpose: i) to develop indicators, based
on the overall sustainability factors, found in the literature review, that can influence whether a
detached dwelling should be renovated, ii) to evaluate the energy renovation potential for each
detached dwelling, iii) to be able to map the renovation potential in the municipality, iiii) to in-
vestigate which areas in Rudersdal have the highest renovation potential.
To fulfil this purpose, it was important that there were enough indicators to gain depth in the re-
search, but they had to be finished within a strict time schedule of approx. two months, including
all nine of Maclaren’s steps. From this, the number of indicators was scoped to be 3-8 indicators,
with the option of sub-indicators and different scenarios within the overall indicators. The target
audience for the indicators are the architects and building engineers working at Rudersdal munic-
ipality. By choosing this target audience, it is ensured that the indicators focus on helping the
municipality in further sustainable city planning and improving the building application processes
(byggesagsbehandling), which is defined as one of the sustainability goals. Additionally, architects
and building engineers have degrees within the building industry, and by already working in Rud-
ersdal, they have detailed knowledge about the data and different buildings and areas within the
municipality. This makes them specialists, which makes it possible to develop relatively detailed
indicators based on raw data.
The indicators should be an evaluation of the detached dwellings today, hence the temporal bound-
aries were set to the year 2023, with the goal of no data older than 2019, as the temporal boundaries
set for the literature review. This means that the newest possible data should be used for the indi-
cators. As stated in Section 3.6, energy labels are valid for a 10-year period, and one could discuss
that if these were given for e.g. nine years ago, it is older than 2019, but due to it is still being valid
today, it is representative within the temporal boundaries for this thesis. The spatial boundaries
were already set in the purpose of the indicators: detached dwellings in Rudersdal municipality.
Scoping the thesis to detached dwellings, the final decision was made during step 2 of Maclaren’s
steps.
After being requested by Kortegaard, in the Creating the Thesis Goal phase, after the
discovery of more data on detached dwellings compared to other buildings types, in the Literature
Page 57
==PAGE:71==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
Review, and after realising that there are a majority of detached dwellings in Rudersdal, in the
Initial Analysis of Buildings in Rudersdal, the decision to scope the project to detached dwellings
was made. This decision started a new loop going back to the Information Gathering phase, so
that more detailed information on detached dwellings could be found. Additionally, it was decided
that protected and conservation-worthy buildings, seen in Table 10, should not be included in the
indicators. These have big limitations when renovating, meaning that they can not be evaluated
on the same basis as the other buildings.
Limitations in available data, that could be found within the thesis time span, resulted in not
finding enough data on the renovation process itself. It was not possible to find data on the reno-
vation process, that was sensitive enough that it resulted in different renovation potential between
the buildings, and there was no reason to create an indicator that gave the same result for every
detached dwelling. The idea of creating different scenarios for different renovation processes, in-
cluding changing different building parts was discussed, but without knowing the height, perimeter
or insulation thickness of the buildings, this idea would have required too much time to complete.
This resulted therefore in scoping the indicators to not cover the renovation process, but focus on
the difference between before and after a renovation.
Summarising the scope of the indicators; 3-8 indicators should be developed, using the newest pos-
sible data, no older than from 2019, about energy renovations for detached dwellings in Rudersdal
municipality. Limiting the indicators to only consist of data for before and after the renovation,
and not the renovation process itself. The target audience are the architects and building engineers
working in Rudersdal municipality. The overall purpose behind the indicators is to evaluate the
energy renovation potential for each detached dwelling, excl. protected and conservation-worthy
buildings, to be able to map the renovation potential in the municipality, and to investigate which
areas in Rudersdal have the highest renovation potential.
5.1.3
Step 3: Choose Indicator Framework
Maclaren (2022), explains six types of frameworks that can be used for developing sustainability
indicators: domain-based, goal-based, sectoral, issue-based, causal, and combination frameworks.
Instead of explaining all of them, this subsection will focus on the ones chosen for this thesis.
There are advantages and disadvantages with all the types of frameworks, but by choosing the
combination framework, which combines two or more of the other five frameworks, it is possible
to overcome many of the disadvantages. This is because many of the disadvantages get solved
through the advantages of the other framework, leading the combination framework to consolidate
the advantages while overcoming their weaknesses (Maclaren, 2022). The combination framework
was therefore chosen as the overall framework for this thesis, combining the domain-based, goal-
based, and issue-based frameworks.
The domain-based framework uses the three pillars of sustainability to identify indicators for each
pillar, ensuring that the whole sustainability domain is covered, but does not target the sustain-
ability goals defined in step 1. This is why, it was decided to combine the domain-based framework
with the goal-based framework. As stated in the name, the goal-based framework uses the defined
sustainability goals to develop one or more indicators for each goal or a combination of goals.
The last chosen framework is the issue-based framework, which identifies sustainability issues and
creates indicators for these. The issue-based framework’s weakness is that the issues are often
identified in a ”shotgun” manner, without having any focus on the three pillars of sustainability,
or the sustainability goals, which is again solved by combining these three frameworks. (Maclaren,
2022)
When identifying potential indicators (step 5), all three frameworks were in focus, asking oneself
what the main issues in low energy efficient houses are, while checking if the three pillars of
sustainability and the sustainability goals were covered.
Page 58
==PAGE:72==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
5.1.4
Step 4: Define Selection Criteria
To define the selection criteria, which is used to choose the final set of indicators (step 6), inspiration
was taken from the Maclaren (2022) report on urban sustainability reporting, and chapter six from
the Gudmundsson et al. (2016) book on indicators for sustainable transportation. The selection
criteria that were defined for the indicators in this thesis are:
• Specific: The indicators should be specific and be related to the purpose of the indicators.
• Measurable: The indicators should be used to evaluate the renovation potential, and they
should therefore be able to measure this for each detached dwelling, which requires a precise
definition of them.
• Achievable: The indicators should be achievable, both for development and getting results
within the limitations of the thesis, but also for the residents and employees in Rudersdal.
◦Timely: The indicators need to be done within the time span of the thesis.
◦Cost-Effective to Collect and Use: The budget of the thesis is 0 DKK.
◦Based on raw and available data: As stated in Subsection 5.1.2, the target audience
are specialists which makes it possible to develop relatively detailed indicators based on
raw data. To be able to get any results, the indicators must be based on available data.
• Relevant: The indicators should also be relevant for the residents and employees in Ruder-
sdal.
• Valid: The indicators should measure what it is meant to measure.
• Reliable: The indicators should give the same result over repeated measurements under the
same conditions.
• Sensitive: The indicators should give different results under different conditions.
• Transparent: The indicators should be justified, well-defined, and explained, to be able to
understand them and trust the way they were produced. This includes being understandable
to the target audience.
• Unambiguous: It should be possible to draw one clear conclusion on the basis of an indi-
cator, and the indicators should not be open to more than one interpretation.
• Ethical: The indicators should not violate any fundamental human rights and only use
data that is consistent with established principles and ethics, including the General Data
Protection Regulation (GDPR).
5.1.5
Step 5: Identify Potential Indicators
Step 1 to step 4, Subsection 5.1.1 to Subsection 5.1.4, sets the conditions the indicators should be
developed by. This includes the goals, target audience, boundaries, no. of indicators, framework,
selection criteria, and the purpose for developing indicators. An essential part of the purpose is
to answer the second research question, by developing indicators based on the the overall sus-
tainability factors, which were found in the literature review. In Section 3.10, the eight overall
sustainability factors, that can influence whether a detached dwelling should be renovated, can be
seen, and these include: 1) Heating consumption, 2) Heating supply, 3) Building parts, 4) Materi-
als, 5) BR18 rules of profitability and minimum energy requirements, 6) Energy labels, 7) Indoor
climate, and 8) Level of obsolescence and dangerous materials and gasses.
As stated in Subsection 5.1.3, the overall framework to identifying potential indicators in this
thesis, is the combination framework combining the domain-based, goal-based, and issue-based
frameworks. The issue-based framework was the main framework, where the domain-based and
goal-based, were used to check if the three pillars of sustainability and the sustainability goals were
covered. The overall sustainability factors defined in Section 3.10, are also the defined issues, in
Page 59
==PAGE:73==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
the issue-based framework, and this was the starting point when identifying potential indicators.
The first thing done, was to explore the possibility of making the issues into indicators, including
checking if there was available data on the sustainability factors for detached dwellings in Rud-
ersdal. From this activity, multiple issues were dismissed due to the lack of data, including 3)
Building parts, 4) Materials, 5) BR18 rules of profitability and minimum energy requirements,
7) Indoor climate, and 8) Level of obsolescence and dangerous materials and gasses. 1) Heating
consumption, 2) Heating supply, and 6) Energy labels, were the only sustainability factors left to
develop indicators from. The missing data included:
• Insulation thickness of exterior walls, roofs, floors, and windows.
• Information on the renovation process itself. This could for example be average values in the
renovation process depending on different parameters, like the BUILD report 2021:08 has on
before and after a renovation depending on area and year built.
• In general more detailed information on building parts, materials, and level of obsolescence
and dangerous materials, in each detached dwelling was needed, which for example could have
made it possible to include the renovation process, by creating realistic detailed renovation
process scenarios by changing different building parts.
• The social sustainability aspect of an energy renovation, as stated in Section 3.9 .
Not being able to include the renovation process in the indicator development, made it impossible
to include the sustainability factor 5) BR18 rules of profitability and minimum energy require-
ments, because the investment to renovate needs to be included to calculate if it is profitable, and
insulation thickness is needed to check the minimum energy requirements. However, one could
argue that even though it is not possible to develop indicators from sustainability factor five, the
factor can still be included by using BUILD report 2021:08, which only includes renovations that
comply with BR18’s rules of profitability and minimum energy requirements.
The identification of potential indicators was done through multiple loops between this thesis’
phases, as explained in Subsection 2.2.2. Other than keeping the three frameworks in mind, devel-
oping from the sustainability factors, and checking that the goals and three pillars of sustainability
are covered, there was no real structure of the development.
As explained in Section 2.2, the
method used was the Trial and Error method. Changing between looking for data in the Infor-
mation Gathering phase, and developing indicators in the Development & Creation phase, while
developing a survey, there was a lot happening at the same time. The multiple loops resulted in
three overall waves of identifying indicators:
Wave One of Identifying Potential Indicators
The first wave started by asking what the overall contributors were to energy in a detached dwelling.
This was before the final overall sustainability factors were defined. As stated in Subsection 2.2.2,
the plan was to try and start developing indicators, and in this way, see what information was
missing to finish the development. To answer what the overall contributors were to energy in
a detached dwelling, the thought was that there is something using energy and and something
supplying energy, making these two contributors into two indicators: Heating Supply and The
Building’s Energy Need. These two are illustrated in Figure 38. The Heating Supply indicator
looked at which type of heating supply each detached dwelling had, in addition to going back to
the Literature Review, and investigating what differences the different heat suppliers had in regard
to sustainability. The second indicator, The Building’s Energy Need, was divided into three: 1)
Building area + No. of residents, 2) Transmission loss, and 3) Energy label today.
After looking at overall contributors to energy in a detached dwelling, it was time to use the
domain-based framework of identifying indicators. The next question was therefore are the three
pillars covered? By this time the meeting with Jakub Kolarik had found place, leading to the
realisation of not being able to include the social pillar as a measurable indicator, therefore the
survey was conducted beside the indicators, which is explained in Chapter 6. Using the domain-
based framework, and the limitation of no social indicator, lead to the addition of two indicators:
Economic and Environmental, both shown in Figure 38. The Economic indicator looks at if it
Page 60
==PAGE:74==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
is worth renovating, and the Environmental indicator looks at how big an environmental impact
renovation has.
Figure 38: Potential indicators identified in wave one.
After completing wave one of identifying potential indicators, a new loop going back to the Infor-
mation Gathering phase was needed, to investigate if the potential indicators created were possible,
in addition to being representative for the sustainability goals defined in step 1.
Wave Two of Identifying Potential Indicators
Before wave two, the BUILD report 2021:08 was found, in addition to having defined the final
sustainability factors, that can influence whether a detached dwelling should be renovated, in the
Literature Review. This meant that wave two was a combination of sustainability factors (issue-
based framework), wave one, domain-based framework, and the BUILD report, and the result of
wave two can be seen in Figure 39. As explained earlier, the sustainability factors left to develop
indicators from were: Heating consumption, Heating supply, and Energy labels.
The average heating consumption depending on the year built and area, for detached dwellings,
is given by the BUILD report. This was the most precise data on consumption, that was found
on the detached dwelling in this thesis, and an indicator was therefore developed from this. The
Consumption indicator replaced the sub-indicators 1) Building area + No. of residents, and 2)
Transmission loss, from wave one, due to the BUILD report’s heating consumption matching the
thought behind the two sub-indicators. In addition to the consumption indicator, two indicators
were developed regarding heating supply: CO2 Emissions and Price of Heating. The BUILD re-
port also gives information on how much CO2 a building emits depending on the heating supply,
which can be used in the indicator, covering the environmental pillar of sustainability. Checking
the economic aspect before and after a renovation, without looking at the renovation process, was
left. This would be done by checking how much money a person can spend on heating depending
on the consumption and the heating supplier, resulting in the Price of Heating indicator. After
developing three indicators, the domain-based framework was fulfilled to the extent it could, but
the issue-based and goal-based were missing an indicator regarding energy labels, which resulted
in a fourth indicator: Energy Labels.
In addition to the four indicators, three scenarios were developed: 1) Improving the Building’s
Envelope, 2) Switching the Heating Supplier, and 3) Both; Switching the Heating Supplier and
Page 61
==PAGE:75==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
Improving the Building’s Envelope.
These three scenarios were developed on the basis of the
BUILD report, which differentiate depending on if the building envelope was improved or the
heating supplier was switched. The idea was that it would be interesting to see what the difference
was between the scenarios, and from this conclude which scenario was the best, but also due to
energy renovations covering all three scenarios, meaning that the residents can choose to only carry
out one of the scenarios, which can influence the renovation potential.
The scenarios were divided into two: 1) Status quo, and 2) Possible savings by doing the scenario.
The thought behind the division, e.g looking at consumption, even though the building would have
a small saving in kWh by e.g. improving the building’s envelope, the building could have a high
consumption today, and therefore have a high impact on becoming a sustainable municipality. As
seen in Figure 39, each scenario is divided into two, which is again divided into four, resulting in
four overall indicators, that have 24 sub-indicators.
Figure 39: Potential scenarios and indicators identified in wave two. Be aware that there
is a total of four overall indicators and 24 sub-indicators. Only 12 are illustrated, to save
space, but it can be seen that both Status quo and Possible savings by doing the scenario
have four each, giving a total of 24. Dotted lines represent indicators that needed further
development.
At the end of this wave, a misunderstanding of BUILD report 2021:08 was realised. In the BUILD
report, there is a figure showing heating consumption in detached houses in kWh/year depending
on heating supply and year built (Figure 21 in the BUILD report by Kragh and Aggerholm (2021)).
From this figure, it was assumed that heating consumption also depended on the type of heating
Page 62
==PAGE:76==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
supply, in addition to the year built, meaning that this figure could be used to see the difference
in consumption for the scenario Switching the Heating Supplier. After finding results for all 24
sub-indicators, the results of the indicators were analysed and assessed. What could be seen was
that the heating supply from gas boilers often had a smaller consumption than district heating,
realising that consumption should not differ between the heating supply, meaning that the heating
supplier should not effect how much heat a building needs to be supplied with. As explained in
Section 3.1, both of the authors of the BUILD report were therefore contacted through email, and
Jesper Kragh replied. Kragh explained that the differences between the heating suppliers, shown
in the figure, were caused by the data they had on the specific buildings they used as cases. This
meant that consumption is not dependent on heating supply, but what could be taken from the
figure, are the average statistics of how much a building with that heat supplier consumed, meaning
that in general detached dwellings with a gas boiler consume less than detached dwellings with
district heating. Kragh guesses that one of the reasons for this is due to district heating being
historically cheaper, and building owners with district heating have therefore not had the same
motivation to energy renovate their homes. This misinterpretation of Figure 21 in the BUILD
report, lead us to question the scenarios that included switching the heating supplier, due to there
being no difference in consumption depending on the heating suppliers, reducing the scenarios to
one: Improving the Building’s Envelope.
In addition to realising the misinterpretation of the figure in the BUILD report, the energy label
indicator had to be reevaluated. After finding results for all six sub-indicators, it was seen that
there was almost no difference between the buildings. For status quo, calculated from the con-
sumption indicator, and compared with the energy labelling scale (seen in Table 5), all detached
dwellings were energy labelled between A2010 and D, which is clearly wrong as seen in Figure 42,
which shows the distribution of energy labels in Rudersdal today, where it can be seen that all
labels are covered. By getting these results, one was reminded that the BUILD report uses average
consumption, making it very unrealistic. After improving the building’s envelope, there were only
two results: either the buildings stayed at the same energy label, or jumped one energy label up.
In Figure 39 it is seen that each scenario includes a status quo, but after finding results for the
indicators, it was seen that each indicator had the same status quo, even though the scenario
changed, which make sense since even though there are different scenarios, all scenarios had the
same status quo.
By using the indicators from wave two, in the Produce Results phase, and evaluating them, in the
Evaluation phase, all indicators were questioned as explained above. To summarise, the scenarios,
energy labels, and status quo in this wave needed to be a reevaluated. It was realised that even
though there is no difference in the consumption between the scenarios, there is a difference in
CO2 emissions and the Price of Heating. It was therefore decided to keep the scenarios, with the
awareness of possible savings in consumption for the switching the heating supply scenario being
zero, this indicator is shown in Figure 39 with a dotted line. It was decided to dismiss Status
quo, because the thesis goal is to map the renovation potential, and even though a building has
a high consumption today, it does not help if there is no possible savings. An example of this
could be a big newly built detached dwelling, due to the huge area, there could be a relatively
big consumption, even though the transmission loss is small. For this case, what would the most
sustainable solution be? Tear down some square meters to get a smaller area, or keep the high
consumption? Tearing down would not be good on the environment, because the disposal of new
materials would have a large CO2 emission impact. but a high consumption would also have a CO2
emission impact. It was therefore decided to only focus on the possible saving for each scenario.
For the energy label indicator, status quo gives the most representative data. It was therefore
decided to remove the energy label indicators for each scenario and make this a stand-alone indi-
cator, giving the most detailed status of the building’s obsolescence level today. The energy labels
are therefore also shown Figure 39 with a dotted line.
Wave Three of Identifying Potential Indicators - The Final Wave
Wave two and three overlap, with no clear starting point of wave three, and ending point of wave
two, where the decisions explained at the end of wave two, give the results of wave three, which

can be seen in Figure 40. As explained at the end of wave two, the three scenarios were kept,
but the energy label indicator was taken out of the scenarios, as a stand-alone indicator. For the
three scenarios, the renovation potential is investigated by looking at the possible savings by doing
the different scenarios. This is done by comparing status quo with the values calculated after a
scenario is done. There is a total of four indicators: 1) Consumption, 2) CO2 Emissions, 3) Price
of Heating, and 4) Energy Label. The three scenarios work as sub-indicators for indicator 1-3,
giving a total of nine sub-indicators.
Figure 40: Potential scenarios and indicators identified in wave three. Be aware that there
is a total of four overall indicators, where three indicators have three different scenarios,
meaning each of the three overall indicators has three different sub-indicators, leading to a
total of nine sub-indicators. For the three scenarios, the possible savings are investigated,
whereas, for the indicator energy label, the status quo is investigated.
By going through three waves, the final potential indicators have been identified. Starting with the
simple question of what the overall contributors were to energy in a detached dwelling, in wave one.
Continued working on the results from wave one, and combined it with the sustainability factors
(issue-based framework), domain-based framework, and the BUILD report, in wave two. Using
the indicators identified in wave two, evaluating the results, and going back in a loop redeveloping
the indicators, ending up with the final potential indicators, in wave three. Finally, the identified
indicators were compared to the sustainability goals (goal-based framework), and it was evaluated
that the potential indicators would contribute to all the goals defined in step 1.
5.1.6
Step 6: Evaluate the Indicators and Select a Final Set
Step 6 evaluates each potential indicator, identified in step 5, against the selection criteria, defined
in step 4 (Maclaren, 2022). This subsection will go through this evaluation, but before doing that,
the overall scale and overall data processing will be explained, in addition to an explanation of
each indicator, including how the data for each indicator was collected, and how the individual
scale for each indicator works.
The Overall Scale
As explained in Section 1.5, an indicator consists of a unit or a scale, and in this thesis it has
been decided to use a scale, to be able to compare the different results for the different indicators,
in addition, to be able to give each detached dwelling a final grade for how big the renovation
potential is, based on the average of the grade for each of the four indicators. The reason for
needing to give a final grade is to be able to map the areas with the highest renovation potential.
In this thesis, a five-point scale was chosen, with grades going from one to five. Grade one gives
the lowest renovation potential, and grade five gives the highest renovation potential. A five-point
scale was chosen due to it not being too detailed, but still detailed enough to show differences,
and makes it possible to draw conclusions. The data found on the detached dwellings are not
Page 64
==PAGE:78==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
very detailed, because it is an average for all of Denmark, and there is therefore no reason to
choose a more detailed scale. Choosing a scale from one to five makes it possible to get one of the
outer grades, the middle grade, and the two in between, giving the opportunity to see a difference
between each detached dwelling, and making a map that shows differences in potential renovation
areas.
A sixth grade was needed when seeing the results for scenarios including Switching the Heating
Supplier. This is due to grade one representing the lowest renovation potential, this means that
there is a renovation potential present, but when looking at the results, it was seen that some of
the potential savings were not present. This includes the consumption for the scenario Switching
the Heating Supplier, as explained in Subsection 5.1.5, where the difference in consumption before
and after the renovation would be zero. A grade representing no renovation potential, the grade
zero, was therefore added, for the three indicators that consist of scenarios as sub-indicators, giving
them a six-point scale.
The goal of the thesis is to map the renovation potential in Rudersdal which the indicators are
used for. Therefore, the scale must focus on Rudersdal municipality, thus the scale was set in
relation to the maximum and minimum potential in Rudersdal. By doing this, it was possible to
get grades across the spectrum, and in that way find the areas that have the largest potential.
The Overall Data Processing
All the data processing for the four indicators was done in Excel. The BBR data on detached
dwellings was downloaded from KMD Cognito Access 2.1.0 on the 18th of July, as CSV-files and
imported to Excel, as explained in Section 4.1. Then the protected and conservation-worthy build-
ings were removed, consisting of 686 buildings (see Table 10), in addition to duplicates consisting
of six buildings that have been demolished, but not removed from BBR yet, resulting in a total of
10,228 detached dwellings used in this analysis.
Grading Method for the Indicators Consisting of Scenarios
To choose the grading method for indicators Energy Consumption, CO2 Emissions, and Price of
Heating, three different approaches were tried: 1) on the basis of how big the savings were in per-
centage, using maximum value and minimum value to set the scale, 2) on the basis of how big the
savings were in value, using maximum value and minimum value to set the scale, 3) on the basis
of how big the savings were in value, using percentile to set the scale. When trying both approach
one and two, there were some big older houses, setting the maximum value so high, that there were
almost no buildings in grades three to five. This resulted in the majority of the detached dwellings
in Rudersdal receiving grade one, low renovation potential. Even though some buildings have a
huge renovation potential, does not mean that there is a low renovation potential for the rest of
the buildings, and it was therefore decided to use approach three to grade the detached dwellings.
Another reason for not choosing approaches one and two is that if these were chosen, the final map
would not be created in regards to all of the building’s renovation potential, but only these few
big older houses.
The grading method used to distribute the detached dwellings, for the indicators consisting of
scenarios, was percentile. Percentile is generally used to show a proportion or ranking by comparing
one case with several cases. It is given as a number between zero and one, and it represents a
percentage of values found within specific values. An example of this would be if 10 people had
taken a test, one of the subjects answered 80% of the questions correctly, and the other nine
answered more than 90% correctly. Even though the first subject had scored 80% on the test, they
are ranked in the bottom 10th percentile among the other participants. (J¨onsson, 2021; Milhøj,
2013)
Page 65
==PAGE:79==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
Figure 41: Grading Method for the Indicators Consisting of Scenarios, using percentile. This method
was used for each of the three indicators separately.
Figure 41 shows how the percentile method was used to set the scale for the three indicators con-
sisting of scenarios. This method was used for each of the three indicators separately. It was done
by first finding the possible savings for each scenario, for the one indicator to which the scale was
set, resulting in a value that represented how much can be saved for each detached dwelling. These
three lists of 10,228 values each, were combined into one long list of all the possible savings by doing
all three scenarios. From these 30,684 values, the values equal to or below zero were removed, due
to these values having no renovation potential, and would therefore received the grade zero. With
the values left, the 0th, 20th, 40th, 60th, 80th, and 100th percentile was found, setting the upper
values for grade zero, one, two, three, four, and five respectively. The lower values were set as the
0th, 20th, 40th, 60th, and 80th, for grades one, two, three, four, and five respectively. To ensure
that values that were on the edge between two grades, equal to one of the percentiles, were not
counted twice, each grade included the lower values and excluded the upper value, using the upper
values to set the grades to everything that was below it. Grade zero consists of values between
the upper value of the 0th percentile, and the minimum value of all the savings for that indicator.
Grade one consists of values equal to and above the lower value of the 0th percentile, and below
the upper value of the 20th percentile. Grade two to five were set the same way as grade one. By
combining the values for the amount of saving and the percentile method, the scale was set for
indicators of Energy Consumption, CO2 Emissions, and Price of Heating, and the result of this
can be seen in Table 15, Table 17 and Table 22. To find each percentile, the PERCENTILE.INC
function in Excel was used.
The percentile method assigned the 20% worst detached dwellings in grade five, the highest renova-
tion potential. This fits great with the sustainability goal of helping the municipality and residents
prepare for the new EPBD initiative, because the new possible labelling system puts the 15% least
energy-efficient residences in label G, and both buildings with labels G and F need to be improved
by 2033.
Assigning Which Heating Supplier to Switch To
Scenario Switching the Heating Supplier, and Switching the Heating Supplier and Improving the
Building’s Envelope, includes the switching of a heating supplier. Due to limited amount of time
and the big amount of data, it was only possible to check for one heating supplier switch. After
reading Rudersdal’s heating plan (see Subsection 3.8.2 for more information), it was believed that
this was a great opportunity to check the impact this plan has on preparing for the EU initiative.
The heating plan consists of three phases, where buildings within phase one will be offered district
heating in 2023-2025, and phase two in 2026-2027. Phase three is not decided yet, but the plan will
be developed in 2028-2029, and hopefully offer district heating between 2028-2035. The following
was therefore decided in regard to switching heating supplier:
• Detached dwellings within phases one and two, switch to district heating.
Page 66
==PAGE:80==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
• Detached dwellings within phase three and not included in the plan, switch to heating pumps.
• Detached dwellings that already have district heating or heating pumps, keep this as their
heating supply.
By doing this, the result was that the detached dwellings had either district heating or a heating
pump after the change. The reason behind not including phase three in the district heating, was
because it has not been planned yet, and to get the buildings ready for the initiative requirement
for 2030, the building owners should look for another alternative. From the Literature Review, it
was seen that heating pumps were the most sustainable option for a heating supplier, which was
the reason behind choosing this as the alternative to district heating. Detached dwellings with
district heating or heating pumps have no reason to change. Getting as many detached dwellings
as possible connected to district heating, is the plan for Rudersdal, and heating pumps are in
general new to the market, and all heating pumps should therefore be able to serve five to ten
years more, according to Per Nielsen. Nielsen also explains that Lyngby-Taarbæk municipality has
offered their residents the option of being connected to district heating without turning it on, and
therefore not using it before the heating pump is obsolete and not usable anymore.
Marie Gammelmark, from the Environmental and Technical Department in Rudersdal, provided a
shape-file for each of the three phases in the heating plan. This shape-file was imported into QGIS,
where each phase was saved as a new layer, and used to find all the detached dwellings within each
phase, which was possible by using ”Select by location” in QGIS. From this, it was possible to copy
the attribute table from QGIS into Excel and assign a value to each detached dwelling, depending
on which phase they were included in. By comparing this value to the heating supply they already
have according to BBR, the heating supplier the detached dwellings switch to can be assigned. If
no switch occurs, if they already have district heating or a heating pump, they just get the same
heating supply, and it can be seen in the results by not having any impact.
In regards to the heating suppliers used today, it is seen in the Initial Analysis of Buildings in
Rudersdal, Figure 36, that 0.1% are registered as other in BBR. This includes seven buildings,
including four buildings with central heating with one heating unit, one building with central heat-
ing with two heating unit, and three buildings with boiler with solid or liquid fuel. To solve the
problem of not knowing what type of heating supply these buildings have, the BBR experts at
Rudersdal municipality were contacted. They check these seven buildings, and could see that it was
the building owners that had registered it, and there was no reason why. It was therefore decided
to assign heating supply to these buildings, based on what was most likely compared to the other
detached dwellings in Rudersdal, with the same heating installation. For the five buildings with
central heating, this was gas, but unfortunately for boiler with solid or liquid fuel, it was 50-50
between oil and biomass. It was therefore decided to investigate what the nearest neighbour with
the same heating installation, had as heating supply, and this was biomass for all three buildings,
and this was therefore the assigned heating supply for these three buildings.
Indicator: Energy Consumption
The Energy Consumption indicator was developed from Figure 27, which shows the consumption
for a detached dwelling today, and Figure 30, which shows the consumption after improving the
building’s envelope, including increased comfort levels. Both figures have the unit kWh/m2 p.a.,
depending on the year built. By using the year built and area from BBR, the consumption before
and after improving the building’s envelope, in kWh p.a., was found. To find the possible savings
by improving the building’s envelope, the following formula was used:
Consumption today [kWh p.a.] - Consumption after improving the building’s envelope [kWh p.a.]
= Possible savings for energy consumption [kWh p.a.]
This was the explanation of the scenario: Improving the Building’s Envelope. The scenario: Switch-
ing the Heating Supplier, is zero for all detached dwellings, as explained in Subsection 5.1.5. Re-
sulting in the last scenario: Switching the Heating Supplier and Improving the Building’s Envelope,
to be exactly the same formula and results as in the scenario: Improving the Building’s Envelope.

Indicator: CO2 Emissions
The CO2 Emissions indicator was developed from Table 7, which shows the CO2 emissions for
detached dwellings, depending on the different heating suppliers, in the unit kg-CO2/MWh. The
first scenario, Improving the Building’s Envelope, was developed using the energy consumption be-
fore and after improving the building’s envelope, calculated in the Energy Consumption indicator,
dividing it by 1000, to go from kWh to MWh, and multiplying it with the kg-CO2/MWh values
found in Table 7, depending on the heating supplier registered in BBR. The following two formulas
show how this was done:
(Consumption today [kWh p.a.] / 1000) * The heating supplier’s CO2 emissions [kg-CO2/MWh]
= The CO2 emissions emitted today [kg-CO2 p.a.]
(Consumption after improving the building’s envelope [kWh p.a.] / 1000) *
The heating supplier’s CO2 emissions [kg-CO2/MWh] =
The CO2 emissions emitted after improving the building’s envelope [kg-CO2 p.a.]
To find the possible savings by improving the building’s envelope, the following formula was used:
The CO2 emissions emitted today [kg-CO2 p.a.] -
The CO2 emissions emitted after improving the building’s envelope [kg-CO2 p.a.] =
Possible savings for CO2 emissions after improving the building’s envelope
The second scenario, Switching the Heating Supplier, was a combination between Table 7 and the
change of heating supplier, as explained in Assigning Which Heating Supplier to Switch To. This
was done by using the following formulas:
(Consumption today [kWh p.a.] / 1000) * The heating supplier’s CO2 emissions [kg-CO2/MWh]
= The CO2 emissions emitted today [kg-CO2 p.a.]
(Consumption today [kWh p.a.] / 1000) * The switched heating supplier’s CO2 emissions
[kg-CO2/MWh] = The CO2 emissions emitted after switching the heating supplier [kg-CO2 p.a.]
The CO2 emissions emitted today [kg-CO2 p.a.] -
The CO2 emissions emitted after switching the heating supplier [kg-CO2 p.a.] =
Possible savings for CO2 emissions after switching the heating supplier
It is seen that the first formula for switching the heating supplier is the same as improving the
building’s envelope. The difference between the first and second formulas, are in the change of
heating supplier, resulting in finding the possible savings for CO2 emissions after switching the
heating supplier.
The last scenario, Switching the Heating Supplier and Improving the Building’s Envelope, is a
combination between the two earlier scenarios, and the following formulas are used:
(Consumption today [kWh p.a.] / 1000) * The heating supplier’s CO2 emissions [kg-CO2/MWh]
= The CO2 emissions emitted today [kg-CO2 p.a.]
(Consumption after improving the building’s envelope [kWh p.a.] / 1000) * The switched heating
supplier’s CO2 emissions [kg-CO2/MWh] = The CO2 emissions emitted after switching the
heating supplier and after improving the building’s envelope [kg-CO2 p.a.]
The CO2 emissions emitted today [kg-CO2 p.a.] - The CO2 emissions emitted after switching the
heating supplier and after improving the building’s envelope [kg-CO2 p.a.] = Possible savings for
CO2 emissions after switching the heating supplier and after improving the building’s envelope
It is seen that the first formula is the same as the other two scenarios, and the second formula is a
combination of the two scenarios, resulting in finding the possible savings for switching the heating
supplier and after improving the building’s envelope.

Indicator: Price of Heating
The Price of Heating indicator was developed using the prices in Table 12, which is taken from
Bolius’s website (Rasmussen and Patursson, 2023). Bolius is a knowledge center with the aim of
improving the quality of life for everyone, by making knowledge about housing accessible and us-
able. It is a non-commercial enterprise, owned by the philanthropic association Realdania (Bolius,
n.d.). In Table 12, it is seen that district heating has, in addition to a price per kWh, a fixed
charge. Bolius has two prices for electricity, one called electricity and another called electric heat-
ing. This indicator uses electric heating, whereas electricity is assumed to be referred to other
usage of electricity than for heating. Prices for different heating pumps can also be seen in Bolius,
where the air to water heating pump has been chosen, due to it being the worst case scenario
(most expensive), and is the most typical according to Per Nielsen. Lastly, there are two types of
biomass to choose from, beech wood and wood pellets, where beech wood was chosen for the same
reasons as the heating pump.
Table 12: Price of different heating supplies
(Rasmussen and Patursson, 2023).
To find the possible savings in the prices for each scenario, the same formulas as in the CO2
Emissions indicator have been used, but by changing CO2 emissions with the price of heating and
removing the division of 1000, due to the price unit being in DKK/kWh. The prices are matched
with the type of heating supplier, bearing in mind that district heating has a fixed charge, which is
added at the end of both the first and second formula of each scenario, before in the end calculating
the possible savings for the price of heating by doing each scenario.
Indicator: Energy Label
The Energy Label indicator investigates status quo, and does not include any scenarios, as ex-
plained in Subsection 5.1.5. To find data on the energy labels in Rudersdal, Energimærkning-
sordningen was contacted but was not able to provide data for all detached dwellings combined.
They are working on a new platform, but it would not be finished within the thesis time span.
All channels that could be thought of were contacted, and Marie Gammelmark, from the Envi-
ronmental and Technical Department in Rudersdal, had a solution: Boliganalysen. Boliganalysen
is a tool that has been developed to make it easier for municipalities to send out information,
about e.g. energy renovations, whereas it is a platform where information from BBR is combined
with energy labelling data, in addition to information about energy savings (Boliganalysen, n.d.).
A username and password were provided by Gammelmark, making it possible to collect data on
detached dwellings in Rudersdal, by downloading a CSV-file and importing it into Excel. The data
included three buildings without any addresses, and 23 buildings that were not included in the
BBR data, which were assumed demolished, and therefore removed from the data set. Addition-
ally, the protected and conservation-worthy buildings were removed, consisting of 299 buildings.
12 duplicates were found and removed from the data set. These duplicate buildings have been
demolished, but still have a valid energy label, probably due to getting an energy label before
being sold, and then demolished by the new owners. This resulted in a total of 3,973 energy labels
used in this analysis.
To be able to give all the 10,228 detached dwellings a grade, and later map them, the data from Boli-
ganalysen needed to be connected to the data from BBR. This was done by using the VLOOKUP
function in Excel, connecting ”ADG ID” from BBR with ”Adgangs-ID” from Boliganalysen, which
Page 69
==PAGE:83==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
both represent the same value and is the same value for each detached dwelling. The result of this
was a column in Excel showing if a detached dwelling had an energy label, and if so which one.
Table 13: Grading scale for the indicator Energy Label.
How each detached dwelling, got its grade, can be seen in Table 13. For the indicator Energy Label,
decimals were introduced. The energy labelling system today has a total of nine energy labels,
which cannot be divided by five, to fit them to the indicators’ five-point scale. The decimals solved
this problem and ensured that all energy labels were weighted equally. Additionally, there were
detached dwellings that did not have any energy label, that needed to be graded. As explained
in Section 3.6, getting an energy label consists of getting an overview of possible energy-efficient
improvements that can be made, with a highlight of the three most profitable ones. This means
by having a label, the building owners already know the condition of the house, in addition to
what can be done, which means that owners that have an energy label are further in the process
than owners without. Section 3.6 also explains that the energy labelling system today requires
that all new building are labelled. This requirement has been a law since 2010, which means that
buildings built in the last 13 years have been labelled. A label is valid for 10 years, resulting in all
new buildings that have been built in the last 10 years being a part of the data given by Boliganal-
ysen. Based on this information, it was decided that detached dwellings without an energy label
were given grade five, representing the highest renovation potential in regard to energy labelling.
Additionally, it was ensured that buildings built in the last 10 years were not given the grade five.
Evaluation of the Potential Indicators
All four indicators were fully developed, as explained above, and were ready to be evaluated based
on the selection criteria defined in step 4. The result of this evaluation can be seen in Table 14,
and it is seen that all selection criterion are fulfilled except Based on raw and available data, which
was almost fulfilled for three of the indicators. All three indicators were based on available data,
but one could argue that they were not based on raw data for each detached dwelling. The Energy
Consumption indicator, the CO2 Emissions indicator, and the Price of Heating indicator, use data
from the BUILD report 2021:08, which is an average of detached dwellings with an energy label
in all of Denmark, depending on the year built. This means that it is an achievable renovation
potential, due to this is how much BUILD has calculated a building can save by energy renovat-
ing, and it is the most likely condition of the building today, but it is not the raw data and true
condition of the detached dwelling. There was unfortunately no available data on this.
Table 14: Evaluation of the potential indicators based on the selection criteria defined
in step 4. Symbol explanation: X = fulfilled and / = almost fulfilled.

5.1.7
Step 8: Prepare and Present Report
The final set of indicators was chosen for this thesis and used as explained in Subsection 5.1.6.
When presenting the results, the challenge was the huge data set of 10,228 buildings per sub-
indicator. It was not possible to show the results for each detached dwelling, and it was therefore
decided to show statistics for all the indicators.
In the indicators, Energy Consumption, CO2
Emissions, and Price of Heating, the upper and lower value for each grade, in addition to the
percentage of buildings in each grade, can be seen in the first table, presented in each indicator
chapter. The second table shows how many buildings that were in each grade, in addition to how
big a percentage of each grade each scenario got. For the indicators CO2 Emissions and Price of
Heating, some additional information about the different heating suppliers is shown. The Energy
Label indicator shows the percentage of buildings in each label and the amount and percentage of
buildings in each grade.
5.2
Indicator: Energy Consumption
Results of the indicator Energy Consumption will be looked into in this section. These results are
not as interesting compared to the other three indicators, whereas there is no renovation potential
for the scenario After Switching the Heating Supplier, as explained in Subsection 5.1.5. This can
be seen in both Table 15, which shows the grade ranges and the percentage of how many detached
dwellings are in each grade, for the three different scenarios, and Table 16, which shows the grade
distribution between the three scenarios and the total amount of buildings in each grade. Both
tables show that 100% of the buildings in the scenario After Switching the Heating Supplier, have
the grade zero. Consequently, as seen in Table 15, the two other scenarios have the same percentage
of buildings in each grade, roughly 20%, since only the improvement of the detached dwelling’s
envelope, influences the indicator. In the Table 16, it is seen that the other two scenarios divide
each grade above zero equally.
Table 15: Grade Ranges for the indicator Energy Consumption after applying the 3 different
scenarios and the percentage of detached dwellings in each grade for the 3 different scenarios.
Additionally, the upper and lower value for each grade in kWh p.a. can be seen in Table 15. As
described in Subsection 5.1.6 the upper and lower values were found using percentiles, meaning
that every building’s value above zero, would be distributed evenly into grades one to five, for the
three different scenarios. Grade zero indicates that there is no renovation potential, included in
the tables are detached dwellings that have received a value equal to zero or lower. Looking at
the grade ranges for grades one to five, it can be seen that grade five has the biggest difference
between upper and lower values, with a 16,351 kWh p.a. difference. This large difference in grade
five is due to a few very big and old detached dwellings in Rudersdal, where the buildings are over
400m2 and built before 1960. The smallest difference between upper and lower values is in grade
two, with a 690 kWh p.a. difference.

5.3
Indicator: CO2 Emissions
Results of the indicator CO2 Emissions will be looked into in this section. Like in Section 5.2
Table 17 shows the grade ranges, the upper and lower value in kg-CO2 p.a for each grade, and
the percentage of detached dwellings within each grade, and Table 18 shows the grade distribution
between the three different scenarios, and the total amount of detached dwellings within each grade
for the three scenarios.
Looking at Table 17 the biggest difference between upper and lower values is in grade five, with
a difference of 8,941 kg-CO2 p.a. Furthermore, it can be seen that 59.5% of detached dwellings
within the scenario After Switching the Heating Supplier and Improving the Building’s Envelope
have a grade of four or higher, meaning that this is the renovation scenario where most detached
dwellings will save the most CO2 if renovated. It can be seen that the least potential, not counting
grade zero, comes from the After Improving the Building’s Envelope scenario in that 85.2% of
detached dwellings have a grade of two or lower. There are a few buildings that have no potential
in the After Switching the Heating Supplier scenario, which is due to the detached dwellings that
already have a heating pump or district heating, they were assumed to keep their heating supplier,
so their potential would be zero. The After Switching the Heating Supplier and Improving the
Building’s Envelope scenario also has a small amount of detached dwellings with a zero grade, this
is due to the buildings that have biomass as a heating supplier, where the CO2 emissions are lower
than district heating and heating pumps, which means that when switching to these, there is an
increase in CO2 emissions.
Table 17: Grade Ranges for the indicator CO2 Emissions after applying the 3 different
scenarios and the percentage of detached dwellings in each grade for the 3 different scenarios.
Table 18 confirms the same things as Table 17, where it is shown that 60% of the detached dwellings
that received a grade five were in the scenario After Switching the Heating Supplier and Improving
the Building’s Envelope. This would therefore mean that the renovation that will save the most
CO2, is to both switch the heating supplier and improve the building’s envelope. It can also be seen

dwellings in the middle grades with 44.60% receiving a grade three, but most of the buildings in
this scenario are in the higher grades.
From these two tables it can be concluded that the type of heating supplier has a huge impact on
CO2 emissions. The two scenarios where the heating supplier is changed, results in more detached
dwellings with a higher grade meaning they have the highest renovation potential and therefore the
highest potential to save CO2. This is caused by the majority of detached dwellings in Rudersdal
being supplied by gas, thereby switching to either district heating or heating pumps, will lead to
a decrease in CO2 emissions.

Figure 42 shows the percentage of detached dwellings in each energy label in Rudersdal munici-

label have a D label, including 14% of all buildings in Rudersdal. The second highest percentage
of detached dwellings have an energy label C, followed by energy label E. There are only 4% of
detached dwellings that have an energy label lower than F, 3% F and 1% G. While only 5% of
detached dwellings in Rudersdal have an energy label of A, 1% A2020, 2% A2015, and 2% A2010.
It can also be seen that 1% of detached dwellings in Rudersdal have an energy label B.
Figure 42: Percentage of detached dwellings, in Rudersdal municipality, in
each energy label according to data from Boliganalysen. It is seen that 61% of
the detached dwellings do not have an energy label.
From Table 27 and Figure 43 the grade ranges, amount of detached dwellings in each grade, and
the percentage of each detached dwelling in each grade can be seen. From Table 27, and as explain
in Subsection 5.1.6, the grade ranges go from one to five, with A2020 getting a grade of one and G
and None getting a grade of five. G and None would therefore have the most potential to renovate
because they have the worst energy label or have no energy label at all, meaning they either have
a lot to improve during a renovation, or they know less about which improvements need to be
made compared to buildings with an energy label. From Table 27, it can be seen that most of the
detached dwellings, 6350 buildings, in Rudersdal have a grade of five. This can also be seen from
Figure 43 where 62% of detached dwellings in Rudersdal have received a grade of five. Therefore
most buildings in the Energy Label indicator would have a high potential to renovate.
