5.6
Discussion
This section will go through a discussion of the development and use of the indicators, a discussion
of the indicators’ results, and step 9 of Maclaren’s steps in the urban sustainability reporting
process: assessment of the indicators’ performance.
5.6.1
Discussion of the Development and Use of the Indicators
Even though Maclaren’s steps in the urban sustainability reporting process were followed, it was
overall a complicated and complex process, with multiple loops between the steps, and between
the overall phases of this thesis. One could think it would have been more effective to finish the
Information Gathering phase before developing indicators, and only take one loop back to the In-
formation Gathering phase to use the indicators. The reason behind the complicated process was
that it was not possible to predict all the information and data needed to develop the indicators,
but the question is if this is truly correct. Would it not have been better to dive deeper into the
literature and available data before starting the development, and thereby try to predict what was
needed to develop the indicators? Whereas the thesis had a strict time span, it was hard to not
feel the pressure of starting the development phase, but maybe the most optimal process would
have been to hold it off until the best literature and data were found. What is the best literature
and data, and how would you know where to stop? On the other hand, one could turn it around
and argue that the thesis process should have started on the development earlier, and even maybe
before starting the literature, and thereby having a more precise and focused literature review.
One could therefore conclude that there is no clear answer to what the best process would have
been.
A big limitation of the development was the absence of social sustainability. The social pillar
was hard to measure because social sustainability is very subjective. Yes, the improvement of
the indoor climate is automatically part of an energy renovation, but it does not mean that the
person actually likes the new indoor climate. This could for example be a newly installed ventila-
tion system, which might make more noise or lower the indoor humidity and therefore annoy the
person more than before the energy renovation. The social pillar is automatically a part of doing
Page 78
==PAGE:92==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
an energy renovation, but none of the indicators truly measure what or how big the social impact
is. One could therefore argue that the domain-based framework is not fulfilled, and the indicators
are not based on sustainability factors, but on environmental and economic factors. On the other
hand, the Energy Label indicator includes that getting a label contains getting information on
possible energy improvements. This is especially important for owners without a background in
construction. Still, it is just an automatic part of getting an energy label and does not measure
the social impact. One could though say, by grading everyone without a label to the highest ren-
ovation potential, that the social aspect explained above, is a part of the grading itself. If there
had been more time, it would have been interesting to keep investigating if the social pillar could
have played a more active role in the set of indicators. The social pillar was investigated in the
survey, presented in the next chapter, where people’s thoughts about renovations were explored,
and it was possible to see how big the psychological potential for energy renovation is in Rudersdal.
Another limitation of the indicators was the lack of detailed data on the detached dwellings. One
could argue that the results of the indicators, do not truly say anything about the specific building,
but only what is most likely. When using the results of these indicators, one should keep this in
mind. BUILD report 2021:08 uses detailed data form Energimærkningsordningen, on everything
that has been included when getting an energy label, for all detached dwellings that had an energy
label in October 2020. Even though 61% of Rudersdal’s detached dwellings do not have an energy
label, it would have been great to get this detailed data on the detached dwellings that do have
an energy label. When contacting Energimærkningsordningen, they refereed to the following link:
https://emoweb.dk/emodata/test/. They provided a username and password for this thesis, but
when trying to extract the information, it was seen that one needed experience in Application
Programming Interfaces (APIs), which neither Energimærkningsordningen nor the municipality
could provide help with. Energimærkningsordningen went on to explain that later this year, they
will launch a new platform where one can see and retrieve data in a different and easier way, but
unfortunately not within the thesis time span. If this data had been available for this thesis, it
could have solved three of the four limitations listed in Subsection 5.1.5. However, there would
still be missing data on 61% of the detached dwellings. A solution for this in the future, could be
if it was a lawful requirement that all detached dwellings had to have a valid energy label and that
the data from each energy label was included in the BBR data. This could make a big difference
in future sustainable city planning.
The district heating data on CO2 and price, used in the indicators, are based on averages for all
of Denmark. There can be a big difference, both in CO2 and price, when it comes to the different
district heating networks in the country. According to Forsyningstilsynet, the price depends on
the choice in heating supplier, the network itself, including heat loss in the wiring, the location in
relation to customers, the size and the ownership (Energitilsynet, 2012; Gregersen, 2023). Whereas
it is seen in Table 7 that there is a big difference in kg-CO2 depending on the heating supplier,
and that the heating supplier can differ within the different district heating network, in addition
to heat loss in the wiring, the CO2 emissions emitted in district heating can differ a lot. All of
this means that the detached dwellings with district heating in Rudersdal can have a bigger or
smaller renovation potential than is seen for the average Dane. According to Rudersdal’s heating
plan, there are two district heating networks in Rudersdal: Holte Fjernvarme and Norfors, and if
there had been more time in this thesis, this would be one of the first things that would have been
investigated further. It is important to keep this in mind when using the indicator results.
All the indicators have included a grade zero, representing no renovation potential, except the
energy label. One could argue that a detached dwelling that has the best energy label, A2020, has
no renovation potential, and should therefore have been assigned a zero. By changing A2020 to
zero, all other energy labels could also have gone one grade down, except the buildings without
any energy labels, which would still get a grade of five. On the other hand, the requirement for the
new EPBD initiative is that buildings with an energy label G have to be energy renovated before
2030, which would argue that G should keep the grade five. With the same argument, one could
argue that F also should get a grade five, whereas buildings with an energy label F have to be
energy renovated before 2033. If G and F should get a grade of five, and grade A2020 should get
a grade of zero, how would the rest of the grades be graded? There would be six left, that needed
Page 79
==PAGE:93==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
to be distributed between four grades (one to four). This would maybe not be a problem, due to
the grading system for the new initiative is different from the one today. One can not be sure if
the buildings that have a grade F today, will get a F after implementing the initiative. The most
optimal way of preparing for the new initiative, would be to include everything that is included
when an energy consultant calculates the energy label today (if the data had been available), use
this to assign an energy label to all the detached dwellings, and from this find the bottom 15%
and assign it the grade G, as the new labelling system will if implemented. If this should be done
correctly, one would need to assess all the residences in Denmark, to find the bottom 15%, and
one would still not be sure, that what is included when calculating the energy label today, will
be exactly the same after the implementation. The calculation today is standardised, and maybe
in the future, one wants to make this more specific. For more information about energy labelling
see Section 3.6. One could argue that the way the Energy Consumption indicator is developed,
by grading the 20% worst detached dwellings with the highest renovation potential, fits great with
the new initiative.
The challenge of using a percentile, as the grading method for the indicators consisting of scenarios,
is that there is a possibility, that buildings with a low renovation potential have gotten a high grade
and the opposite. The percentile method distributes an equal amount of buildings between the five
grades, which means that they are in principle not divided depending on the value, but on how big
the value is compared to the other detached dwellings in Rudersdal. This led to the challenge with
buildings maybe not getting the grade they deserve, but what grade do they deserve? How can one
tell if a building has a high or low renovation potential? This must be in relation to something, but
what should it be in relation to? This thesis has set the overall scale to Rudersdal, due to the goal
being to find the renovation potential in Rudersdal, but do the detached dwellings maybe deserve
to be compared to Denmark instead, or the world? The lower the bar, the less environmental
sustainability, whereas with a high renovation potential, more buildings will be energy renovated,
but it is still important to not set the bar too high because then it will not be achievable, which
could lead to people not taking it seriously. If the comparison should be to Denmark, instead of
Rudersdal, one could argue that the detached dwellings get their grade already in relation to the
renovation potential in Denmark, because the data used for all three indicators are the average
data in Denmark.
5.6.2
Discussion of the Indicators’ Results
Looking at the Energy Consumption, CO2 Emissions and Price of Heating indicators, it could be
seen that generally the scenario After Switching the Heating Supplier and Improving the Building’s
Envelope, is the scenario that leads to the highest renovation potential, because most detached
dwelling receive a grade of five when this scenario is applied. When looking at the scenario After
Improving the Building’s Envelope, it could be seen that there was always a potential to apply this
scenario, because none of the indicators for this scenario ended up receiving a zero grade. This was
due to the fact that an improvement of the building’s envelope, would lead to lower consumption in
all detached dwellings in the municipality, and would therefore always lead to an improved poten-
tial because all the values for the different indicator scenarios are found through the consumption.
It could also be seen that there was a higher potential to save kWh and money when applying the
After Improving the Building’s Envelope scenario, with a smaller potential to save CO2, because
the grades were in the low end compared to the two other scenarios for this indicator, which proves
that switching to district heating or a heat pump generally lead to higher potential savings in CO2
emissions. This was however not the case for biomass (beech wood), where there was no potential
to switch the heating supplier to district heating or a heating pump, there was not even potential
for it when improving the building’s envelope and switching heat suppliers, which was due to the
fact that biomass (beech wood) emits very little CO2, but the downside to biomass heat suppliers
is that they then burn a lot of wood. This burning of wood could then cause deforestation, which
in the future can be a problem if the planet runs out of wood, which could happen if people cut
down trees faster than they can grow back. The CO2 numbers from the BUILD report do not
take the transportation of the heating supplier or the extraction of the raw material into account,
which both could be high for biomass (beech wood). However, the extraction and transport of the
Page 80
==PAGE:94==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
heating supplier, can affect the CO2 of all of the heating suppliers, which would mean that the
CO2 results could be even more specific for the different heating suppliers, if the transport and
extraction of raw materials were taken into account.
Switching the heating supplier to district heating or heating pumps generally leads to a lower
potential in saving money. This is because the heat is supplied by gas in most of the detached
dwellings in Rudersdal, which is one of the cheapest options for heat today. The only way for
there to be potential for a gas-supplied detached dwelling to save money, would be to switch to
a heating pump or to improve the building’s envelope, since the consumption would be lower and
therefore cost less. Gas, when switched to district heating or heat pumps, had however one of the
biggest potentials when it came to CO2 emissions. When it comes to gas heat supply and biomass
heat supply, switching to district heating or heating pumps, there will be some gives and takes;
where biomass is better for the environment but costs more, as seen by biomass receiving a grade
of zero for CO2 emissions, but mainly receiving a grade of five for price, and where gas costs less
but is worse for the environment, seen by gas mainly receiving a grade of zero or one for price,
but five for CO2 emissions. This means that the detached dwellings with gas or biomass as heat
supply, have gotten a high grade in one of the indicators CO2 Emissions or Price of Heating, but
a low grade in the other. Electric heating and oil heat suppliers are more straightforward because
the potential to switch is relatively high, for CO2 emission the detached dwellings with these heat
suppliers mainly received a grade of three, and for the price, they mainly received a grade of five.
As explained in Subsection 5.1.6, one of the reasons behind the scenarios was to check the im-
pact Rudersdal’s heating plan has on preparing for the EU initiative, but to do this, one needs
to know what the new energy labelling system includes when labelling. If it uses the definition of
energy-efficient renovations, seen in Subsection 3.2.4, as discussed in Section 3.9, it includes energy
consumption from heating, cooling, domestic hot water, and electricity, in addition to solar or
wind energy. When focusing on the energy consumption part, it can be compared to the Energy
Consumption indicator developed in this thesis, and from this, it is seen that type of heat supplier,
has no influence on the energy consumption. However, solar or wind energy are also a part of
energy renovations, which would indicate that the type of heat supply could have an influence.
As stated in Subsection 3.8.2, Rudersdal is working on making the district heating fossil-free, and
changing to renewable energy sources by 2035. Comparing this to the solar or wind energy part
of the definition, one could argue that the heating plan will have a great impact on preparing for
the EU initiative. Going back to the impacts today, it is seen in Table 7 that district heating
and heating pumps have approximately the same CO2 emission impact, where only biomass has
less impact. Looking at prices in Table 12, it is seen that the kWh price for district heating and
heating pumps are the two cheapest options, followed closely by gas boilers, but one needs to be
aware of district heating having an extra expense - the fixed charge. Comparing this, with what
has been discussed earlier about the indicator results, it is seen that switching heat supplier has
a high positive environmental potential, and one could therefore argue that the heating plan has
an impact on preparing for the EU initiative, except for buildings that do not switch heat supply,
and buildings with biomass, as discussed above. However, the economical impact is smaller due to
the low expense of gas, and the high usage of gas in the municipality, though one could argue that
gas is an unreliable heat supply, whereas one does not know if a new energy crisis will occur this
winter, or in the years to come, and the prices for gas can skyrocket again. Additionally, there is
a high positive economical impact for all the other heating suppliers, including oil, biomass and
electric heating.
As the data on the detached dwellings in Rudersdal comes from BBR, the data could be outdated
on the buildings, as explained in Section 4.1. The BBR data used to calculate the grades are the
year built, building area, and the heating installation and heating supplier, which should therefore
be as updated as possible, to make the results as correct as possible. As explained in Section 4.1, it
is the building owners who are responsible for keeping it updated and therefore, there is a chance
that this has not been done. This could cause some of the indicators results to be wrong, in that
an owner has to update if they e.g. switch from a central heating unit that is supplied by gas, to
district heating. A recommendation to Denmark’s municipalities is, therefore, to remind building
owners to update their detached dwelling’s data in BBR, to ensure as updated data as possible,
Page 81
==PAGE:95==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
and that data used in these types of analysis are building data today and not data for e.g. 20
years ago. Another recommendation would be to make BBR more specific with more details of the
buildings. As explained in Section 4.1, BBR has no data on materials used, other than cladding
materials on exterior walls, and roofs, and from the literature review, it was seen that building
materials are a big reason why buildings emit a lot of CO2, and building parts influence how much
heat the building loses. Additionally, BBR has no data on insulation, window types etc., which
would be helpful to know because then the heating consumption can be found for each specific
detached dwelling in Rudersdal. This would make the indicators even more specific and accurate
for that building, instead of using the average heat consumption given in the BUILD report.
5.6.3
Step 9: Assessment of the Indicators’ Performance
The indicators fulfil the goal, scope, framework, and selection criteria, defined in steps one to four,
to a somewhat degree. To truly help the municipality and its residents to prepare for the new EPBD
initiative, more raw detailed data on the detached dwellings are needed, as well as information on
the renovation process. Additionally, the social pillar of sustainability needs to be measured to truly
call it a domain-based framework. Within the limitations of this thesis, the indicators performed
as well as they could, giving results for each detached dwelling in the municipality, that can be
used in future planning and in the building application processes (byggesagsbehandling).
5.7
Key Takeaways
Energy Consumption:
For the scenarios Improving the Building’s Envelope and Switching the Heating Supplier and Im-
proving the Building’s Envelope, the potential saving in kWh p.a. is the same, so one is not better
than the other, in that the detached dwellings are equally spread in each grade for both scenarios.
For the scenario After Switching the Heating Supplier, it could be seen that there was no potential
savings in kWh p.a., in that every building in this scenario received a grade of zero.
There was a total of 4078 buildings that got the grade five in the Energy Consumption indicator,
and are among the 20% highest renovation potentials when it comes to energy consumption in
Rudersdal.
CO2 Emissions:
The scenario Switching the Heating Supplier and Improving the Building’s Envelope has the highest
potential to save on CO2 emissions, because most detached dwellings in this scenario have a grade
of five and mainly have higher grades. Whereas scenario Switching the Heating Supplier also has a
lot of detached dwellings in higher grades, it can be seen that switching to either district heating or
a heat pump from has the highest impact on CO2 emissions, compared to improving the building’s
envelope.
For the scenario Improving the Building’s Envelope, renovating the gas-supplied detached dwellings
has the highest potential, but in general, there is a low CO2 saving potential for this scenario.
For the scenario, Switching the Heating Supplier, renovating the gas-supplied detached dwellings
have also the highest potential, when it comes to CO2 emissions, but oil and electric heating,
switching to district heating or a heat pump, also have a high potential, with electric heating
having a slightly higher potential.
Renovating the gas-supplied detached dwellings also has the highest potential for the scenario
Switching the Heating Supplier and Improving the Building’s Envelope. For the majority of de-
tached dwellings, it is worth both switching and improving the building envelope, except for de-
tached dwellings with district heating or a heat pump, which has the same potential as only
improving the building envelope. For biomass, it is better to only improve the building envelope
than switch the heating supply, in regard to CO2 emissions, due to biomass having lower CO2
Page 82
==PAGE:96==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
emissions than district heating and heat pump.
There was a total of 5660 buildings that got the grade five in the CO2 Emissions indicator, and
are among the 20% highest renovation potential when it comes to CO2 emissions in Rudersdal.
Price of Heating:
Scenarios Improving the Building’s Envelope and Switching the Heating Supplier and Improving
the Building’s Envelope have the highest potential to save money, as the detached dwellings in
these two scenarios have higher grades than the Switching the Heating Supplier scenario.
For the scenario Improving the Building’s Envelope, the highest potential comes from detached
dwellings with oil, biomass (beech wood), and electric heating, due to most of the detached
dwellings with these heating suppliers, receiving a grade of five. No detached dwellings received a
grade of zero for this scenario, leading to all detached dwellings saving money by improving the
building’s envelope.
For the scenario Switching the Heating Supplier, there is also a high potential to switch heating
supplier, for detached dwellings that have oil, biomass (beech wood), and electric heating, because
most detached dwellings with these heating suppliers received a grade of five. Detached dwellings
with electric heating have the highest potential, 100% received a grade five, meaning that building
owners should definitely switch to district heating or a heating pump, when looking at the price
of heating. The detached dwellings that use gas have a low potential of saving money, if switching
heating supplier, since gas is a cheap option.
For the scenario Switching the Heating Supplier and Improving the Building’s Envelope, there is
again a high potential to switch heating supplier, for detached dwellings that have oil, biomass
(beech wood), and electric heating, because most detached dwellings with these heating suppliers
received a grade of five. All detached dwellings that have electric heating, all detached dwellings
that are supplied by oil and switch to a heating pump, and all detached dwellings that are supplied
by biomass (beech wood) and switch to district heating, received a grade of five, meaning that
building owners should definitely switch heating supplier and improve the building’s envelope, in
regards to the price of heating. Detached dwellings with a gas heating supply have high saving
potential if switching to a heating pump, but low potential if switching to district heating in this
scenario.
There was a total of 4547 buildings that got the grade five in the Price of Heating indicator, and
are among the 20% highest renovation potential when it comes to the price of heating in Rudersdal.
Energy Label:
The results from the Energy Label indicator shows that 62% of the detached dwellings in Rud-
ersdal, are placed in grade five, with the highest renovation potential. This is due to 61% of the
buildings do not have an energy label, and these are given a five. It can therefore be seen that
Rudersdal has a great energy label potential.
There was a total of 6350 buildings that got the grade five in Energy Label, which means that they
either had energy label G or none at all.

s164637 & s174617
6
Surveys
The indicators in this thesis were used to investigate the physical renovation potential in Rudersdal.
However, detached dwellings are privately owned, and it does not help that there is a physical
renovation potential, if there is no interest from the owners to carry it out. A survey was therefore
used to evaluate the psychological renovation potential, so that resident’s thoughts about energy
renovations could be understood. Additionally, it is a part of the third and last research question:
Are people interested in renovating their houses? If so what is their motivation to do it?
As explained in Subsection 2.2.3, the survey is, like the indicators, a part of three out of the six
phases; the survey was created in the Development & Creation phase, conducted in the Produce
Results phase, and the results were evaluated in the Evaluation phase. Through a series of loops
between the phases, the survey was refined, arriving at a final set of survey questions used to find
the psychological renovation potential in Rudersdal. This chapter will go through the development
and sharing of the survey, and the survey results, in addition to a discussion and evaluation of the
results, ending with the key takeaways of this chapter.
6.1
Method
This section will go through how the survey was created, starting off by doing a brainstorm, leading
to the initial survey. The initial survey was improved and finished through a set of meetings with
the thesis supervisor and co-workers in the municipality. The finished survey was shared through
the municipality’s Facebook page, and through different private Facebook city groups. The survey
method is explained in more detail in the following subsections.
6.1.1
Identify Potential Questions
The initial survey questions were created through open brainstorming, where all the possible
questions that could be thought of, were written down. The result was 16 questions about people’s
thoughts and knowledge about renovation, building anew and political agendas, questions about
why people renovate, and their thoughts about their homes today. These questions were later
further developed and categorised, leading to the creation of more questions. The following overall
structure was created, excluding the question within each category: 1) basic questions about the
participant, and where and how they lived, 2) questions regarding the condition of the house
they lived in, including how old and the level of obsolescence, 3) if they have any experience in
renovating, and if so, what and how that experience is, 4) general knowledge about renovation, 5)
thoughts about renovation and build anew, and 6) knowledge about political agendas.
6.1.2
Choosing the Type of Survey
A large number of questions were created, but the type of survey had not been defined. To truly
find the psychological renovation potential, in addition to later being able to compare it to the
mapping, a quantitative survey was believed to be the best fit. By choosing a quantitative survey,
the focus would be to get as many answers as possible, thereby getting as good coverage of the
municipality as possible, making the results as representative for all the residents in Rudersdal as
possible. Additionally, it was important to be aware of leading questions and making the survey
anonymous, making sure that people answered as truthfully as possible.
6.1.3
Co-creation Process
From the creation of the initial survey questions, a co-creation process was needed. The idea was
that the municipality maybe had some wishes to questions or topics for the survey, in addition
to gathering information for experienced survey developers. The following people were a part of
finalising the initial survey:
• Jakub Kolarik, professor at DTU.
• Sandra Scheller, from the City Planning Department.
Page 84
==PAGE:98==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
• Per Sieverts Nielsen, the supervisor of this thesis.
• Kasper-Emil Ørtved Tornfeldt, from the Communication Division.
Meeting with Jakub Kolarik
In the meeting with Jakub Kolarik, which has been referred to multiple times throughout this
report, he revealed that he has great experience in surveys. The experience he shared, was the
size of a survey, explaining that making the survey too long would lead to nobody answering it.
He stated that a survey should always be between three to five questions. The potential questions
identified, before the co-creation process, were way too many, and a process of shortening the
survey began, choosing the most important questions.
The restriction of as few questions as
possible was implemented, leading to a total of six questions, four basic questions and two about
energy renovation:
• What is your zip code?
• What type of building do you live in?
• When was the building you live in built?
• Are you the owner or renter of your home?
• What are your thoughts on energy-efficiency renovation of your home?
• Can you elaborate on your answer from the question above?
It was believed that these six questions were the most important ones. The question about the zip
code helped compare the results to the potential of the physical renovation. The type of building
would make it possible to check what the detached dwelling residents had answered, and the year
built would tell something about the condition and age of the building. Lastly knowing if it is
an owner or a renter, could explain the level of interest and amount of options for the partici-
pant. Those were the four basic questions, hereafter a question was made to understand residents
thoughts about energy-efficiency renovation. The last question was made to explore why the par-
ticipant thought what they thought about the renovation question.
Meeting with Sandra Scheller
After creating the six questions, they were presented to city planner Sandra Scheller, who has
great experience in citizen involvement and is working on the future ”Planstrategi” for Rudersdal
municipality. She would therefore be the one to ask for Rudersdal’s wishes for additional questions,
in addition the new temporary head of the City Planning Department Jane Riskjær Hansen, was
also contacted. Hansen had no wishes, but Scheller had multiple recommendations:
• For the six questions listed above, there were answering options for the first five. For the
zip code question, the answering options had only the zip code numbers, but Scheller recom-
mended to add a city name to the zip code, making it easier for the participants to choose
the correct answer. This was therefore added.
• Scheller asked how many replies were wanted, this was therefore later discussed with Nielsen.
She explains that the amount of replies wanted, should affect how much time one should use
on gathering replies.
• To get as many replies as possible as fast as possible in as big an area as possible, she
recommended Facebook. Scheller also recommended sharing the survey through Rudersdal
municipality’s Facebook page, if allowed. We had already thought of both using Facebook
and asking our boss to post it through Rudersdal, it was therefore great to get this idea
confirmed. This possibility was therefore explored by talking to Hansen, who approved the
request, with the requirement that the survey went through the Communication Division in
the municipality, before posting it. This process will be explained later.
• Scheller explained that it was important to give residents a reason to use their time on
answering the survey. Time was therefore used on making a good opening sentence that
would catch people’s eye, and make them intrigued to answer the survey.
Page 85
==PAGE:99==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
After talking to Sandra Scheller a brainstorm started on how to get residents in Rudersdal to be
intrigued to answer the survey. The idea was to make them as affected by the opening sentence on
Facebook as possible, and make them stop their Facebook scrolling, and answer the survey. To do
this a new brainstorm was done, resulting in 13 arguments why they should answer it. To choose
the best ones, the thought behind ethos, pathos, and logos were used, resulting in the following
Facebook post:
Nye energikrav fra EU kan ramme DIG, og gøre DIN bolig usælgelige hvis du ikke renoverer.
To master studerende fra DTU, arbejder sammen med Rudersdal kommune, om hvordan de bedst
kan hjælpe sine borgere imødekomme disse krav. En del af projektet er at undersøge hvilke tanker
borgerne har om renovering. Vi h˚aber derfor DU har mulighed for at svare p˚a 6 spørgsm˚al ang˚aende
din holdning.
The Facebook post above is in Danish, whereas to get as many answers as possible, it was believed
that both the Facebook post and the survey should be in Danish. The main points and adjust-
ments to the post will be explained in English, and the final Facebook post has been translated
below in Mail Correspondence with Kasper-Emil Tornfeldt. The initial Facebook post above is
made intriguing by telling the residents, that a new EU initiative can affect THEM, by making
THEIR residence unsellable, using pathos to appeal to people’s emotions. Ethos has been used,
by stating that it is two DTU students on their masters, working together with the municipality,
who have made this survey. Logos has been used by arguing that they should answer the survey
because their answers will be used to improve their conditions, by helping the municipality, help
them avoid having an unsellable house.
Meeting with Per Nielsen
In the meeting with the thesis supervisor Per Nielsen, both the survey questions and the Facebook
post were discussed, in addition to the reply target of the survey. From this meeting, it was seen
that a question about energy labels was missing from the tentative survey questions. There were
already six questions, and therefore already one question more than what Kolarik recommended.
The energy label plays such a big part in this thesis and the new EPBD initiative, that it was
decided to remove one of the initial questions and ad one about energy labels. The second ques-
tion: What type of building do you live in? was removed, due to this being assessed to be the least
important compared to the other five. This was assessed, because it was not believed that there
would be enough answers from owners of detached dwellings, from all the different zip codes in the
municipality, to be representative enough, to be able to make a conclusion of it. It was therefore
believed that zip code, year built, and owner or renter, were more important questions, in addi-
tion to the two main questions about energy renovation. The energy label question added was:
Do you know if the building you live in has a valid energy label? And if so, do you know which one?
The feedback on the Facebook post was two minor alterations, adding two words two different
places: 1) ”(...), der arbejder sammen med Rudersdal kommune (...)”, and 2) ”(...) undersøge
hvilke tanker kommunens borgere har om renovering. (...)”. The second should be added to make
it more transparent who should answer and who will be affected by the answers. Additionally, he
recommended adding how long it would take to answer the survey, which was agreed upon would
take approx. 2 minutes. This was therefore added at the end of the Facebook post. Lastly, the
reply target was discussed, and due to the indicators being the main thesis activity and the survey
being a sub-task, the reply target was set to between 100 and 150 replies.
Mail Correspondence with Kasper-Emil Tornfeldt
As explained earlier, to be able to post the survey through Rudersdal’s Facebook page, Hansen
requested that the survey went through the Communication Division in the municipality. This
was seen as a great opportunity to make the survey and the Facebook post as good as possible, by
being checked by a communication expert in Rudersdal. Additionally to asking if they could post
it on Facebook, it was asked if it could be posted on Rudersdal’s website. Unfortunately, Tornfeldt
explained that the website was not possible, but that they would like to help post it on Facebook.
The survey was approved as it was, but the Facebook post was too bombastic. He rewrote it, sent
it back to be approved by us, and posted it on Facebook. Rudersdal as a working place has an

internal company website, and after request, the survey was also posted there. The final Facebook

6.1.4
Testing the Survey
Before posting the survey on Facebook, it was tested on a small group of people at an event at
Birkerød Library. In week 17 in 2023, Birkerød Library had Sustainable Development Goals week.
A part of this week was a lecture by Kristine Vir´en from Bolius, on green housing and sustain-
able renovations, on the 24th of April. When realising this, it was decided to buy tickets for this
lecture, to explore what the municipality tells their citizens. The lecture mainly focused on the
use of electricity, and therefore less relevant to this thesis, but before attending the lecture, it was
decided that it was a great opportunity to test the survey we had created. The initial survey was
handed out in paper format, in addition to a pen, before the lecture started. There was a total
of 13 participants, and all of them answered the initial survey. The test survey can be seen in
Appendix A Section A.2. The test method was not to ask them for feedback on the survey, but to
use the answers to refine the questions, thinking that they will probably comment on the paper if
something is unclear, or if an answering option was missing.
By testing the survey, it was possible to see that the survey worked as was hoped. Even though
we were standing close by all the time they were answering, no one asked any questions. The main
altering done on the basis of the test, was done to one of the answering options to the energy label
question. The answering option translated to English was ”Yes, the house has a valid energy label,
but I can’t remember which one.”, which was changed to ”Yes, the house has a valid energy label,
but I don’t know which one.”
After the test of the initial survey, all the parts of the survey were worked on and refined. The parts
consisted of an information part in the beginning, the survey questions, and the answering options
to the questions. The information part explained to the participant why they should answer the
survey, what the answers were going to be used for, who had made the survey, that it was anony-
mous, no. of questions, and approx. how much time it would take to answer. Additionally, energy
renovation was explained, to ensure that the participant understood the questions. The final sur-
vey is explained in the next subsection, and the differences between the test survey and the final
survey, are in the information part, in the question about the year built, in the question about en-
Page 87
==PAGE:101==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
ergy label, as explained above, and in the last question, where an answering option has been added.
6.1.5
Final Survey
The final survey can be seen in Appendix A, Section A.3 in Danish, and the final translated
questions for the survey were:
• What is your zip code?
• Are you an owner or a renter?
• About when is the building you live in built?
• Do you know if the building you live in has a valid energy label? And if yes, do you know
which?
• What are your thoughts on renovating (energy optimising) your home?
• Can you elaborate your answer from the question above?
All questions had answering options, the reason for this was there would not be enough time to
process the answers if all 100-150 people answered differently, for all the questions. For the last
question, the participants had the option of answering ”other”, in addition to adding their own
text. They could also choose multiple answers. The idea behind the last question was to explore
why Rudersdal’s residents thought, the way that they did, about energy renovation. To minimise
the result processing time, the 12 most likely answers had been predicted, and it was these 12
answers that were the answering options for the last question.
When developing the survey questions and answering options, there was a great focus on making
them as neutral as possible and thereby not influencing their answers. Answering options were
developed to fit all the possible thoughts and options one might have.
6.1.6
Additionally Sharing of the Survey
After the survey had been shared through the municipality’s Facebook page, it was seen that the
reply target would not be met by this post alone. This assessment was taken on the basis of the
assumption that the biggest amount of replies would come in the first 24 hours. A bigger pro-
cess of sharing the survey started, and it was done by joining all the possible public and private
Facebook groups, one could think of that were created for the residents of Rudersdal. This was
done through a systematic search for groups including either the different zip codes, zip code name
areas, and city names in Rudersdal. A total of 22 memberships were requested, 15 groups approved
the membership, the survey was shared on 14 groups, due to one administrator not allowing the
post, and the post was removed after one hour by another administrator of the 14 groups it was
shared in. The 13 groups the survey were allowed to be shared on was: 1) ”Vi, der bor i Søllerød”,
2) ”Os fra Nærum”, 3) ”2850 Nærum”, 4) ”STØT LOKALT - 2950 VEDBÆK OG OMEGN”, 5)
”Trørød”, 6) ”2840 Holte”, 7) ”Os i Birkerød - hjælp hinanden”, 8) ”Alle os i Høsterkøb”, 9) ”Det
sker i Rungsted & Hørsholm”, 10) ”2830 Virum”, 11) ”Hjælp hinanden i 2800 Kongens Lyngby og
omegn”, 12) ”LYNGBY - OS DER BOR I LYNGBY OG OMEGN”, and 13) ”Hørsholm Rung-
sted Beboergruppe”. It is seen that no. 9-13 are places outside Rudersdal, but as explained in
Section 1.3, Rudersdal municipality shares zip codes with neighbouring municipalities, addition-
ally some of the groups are city centres close to Rudersdal, and the residents could therefore be
members of the Facebook groups.
The Facebook posts on these groups were done from our personal Facebook accounts, and were a
re-share of the Rudersdal municipality’s post, with a little information text before explaining what
was shared. An example of one of the posts can be seen in Appendix A Figure 60.

6.1.7
Processing the Results
As explained earlier, this was an online quantitative survey, where Google Forms was used to make
it. The survey was first shared on the 2nd of May and was closed on the 1st of July when the
result processing started. The result data was downloaded from Google Forms and processed in
Excel. The 13 replies from the test survey were added to the final results, and replies from residents
outside Rudersdal were removed.
6.2
Survey Results
After having sent out the survey on different Facebook groups and handing out the survey to the
people at the lecture in Birkerød library, 295 responses were received. 282 responses from the
Facebook groups and 13 responses from the library lecture. After analysing the results it was seen
that 37 respondents did not live in Rudersdal and were therefore taken out of the results, which
meant that there were 258 usable responses. The following section will present the results of the
survey from the 258 received responses from people who live in Rudersdal municipality.
6.2.1
Question 1
From Figure 44 the zip code of where the respondents live can be seen. It can be seen that the
people who took the survey mainly live in 3460 Birkerød where over a quarter of respondents are
from (31.0%). This is then followed by 2840 Holte (20.2%) and 2970 Hørsholm (15.5%). It can
be seen that most people who took the survey are from the bigger areas of Rudersdal. The least
amount of people who took the survey come from 2942 Skodsborg (1.2%), 2830 Virum (the part
that is in Rudersdal municipality) (2.3%), and 2800 Kgs. Lyngby (the part that is in Rudersdal
municipality) (4.7%).

zip code?

6.2.6
Question 6
To get a better understanding of why people answered what they did in question 5, the sixth
question asked them to elaborate on their answer to question 5. Figure 55 shows the pre-made
answers that were created for people to select, where people were allowed to select more than one
answer, in addition to write in their own answers in an open field (not shown in Figure 55). From
Figure 55, it can be seen that most people who answered the survey, thought that renovating could
save them money in the long run, where 160 people chose this option. A lot of people also said it
was good for the environment to renovate (139 people) and that it was good for the indoor climate
to renovate (113 people). After these three high answers, the responses even out more, where a
few answers people chose have more to do with outside factors, including 32 people said that they
renovated because they were inspired by the environment around them, whereas others were about
outside factors that limit them from renovating, such as their economic situation at the moment
(41 people), they live in an apartment where they do not get a say in when a renovation is done
(20 people), they just do not have a say in if their home gets renovated (21 people), or they do not
get permission from the bank to renovate (4 people).
People’s thoughts are not all high on renovation, where 32 people say that it is not worth it
economically to renovate, 6 people think that it is better to build anew than renovate and 2 people
do not think renovating is good for the environment at all. 13 people live in newly built buildings
which could indicate that the building anew option was a better choice for them.
Page 96
==PAGE:110==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
Figure 55: 258 respondents’ replies on the options written from survey question 6, they had the
option to select more than 1 answer: Could you elaborate on your answer from question 5?
Other than the options that were pre-made for the respondent to just click on, they also had the
option to write in answers. These written comments can be categorised into three groups: Not up
to them or something is limiting them to renovate, Renovating is a bad idea and Renovating is a
Good Idea. The most informative comments from the three groups will be discussed starting with
the Not up to them or something is limiting them to renovate group. From the comments, it can
be seen that some people are forced to renovate because they are not owners, and it was up to the
owner to renovate, or they live in cooperative housing so it is a mutual decision between everyone
who lives there, if they want to renovate or not. There are also comments that limit people from
renovating because of the age of their building, where a person has written that renovating can be
difficult because they had a different building practice when the building was built. The building
type has also limited people from renovating, in that someone lives in a townhouse and therefore a
renovation can be difficult because you would be sharing walls with another building. A person also
wrote about materials being a reason renovation can be hard because they want to be sustainable,
but they found it hard to find materials that were not greenwashed.
From the Renovating is a bad idea group, there are a few comments about the renovations costing
way too much money for them, making it not worth renovating since the payback period would be
too long. It can also be seen that a person has commented that they have already renovated, but
doing it again would cost them too many m2 and therefore not something they want to do. People
also do not want to renovate because the building, because they believe the building will lose its
architectural value, the building will just not be the same architecturally after renovating.
In the last group, Renovating is a Good Idea, people think it is environmentally better to renovate
and maintain the building, than it would be to tear down and build anew, with completely new
materials. People have also commented that a renovation is a good idea because it improves the
comfort of the building.
6.3
Discussion
This survey was a quantitative survey with few question and answering options, trying to limit
the results processing, as the reply target was 100-150 residents in Rudersdal. The reply target
was fulfilled with answers from 258 residents in Rudersdal, but was a quantitative survey the best
choice? Could the survey have shown more if fewer responses had been received, and more in-depth
Page 97
==PAGE:111==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
questions could have been asked? Would interviews have shown more than a survey, where peo-
ple’s thoughts and opinions could have been discussed, using a more qualitative approach instead
of quantitative? One could argue that a qualitative interview, would have given more knowledge
about the interviewee, both more in-depth information about what the person thought, and what
they believed the reason was behind these thoughts, in addition to asking them further questions
to check if there could be other additional reasons behind their thoughts. On the other hand, this
would have resulted in fewer replies, due to it taking more time to collect the data, making it
less representative of all the residents in Rudersdal. One could have asked one person from each
zip code, and used these qualitative interview answers to compare with the mapping of the phys-
ical renovation potential, but only asking one person to represent that entire zip code area seems
unrealistic and not very representative. Additionally, if a qualitative approach had been chosen,
it would not have been possible to collect replies in the background, while focusing on the indicators.
Due to the high reply target, and the assumption that people are in general tired of answering
surveys, it was a great focus of this thesis to keep the questions in the survey to the bare minimum.
It was hard to find the right balance between enough questions and too many questions, if more
questions were asked, it could have led to more information about the resident’s thoughts and
circumstances, but it could also have resulted in fewer replies. Even if answering the questions
only took e.g. 10 seconds, people could have chosen not to answer if there were e.g. 15 questions.
If more questions were asked, more basic information about the respondents could have been col-
lected, making it possible to check how people’s circumstances could affect their answers. It would
for example have been interesting to see if there were different answers provided based on people’s
age or gender. Additionally, as this thesis focuses on detached dwellings, it would have been nice
if the building type question from the initial survey, was kept for the final survey. However, it
is not possible to know if this one extra question, could have affected the number of replies, and
what is more important, keeping this question or keeping the high reply amount? One could argue,
that whereas 258 is well beyond the reply target, it might not have hurt to keep the building-type
question, but one could not have known this before collecting survey replies. In addition to asking
basic questions, one could have asked more questions about energy renovations. An example of
this is that it would have been interesting to ask if the person had any renovation experience, and
if so, to share this experience. However, if more questions were asked, there would still have been
the possibility of getting fewer answers, maybe less than the reply target, and with fewer answers,
the less representative the survey would have been for all of Rudersdal.
The survey was shared using Facebook, in both private and public groups, and through Ruder-
sdal municipality’s Facebook page.
The sharing method can definitely have an impact on the
respondent pool, including the age group. However, this cannot be said with certainty, as the
age of the respondents is unknown. A part of creating the survey, was also brainstorming differ-
ent ways of sharing the survey. If not enough replies could be collected through Facebook, the
plan was to hand out the survey to people in person, at busy areas of Rudersdal like e.g. Holte
Midtpunkt. QR codes could also have been hung up in different areas around Rudersdal, such
as libraries, sports facilities, city hall, etc. This was however not necessary, due to the big reply
amount, and the survey being a sub-task, it was decided to use more time on the indicators instead.
As explained in Section 6.1, some time was used on creating the best possible opening sentence
on Facebook, that would affect people as much as possible, getting Rudersdal’s residents intrigued
to answer the survey. However, one could argue that this opening sentence also affected the re-
spondent pool of the survey. The final Facebook post used pathos, where people could become
affected by the post telling them that their building can become unsellable. For this, one could
argue that because people who have answered the survey felt affected by this, it was most likely
people who have buildings that are not in the best shape, most likely on the older side. However,
comparing the building age of the building stock in Rudersdal, Figure 34, with the building year
the building was built in, Figure 46, it is seen that they are compatible, whereas both have the
highest percentage in the period 1960-1969, followed by the period 1950-1959. The time periods for
the two figures are not exactly the same, but it is still possible to compare them and see that they
are very similar to each other. Additionally, one could argue that only people who are interested
in energy renovations, would answer a survey about energy renovations. There can always be some
Page 98
==PAGE:112==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
people who are against it, and want to represent this in the survey, but they would still have an
interest in renovation. It is therefore hard to truly say, if the results from this survey can in reality
represent all the residents in Rudersdal. However, one could argue that the high reply amount, is
way above what was expected, it can be argued that there truly is an interest in Rudersdal. As
explained in Section 1.1, people have become more aware of how much heat and electricity they
use, what kind of heating supply they have, and how energy efficient their home is, after the energy
crisis, which could be a reason behind this big interest in energy renovation.
In the next chapter, the areas in Rudersdal with the highest physical renovation potential will
be found, and one would therefore ask in this chapter where the highest psychological renovation
potential in Rudersdal is? To answer this one would argue that the answering option for question
five, I think it is a good idea and have either already done it or have plans to do it in the near future,
should be the focus. However, one could argue that it would have been better if this answering
option had been split into two, whereas there is a potential for the future, and therefore for the
people that have already done it, there is not that high a renovation potential. However, from
question six, it was seen that people are affected by their surroundings, arguing that if a house
has been renovated in that area, it would affect psychological renovation potential. Going back to
question five and its answering option, it is seen in Figure 52, that there is a high psychological
renovation potential in 3460 Birkerød, 2840 Holte, and 2970 Hørsholm, followed by 2850 Nærum
and 2950 Vedbæk. These are also the ones with the highest replies, but this can also indicate
the interest in these areas. 2970 Hørsholm has the highest percentage of people who have said
that they would renovate or have renovated, compared to the other four answering options, which
could argue that the highest psychological renovation potential today, is in 2970 Hørsholm. All
the other areas have also this answering option as the highest, except 2830 Virum, here there is
a higher percentage of people that have said that renovating is a good idea, but not something
they would do themselves, which could argue that 2830 Virum has the lowest renovation potential
today. However, one should be aware that these psychological potentials could change in the future,
where people who have said that they like renovation, but it is not something they would do, might
change over to think that it is something they will do in a few more years, when their building
needs a renovation, therefore these potentials should be seen as today’s renovation potential.
6.4
Key Takeaways
The survey shows what residents of Rudersdal think about renovation. From the survey, the psy-
chological energy-efficient renovation potential in Rudersdal is high since 90% of respondents said
that renovating was a good idea. In all cities in Rudersdal it could be seen that residents thought
that renovating was a good idea, where arguably 2970 Hørsholm has the highest renovation po-
tential and 2830 Virum has the lowest today. It could be seen that owners typically think that
renovating is a good idea and something that they have done or will be doing in the near future,
and renters see renovating as a good idea but not something they are planning on doing them-
selves. It could also be seen that the newer the respondent’s building was, the less likely they
were to answer that renovating was a good idea and something they had done or planning to do,
but more likely that they thought it was a good idea, but not something they would do themselves.
The big reasons respondents think renovating is a good idea is because renovating is good for the
environment, money could be saved in the long run, and the indoor climate and comfort of the
building are improved from a renovation. There are however a few limitations in why people do not
renovate like people’s economic situation or that they live in an apartment and renovation options
are limited in that lots of people share the building. The few people who have said that they do
not think renovating is a good idea is because they think building anew is the better option, that
renovating would cause the building to lose too many m2, or that the building would lose some of
its architectural values after renovating.
The survey also proved that the newer the building is the more likely the person is to know the
energy label. It was also more likely to know the energy label of the building if the person owned
the building rather than rented it. Lastly, it was also more likely not to know the energy label if
the respondent lived in 2850 Nærum or 2950 Vedbæk.
Page 99
==PAGE:113==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
7
Mapping of the Renovation Potential
The purpose behind the indicators was to 1) develop indicators, based on the overall sustainability
factors, that can influence whether a detached dwelling should be renovated, 2) to evaluate the
energy renovation potential for each detached dwelling, 3) to be able to map the renovation poten-
tial in the municipality, and 4) to investigate which areas in Rudersdal have the highest renovation
potential. This purpose was defined on the basis of the overall goal of the thesis:
Mapping the Renovation Potential in Rudersdal Municipality Based on Sustainability Factors.
together with the second research question of the thesis:
Which areas in Rudersdal have the highest renovation potential when looking at these
sustainability factors?
All the information gathered, indicators developed, and results produced and assessed were done
to be able to map the results and find the areas with the highest renovation potential. The thought
behind the mapping was to help the municipality and its residents to prepare for the EPBD IV, in
addition to helping the municipality in further sustainable city planning and improve the building
application processes (byggesagsbehandling).
As explained in Subsection 2.2.2, the Mapping phase started when all the other phases of the
thesis were completed without any loops back to previous phases. This chapter will go through
the method of mapping the indicator results, the mapping results, a discussion of these results,
and the key takeaways from this chapter.
7.1
Method
To map the renovation potential in Rudersdal municipality, a combination of Excel and QGIS was
used. As explained in Section 5.1, each detached dwelling got its final grade, for the scenarios and
indicators, using data from BBR and Boliganalysen, and processing them in Excel. The grades
from the scenarios and indicators were all gathered in a new Excel spreadsheet, in addition to the
building coordinates from BBR. It was believed that mapping the different scenarios to see where
the highest renovation potential is, could have great value. To do so, the average score of the three
indicators for each scenario was calculated. Additionally, it was believed that creating a map of the
Energy Label indicator would be interesting. To answer the second research question, in addition
to fulfilling the thesis goal, an overall map combining all the indicators was needed, leading to the
following maps being created:
• Map of each of the scenarios.
• Map of the Energy Label indicator.
• Map of the total renovation potential in Rudersdal.
To map the total renovation potential, an average score of all the indicators was calculated. This
resulted in an Excel spreadsheet including:
• The average score of the scenario Improving the Building’s Envelope for each detached
dwelling.
• The average score of the scenario Switching the Heating Supplier for each detached dwelling.
• The average score of the scenario Switching the Heating Supplier and Improving the Building’s
Envelope for each detached dwelling.
• Each detached dwelling’s grade for the indicator Energy Label.
• The average score of the four indicators.
• The building coordinates from BBR.
Page 100
==PAGE:114==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
From this Excel spreadsheet, it was possible to make a CSV-file, which would be imported
into QGIS. In QGIS the function ”Add Delimited Text Layer” was used, where the detached
dwelling data was geographically placed using the ”KoorOest” from the BBR data as X-field, and
”KoorNord” as Y-field. This resulted in a point layer, where each detached dwelling had its own
point.
To show where the highest renovation potential is, the symbology ”Heatmap” was used in QGIS.
Heatmap works by colouring each point a colour based on a column in the attribute table, the
attribute table being the imported CVS-file. The colour ramp ”Turbo” was chosen, to highlight
the differences as much as possible, and altered a bit, by making the background transparent, to be
able to see the municipality and not just blue colour. The radius of each detached-dwelling-point
was set to 70 m, after using the trial and error method, this was believed to be the radius which
both showed the most detail and made a map of the areas and not just each detached dwelling.
When making the radius 70 m, the different points blend with each other, making the colour based
on the average between the points. In the heatmap settings, it is possible to set a maximum value,
and there was some discussion on what the maximum value of the maps should be. This was
because if the same maximum value was set for all of the maps, it would be possible to see which
map had the highest renovation potential, and it would therefore be illustrated which scenario was
the best, but it was believed that this had been covered in Chapter 5. If this had been done there
would not be a big difference in the maps showing potential after Switching Heating Supplier and
the Energy Label potential, individually. Where the Switching Heating Supplier map would only
have been blue (lowest renovation potential) and the Energy Label map would only have been red
(highest renovation potential) everywhere. It would therefore not have been possible to see where
the biggest potential for each of the approaches were, which was the idea behind the maps. It was
therefore decided to use automatic as the maximum value, which uses the maximum value for each
map, ensuring that each map showed where the highest and lowest renovation potential for that
map is. The best rendering quality was chosen, and what the points should be weighted (coloured)
by, differed between the different maps.
Lastly, it was decided to zoom in on the areas with the highest renovation potential. Five maps
were created, using all the same QGIS settings as for the maps showing the whole municipality,
except the radius of the points. When zoomed, it made sense to make the maps more detailed,
setting the radius to 45 m.
7.2
Maps of the Renovation Potential in Rudersdal
The following section will present the renovation potential in Rudersdal through maps. Firstly the
renovation potential for each of the three scenarios will be looked at, in addition to the Energy
Label indicator. Then the total renovation potential will be looked at, and lastly, the renovation
potential will be looked at in some zoomed-in areas of Rudersdal.
7.2.1
The Three Scenarios and the Energy Label Indicator
A map of the renovation potential for each of the three scenarios can be seen in Figure 56, in
addition to the Energy Label indicator. Larger images of the maps in Figure 56, can be seen
in Appendix B (Figure 61 - 64). The renovation potential is shown through colours on the map
where high renovation potential is red and low renovation potential is blue, the scale can be seen
on the legend on each map. The renovation potential for the scenarios was found by calculating
the average score from the indicators involved in that scenario, whereas the map of the Energy
Label indicator is based on the grade in the indicator.
From Figure 56 it can be seen that the indicator Energy Label has the map with the most red in it,
which indicates that there are a lot of detached dwellings that have high renovation potential for
this indicator. For the three scenarios it can be seen that there are a few areas that have orange
even dark red spots, on the map which shows that these areas have high renovation potential
within these scenarios.

The Total Renovation Potential
The total renovation potential in Rudersdal can be seen from Figure 57. A larger image of the
renovation potential in Rudersdal can be seen in Appendix B Figure 70. The renovation potential
was found by taking the average score of all the indicators combined. From the map, it can be seen
that there is high renovation potential in highly populated areas in Rudersdal, which is due to the
fact that a lot of detached dwellings would be in those areas. It can be seen that there is not a high
renovation potential in less dense areas of Rudersdal, since there are a lot of blue-green dots on
the map. The denser areas of Rudersdal that have higher renovation potential include Birkerød,
Holte, Nærum, and Vedbæk. It can be seen that these areas all have multiple areas that have high
renovation potential, in that there are multiple spots of red in several areas of these cities. From
the map, the darkest coloured red that can be seen is in Vedbæk. The other more noticeable areas
are Birkerød Lake, Bistrup, the center of Holte, and Kollemosevej, because they, in general, have
more red in them, therefore the renovation potential would be higher in these areas.

really strong dark red spots which were obvious to see in Figure 57, but otherwise, the rest of
Vedbæk is not that red, this would mean that there in this area are a few buildings that have very
high renovation potential compared to the rest.

7.3
Discussion
The maps created above can be used to see the renovation potential in Rudersdal. Whereas just
presenting the results as a lot of numbers would lead to a longer process, the maps would allow
employees at Rudersdal to get a quick overview of where the biggest renovation potential is. A
drawback of the map, however, is that it gives less detail whereas looking at the numbers could
give specific details about the specific detached dwelling.
The map would allow employees at Rudersdal, such as people who work in the Environmental and
Technical department who work on the heating plan, to see where in Rudersdal their focus should
Page 104
==PAGE:118==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
be to improve upon environmental impacts when it comes to detached dwellings in the municipal-
ity. In this way, they have a quick overview of where improvements need to be made to detached
dwellings in Rudersdal in order to achieve their goal of being carbon neutral by 2040. The map can
also focus on a building or area, but to get more detailed, they will need to look at the total scores
found for the different scenarios, to see what is the best renovation option for a specific detached
dwelling. The Environmental and Technical department has already shown interest in the results
of this thesis, they would like to know in which areas energy improvements can be made, and CO2
emissions can be saved, as part of the municipalities project to support residents of Rudersdal, in
making their housing more energy efficient.
The map could also be used to see if Rudersdal’s heating plan, described in Subsection 3.8.2, has
the right detached dwellings in focus and if the detached dwellings are placed in the right phases
of the plan. From Rudersdal’s heating plan, it can be seen that Bistrup and Vedbæk are placed in
phase two, and Kollemosevej is in phase three, which is surprising considering they are areas with
high renovation potential, as seen in Figure 57, and in the scenario Switching the heating supplier
in Figure 56. The centre of Holte and Birkerød are both in phase one, which makes sense since they
are two of the biggest areas in Rudersdal, and it would therefore be obvious that they are offered
a change to district heating, before Bistrup, Vedbæk or Kollemosevej. The map could, however,
be used to question why Bistrup, Vedbæk, or Kollemosevej are not in phase one over Skodsborg,
where from Figure 57 it can be seen that there is little renovation potential. When looking at
the survey, it can also be seen that of the few who answered from Skodsborg, 1.2%, there was
an equal amount of people who said they like renovating and have renovated or were planning on
doing it, as there were people who said they do not like to renovate, showing low psychological
renovation potential compared to Vedbæk, Holte, and Birkerød. From the survey it can be seen
that Vedbæk, Holte, and Birkerød all had a high percentage of respondents saying that renovating
was a good idea and something they have done or are planning on doing, showing a high psycholog-
ical renovation potential. This all leads to questioning why Skodsborg is prioritised over Vedbæk,
Holte (Kollemosevej), and Birkerød (Bistrup). There were however only a few respondents from
Skodsborg, which means that Rudersdal should look further into the willingness to renovate in
this area. It can also be seen that a lot of people in Birkerød and Vedbæk, have said that reno-
vating is a good idea but not something they have thought about doing themselves, resulting in
that one could argue that Holte should be prioritised, because over 60% of the respondents living
there, said renovating was a good idea and was something they are planning on doing or have done.
The map shows that there is a low potential to renovate in Hørsholm. An explanation of this might
come from the survey, where Hørsholm has the highest psychological renovation potential, where
70% of the respondents living there, said renovating was a good idea and was something they are
planning on doing or have done. This could mean that people have already renovated in this area,
contributing to a lower renovation potential. However, the indicators use BUILD data, which is the
average potential for all of Denmark, dependent on building year and heating supply, which means
that even though they might have improved their building’s envelope, it should not appear on the
map. However, if they already have a sustainable heating supplier, could be the explanation for the
low physical renovation potential. In Birkerød, Vedbæk, Nærum, and Holte, it can be seen from
the survey, that more people than in Hørsholm have said that they like to renovate, but it is not
something they would do, which could be why these areas of Rudersdal have a higher potential to
renovate. This could however also be because more people in those four cities live in apartments or
live in cooperative housing, where the decision is not theirs to renovate. Furthermore, the centres
of Birkerød, Vedbæk, Nærum, and Holte are in Rudersdal municipality, whereas Hørsholm’s city
centre is in Hørsholm municipality, meaning that more people from Birkerød, Vedbæk, Nærum,
and Holte would live in Rudersdal’s more denser areas.
For the building application managers (byggesagsbehandler) in Rudersdal, the map would be less
relevant because the results are shown as an overview and not on a building-by-building basis,
which is what building application managers work with. Therefore an application manager could
use the specific results found for detached dwellings, in this way, they would have the specific CO2,
kWh, and money saving results on the specific detached dwelling for each scenario. In this way,
they can help the resident figure out what is the best option for their detached dwelling.

7.4
Key Takeaways
Looking at the three different scenarios and the indicator Energy Label, it could be seen that the
Energy Label indicator had the most detached dwellings, with a high renovation potential, in that
the map of this indicator had the most red.
From the final map of the total renovation potential in Rudersdal, it could be seen that there
were five areas that seemed to have higher renovation potential than others, these being Vedbæk,
Birkerød Lake, Bistrup, Center of Holte, and Kollemosevej. From the closer inspection of the
areas, it could be seen that all areas had red in them, meaning that there was a high renovation
potential, but it was Bistrup that seemed to be the area with the highest renovation potential.
