8
Discussion
This chapter will look at the thesis as a whole, and discuss the overall thesis purpose and goal,
and the indicators and mapping, in addition to how the new EPBD will be implemented, finishing
off by listing some of the future studies that could have been done, if there had been more time.
8.1
The Thesis Purpose and Goal
The new possible EPBD initiative can end up trapping people; not being able to renovate and not
being able to sell. The purpose of the thesis was therefore defined as finding a way to help the mu-
nicipality and its residents prepare for the new EPBD initiative, and to do this, the thesis goal was
set to: mapping the renovation potential in Rudersdal municipality based on sustainability factors,
but the question is if it even makes sense to prepare for something that is still not been decided.
The legislation needs to be approved by the Council of the European Union, which means that the
Council of the European Union needs to agree with the European Parliament, before becoming an
official EU law. After this, each country makes their own plan for implementation. This means
that there may still be changes to the initiative, and specifics are still missing. This has definitely
had an effect on this thesis, on how to best help the municipality and its residents prepare for
it. Had the initiative been more specified, the indicators could have been more applicable to the
initiative itself. Instead, different sustainability factors for energy renovation were found through a
literature review. This did however make the indicators wider and more comprehensive in regards
to sustainability. Even though it was not possible to make the indicators specific to the initiative,
the indicator set included an energy label indicator, which is a central part of the initiative, in
addition to the percentile grading method, ensuring 20% of the highest renovation potential was
graded five, which is compatible with the new EPBD labelling system.
After completing this thesis, one could argue that the world and Denmark were not ready for it,
especially not in this big municipality scale, due to the missing data listed in Subsection 5.1.5,
including insulation thickness, renovation process, building parts, materials, level of obsolescence,
dangerous materials, and the social aspects on energy renovation. Without specific data on the
buildings, they were only graded on what is most likely, and not the true renovation potential.
Additionally, the renovation process is missing, which has a big impact on the material use, and
thereby CO2 emissions and environmental sustainability, and the costs of the renovation, and
thereby economic sustainability. Lastly, the direct social impact was missing. A solution to all
these challenges, could have been to only focus on one detached dwelling in Rudersdal throughout
the project. The focus could have been to see how this one detached dwelling, could have prepared
for the new EPBD initiative. By doing this, it would be possible to find the best energy renovation
for that specific house, make a LCA of that renovation, try to give the building an energy label,
investigate the costs of the renovation in addition to the savings, calculating the payback period,
and lastly investigating the social impact for the residents of the house. This could then have
been the implementation strategy for all detached dwellings in Rudersdal. However, the question
would then become if this, in fact, could be implemented for all buildings in Denmark, since there
is a huge difference between buildings in both Rudersdal and Denmark. Therefore, it would have
limited the use of the project’s results, if the project only focused on one building.
If Denmark should get ready for a similar project in the future, a solution for more specific data
concerning energy renovation, could be to make it a law that all detached dwellings have to have
a valid energy label at all times. Just like cars have to be checked periodically, detached dwellings
should be checked every 10 years to get an updated energy label, where the owners could be
reminded by the municipality. Additionally, the energy labelling data should be accessible for
everyone, and connected to the BBR data.
Bolius was used to find the prices of what each heating supply costs in DKK/kWh, the prices are
the average for the specific heating supplier. From Bolius there were different types of heating
suppliers when it comes to heating pumps and biomass, so the most expensive heat pump and
biomass heat supply were chosen, which is not very specific for each detached dwelling. This is
one of the areas BBR needs to become more specific and show what type of heating supply they
Page 107
==PAGE:121==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
have, and where they get their supply from, since e.g. district heating can vary in cost, depending
on where you get the district heating from.
8.2
Indicators and Mapping
Three of the indicators were developed based on data of the averages in Denmark, one could ask
what the result of this thesis can be used for, because one can not be sure that the potential shown
in the results, matched the reality. However, Rudersdal municipality used the same method when
they worked on their heating plan as explained in Subsection 2.2.1. One could therefore assume
that this data is truly the best of the worst, of what is available today, though one would encour-
age Denmark or Rudersdal to get better building data. Even though the data is not that specific,
one could argue that by being based on average data for Denmark, the developed indicators can
be reused for all the other municipalities in Denmark, making it possible to make a map of the
renovation potential in all of Denmark.
The same three indicators had scenarios, and all of them are included in the final renovation poten-
tial grade. The reason for this is due to energy renovations cover all three scenarios, meaning that
the residents can choose to only carry out one of the scenarios, which can influence the renovation
potential, and therefore to ensure that all three scenarios are accounted for, all were included in
the total final renovation potential grade. The question is if this truly was the best choice, would
it have been better to choose one of them? Maybe the one with the highest renovation poten-
tial, the scenario including both switching heating supplier and improving the building envelope,
should have been chosen? However, one usually chooses the worst-case scenario, but what is it
in this case? This is maybe the scenario with the highest potential, however, if an owner can
only afford one of them, should not this also be accounted for, but how? There are differences
between the two scenarios, switching heating supplier and improving the building envelope, when
it comes to CO2 and prices, so there is no clear best or worst case scenario out of those two
scenarios. Due to there not being any clear reason for choosing any of the three scenarios, one
would argue that it was the best choice to include all of them in the final renovation potential grade.
When comparing the BUILD report and the indicators developed, one could ask why it is called
heating consumption in the BUILD report, but energy consumption in the indicator. This was not
intentionally, and if there had been more time, this would have been processed further.
When mapping the renovation potential, heatmap in QGIS was used, however, would it have been
better to map it based on zip codes, and therefore have been more comparable with the survey?
This would however not have shown the same level of detail, the heatmap can provide. And if there
was more time, could it have been possible to also map the psychological renovation potential, and
how would that have worked? If that should have been possible, each survey reply should have
gotten a grade from one to five, as the physical renovation potential, to be comparable, based on
the answers given. One could argue that the questions and the answering options in the survey, do
not work well with this five grade scale, and the best option would therefore have been to develop
the survey with this mapping goal in mind. This could for example have been done, by giving the
respondents different statements on energy renovation, and asking them to grade that statement
from one to five, on how much they agreed with the statement.
8.3
Implementing the EPBD IV
At the moment it is hard to see how the new EPBD can be implemented in Denmark, because a
lot of information about how the new initiative will be implemented, is missing. How will the new
initiative find the 15% worst residences in Denmark, to be able to set the scale? Just in Rudersdal,
it is seen that 61% of detached dwellings do not have an energy label today, and there is no specific
data on these buildings. Will this lead the government to require all residences to get an energy
label at the same time, to get an overview of how energy-efficient the residences are in Denmark?
Or will they assume how energy-efficient the residences are? And how will an energy label be
given? If the new initiative introduces a new labelling scale, there could be requirements of what
should be included when labelling. Will it even be Denmark that is allowed to decide how they
Page 108
==PAGE:122==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
find the 15% worst buildings, or is the description a part of the initiative?
One way of implementing the EPBD IV, could be to make it a law that a residence has to have
a valid energy label at all times, as suggested in Section 8.1. There could be an implementing
phase of e.g. two years, where within this time span all residences have to be evaluated. First, the
residences have to be evaluated, and thereafter it would be possible to set the new labelling scale.
From this, the system would work automatically, requiring the building owner to update their
energy label every 10 years. Each municipality could have the overview, reminding the owners
when it is time to get it updated.
Another way to implement the EPBD IV, would be the same way it was done in this report. If
buildings were up to date in BBR, regarding the heat consumption, year built and area, the indi-
cators in this thesis could be used, or if more specifics about what is included in the new initiative
are given, new indicators based on this, can be developed. Then each building could be placed
into a grade and the 15% worst buildings in Denmark could be improved upon.
8.4
Future Studies
If there had been more time in this thesis there are multiple studies that could have been investi-
gated further, some of them include:
• As explained in Subsection 5.6.1, one of the first things that would have been investigated
further, was the district heating suppliers in Rudersdal, and figuring out what heating supply
Holte Fjernvarme and Norfors use to deliver district heating.
• The social sustainability pillar, would also have been investigated further, and exploring if it
could have played a more active role in the set of indicators.
• The link provided by Energimærkningsordningen, as explained in Subsection 5.6.1, would
have been investigated further by contacting teachers or students at DTU that have API
experience. If there had been even more time the platform Energimærkningsordningen is
making would have been used.
• An investigation of Boliganalysen, which is a website that is designed to help municipalities
achieve their energy-saving goals and help with the conversion to climate-friendly heating in
private homes. It is a tool that can be used by the municipality to show residents how much
potential there is in an energy-efficient renovation (Boliganalysen, n.d.). Boliganalysen was
found late in the project, so an in-depth study of what data Boliganalysen had, could not be
made, but based on the explanation of the webpage, there could be additional information
about energy renovations that could have been used in the thesis.

9
Conclusion
This thesis investigates the energy-efficient renovation potential of 10,228 detached dwellings in
Rudersdal municipality, and simultaneously investigates how the new EPBD can be implemented.
The majority of the building types in Rudersdal are detached dwellings and were therefore fo-
cused on. The goal of an energy-efficient renovation is to reduce the energy consumption for the
building’s operation so that the building satisfies the users’ needs. In this way, this renovation
covers all three pillars of sustainability, since reducing energy consumption will reduce the price
and environmental impacts the building has, and socially the building has to satisfy the user.
Through the literature review, eight overall sustainability factors that can influence whether a
detached dwelling should be renovated were found.
These eight factors being 1) Heating con-
sumption, 2) Heating supply, 3) Building parts, 4) Materials, 5) BR18 rules of profitability and
minimum energy requirements, 6) Energy labels, 7) Indoor climate, and 8) Level of obsolescence
and dangerous materials and gasses.
To investigate the energy-efficient renovation potential of the detached dwellings in Rudersdal,
four indicators were created that look at the energy consumption, CO2 emissions, price of heating,
and energy labels that the detached dwelling has. These indicators were developed on the basis
of the sustainability factors that were found in the literature review and listed above. Three dif-
ferent scenarios were created to investigate which energy-efficient renovation was the best option
for residents in Rudersdal, these being Improving the Building’s Envelope, Switching the Heating
Supplier, and both;Switching the Heating Supplier and Improving the Building’s Envelope. The
three scenarios were sub-indicators for three of the indicators, excluding energy labels which were
evaluated based on status quo. The detached dwellings were then given a grade from zero to five
depending on how well the building did for each indicator and scenario, with zero being no poten-
tial, one being the lowest potential and five being the highest potential. The detached dwellings
were distributed between the grades using percentile.
From the results, it could be concluded that the scenario with the highest renovation potential was
the scenario Switching the Heating Supplier and Improving the Building’s Envelope, in that most
detached dwellings in this scenario got a grade of five. Furthermore, the results showed, for the in-
dicator Energy Consumption, that there is no potential in switching the heating supplier when
it comes to energy consumption, due to the heating supplier having no effect on the consumption,
which resulted in scenarios Improving the Building’s Envelope and Switching the Heating Supplier
and Improving the Building’s Envelope having the same results. For the indicator CO2 Emissions,
60.25% of the detached dwellings that had received a grade of five were in the scenario Switching
the Heating Supplier and Improving the Building’s Envelope. From this indicator it could be seen
that switching the heating supplier had a big effect on the detached dwellings, in that 99.75% of
the detached dwellings that received a grade of five were in the two scenarios where the heating
supplier was changed. When it came to CO2 emissions it could be seen that switching from a
biomass heating supplier to district heating or heat pump resulted in no renovation potential, but
switching from gas, oil or electric heating supply would reduce CO2 emissions substantially. For
the indicator Price of Heating, it could be seen that 44.25% of detached dwellings that received
a grade of five were in the scenario Switching the Heating Supplier and Improving the Building’s
Envelope. For this indicator, it could be seen that it was the least beneficial to switch from gas
heating supply to district heating or heat pump, whereas, for biomass, electric heating, and oil
heating supply, it was very beneficial to switch to district heating or heat pump.
For the Energy Label indicator it could be seen that 39% of the detached dwellings in Rudersdal
have a valid energy label, leaving 61% without. The indicator results show that most buildings in
Rudersdal would end up with a grade five. This is due to the fact that so many detached dwellings
in Rudersdal do not have a valid energy label, and that 1% of the detached dwellings in Rudersdal
have an energy label G, resulting in 62% of the detached dwellings receiving the grade of five.
The indicators looked at both the environmental and economic side of sustainability in renovation
therefore a survey was created so that the social side of sustainability could be investigated. The
Page 110
==PAGE:124==
M.Sc. Thesis
DTU
s164637 & s174617
indicators showed that there is a physical renovation potential in Rudersdal, but this does not
help if the owners are not interested in renovating. The survey, therefore, focused on whether
or not people were interested in renovating their homes, and the reasons they were or were not
interested. A total of 258 responses were received from residents living in Rudersdal municipality.
From the survey results, it could be seen that the psychological energy-efficient renovation poten-
tial in Rudersdal is high, since 90% of the respondents said that renovating was a good idea. In all
cities in Rudersdal, it could be seen that there is a larger amount of people who think renovating
is a good idea than there are those who think it is bad, where arguably 2970 Hørsholm has the
highest renovation potential and 2830 Virum has the lowest today. It could be seen that owners
typically think that renovating is a good idea and something that they have done or will be doing
in the near future, and renters see renovating as a good idea but not something they are planning
on doing themselves. It could also be seen that the newer the respondent’s building was, the less
likely they were to answer that renovating was a good idea and something they had done or were
planning on doing, but were more likely to answer that they thought it was a good idea, but not
something they would do themselves. From the survey it could also be concluded that most people
think that renovating is a good idea because it can save them money in the long run, it is good for
the environment and the community, and it improves the indoor climate. Where people who do
not think renovating is a good idea, do not think it is worth it economically or they think building
anew is the better option.
After having created the indicators the physical renovation potential could be mapped. This was
done by taking the average score of the three indicators in the three scenarios, then finding the
overall average of the scenarios and the energy label indicator, so that one overall score was calcu-
lated, which resulted in all four indicators weighting equal on the total renovation potential grade.
This overall score was then mapped in QGIS using the symbology heatmap. From the result of
the map, it could be concluded that the renovation potential was highest in the following five
areas of Rudersdal: Vedbæk, Birkerød Lake (in Birkerød), Bistrup (in Birkerød), Centre of Holte,
and Kollemosevej (in Holte). From these five areas, it was seen that there was a higher potential
to renovate in Bistrup because the map had the most red spots on it, where red indicates high
renovation potential. The map combined with the survey shows that the renovation potential in
Birkerød and Holte are high, since four of the five areas with the highest renovation potential are
in Holte and Birkerød, and from the survey it could be seen that a high percentage of people do
like the idea of renovating in these two areas.
It can be concluded that the thesis goal, Mapping the Renovation Potential in Rudersdal Munici-
pality Based on Sustainability Factors was fulfilled. The methods used in this thesis can be applied
if the EPBD initiative is implemented, since the method ranks detached dwellings according to
renovation potential. However it could also be concluded that more specific data is necessary to
find the specific detached dwellings renovation potential, therefore a bigger effort should be made
by the municipalities to collect more data on detached dwellings in Denmark, if this new EPBD
initiative is to be implemented.
